1034	<h2 class="blog_entry-title"> BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [C]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  16BLD(HCD)337, 14BLD(AD) 161, 20 BLD(HCD)177, 15BLD(HCD)449, 16BLD (HCD)294,16BLD(HCD)312, 15BLD(HCD) 298,  13 BLD (HCD)392, 15BLD(HCD)339,15BLD(AD)11, 16BLD(AD)27, 16BLD (HCD)580, 17BLD(AD) 193, 17BLD (HCD) 223, 17BLD(HCD)11,  17BLD (HCD) 366,15BLD (HCD</p><p><b>Subject:</b>   BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [C]</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CASE DIARY</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Case diary</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 172 (2) Cr.P.C. a criminal Court may send for the police diary of a case under enquiry or trial before it and may use it in the case not as evidence but in aid of such enquiry or trial. If the case diary is used by the police officer, who made it or the Court uses it for the purpose of contradicting such police officer, the provisions of the Evidence Act or of section 161 Cr.P.C. shall apply.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Sukur Miah Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)337</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CHARGE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Charge-alteration or amendment</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">The Court may at any time alter or amend any charge during the trial under section 227 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Hussain Mohammaa Ershad Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD) 161</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Charge-object of</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When any section of any law is quoted at the time of framing of charge, the section must be quoted in the language in which the law is framed.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Habibur Rahman alitis Raju Vs. The State, 20 BLD(HCD)177</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A charge is a formal accusation framed against the accused persons facing a trial.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The object of framing charge is to enable the accused to precisely know the substantive allegations they will have to meet at the trial. When evidence is admitted on a distinctly different charge beyond the accusation, the accused are prejudiced in their defence. Cr.P.C. S. 221</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kamala and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)449</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Charge</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Proceedings of pending cases of Martial Law Courts after withdrawal of Martial Law</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Martial Law Courts are extra-constitutional Tribunals and these do not leave behind any legacy of continuation or succession after the lifting of Martial Law.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Divisional Special Judge was wrong in disposing of the case, which was sent to him for disposal after withdrawal of Martial Law, on the basis of the evidence recorded by the Summary Martial Law Court.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Saidur Rahman Vs. The State, 16BLD (HCD)294</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Charge</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Particulars of Charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A charge is to contain particulars as to time, place and manner of the-occurrence so that the accused can effectively meet the allegations brought against him. Unless the charge contains particulars regarding the time, place and manner of the occurrence the accused is likely to be seriously prejudiced in his defence. Section—222. Cr.P.C</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdur Razzak alias Geda Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)312</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Charge</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Conviction without framing charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Conviction of the appellants under sections 302/34 of the Penal Code without framing any charge thereunder is clearly illegal—Cr.P.C. Ss. 221, 232(2)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Muslim and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 298</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Framing Charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 242 of the Code of Criminal Procedure has given wide power to the trial Court to frame a charge under a particular section of the Penal Code which can not be interfered with by the revisional Court by way of giving direction for altering the charge or framing the charge.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shariful Islam Vs. Md. Billal Hossain and the State, 13 BLD (HCD)392</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Framing of charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 241A of the Code the Magistrate will consider only the documents of the prosecution submitted with the case record. Documents in support of the defence may form part of the record after charge is framed and trial begins.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The learned Additional Sessions Judge while exercising revisional jurisdiction was wrong to find the charge groundless on consideration of the documents filed for the first time before him in support of the defence case. The order of discharge is illegal and the same is quashed Cr.P.C. Ss. 241A and 561A</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mahbuba Akter and others Vs. Md. Mozemmel Hoque and others, 15BLD(HCD)339</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Framing of charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The usual and well-settled principle of law is that a criminal proceeding can only be quashed after cognizance has been taken and process issued thereupon. But there may be a rare case where the High Court Division may be justified in interfering even at the initial stage where the continuation of the proceeding is barred under any law or the F.I.R. or the petition of complaint does not disclose any offence—Cr.P.C. Ss. 241A, 265C and 561A</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Syed Mohammad Hashem alias Hashem Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD)11</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Framing of charge<br/></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">While framing charge the trial Court is only to see if on the basis of the materials on record a prima facie case to go for the trial has been made out against the accused persons. Section—242 Cr.P.C</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mr. Moudud Ahmed Vs. The State, 16BLD(AD)27</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Framing of charge--particular of a charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A charge must contain the particulars as to the time and place of the alleged offence and these should be reasonably sufficient to give the accused notice of the matters he will have to face at the trial. In the instant case, no date or dates of the commission of the alleged offence having been mentioned in the charge, it is to be held that the provision of section 222 Cr.P.C. has not been complied with.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nizamuddin Dhali Vs The State, 16BLD (HCD)580</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Framing of charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">While taking cognizance of an offence under this Act the Bishesh Adalat will consider whether a prima facie case has been disclosed against the accused on perusal of the FIR, the charge-sheet and other materials on record. After hearing the prosecution and the accused the Bishesh Adalat will decide whether to frame charges or to discharge the accused—Nari-O-Shishu Nirjatan (Special Provisions) Act, 1995(XVIII of 1995) Section—17</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mohiuddin and others Vs. Md. Motiur Rahman and another, 17BLD(AD) 193</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Framing of charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Law requires that the first and last dates of the charge of criminal breach of trust or dishonest misappropriation of money shall not exceed one year. In the present case the charge of misappropriation of money extended over a period of 2 years which vitiated the trial. The case was sent back on remand to the trial Court for holding trial according to law—Cr.P.C., Section.222(2), Proviso.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abdul Ban Molla Vs. The State, 17BLD (HCD) 223</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 7 BLD 413; 39 DLR 184; 45 DLR 352—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Framing of charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The object of section 265C of the Code of Criminal Procedure is to enquire into the materials on record for prima facie satisfaction of the Court as to whether the accused should be discharged or proceeded against so that innocent persons maynot be harassed on false and frivolous allegations.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">If on the other hand, if the Court finds that there is ground for presuming that the accused has committed an offence it shall frame necessary charges against him under section 265D of the Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Khondker Maniruzzaman Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD)11</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Framing of charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sections 265C and 265D Cr.P.C. are abridged substitution of the now repealed Chapter XVIII of the Code and these cast a duty upon the Sessions Judge to apply his judicial mind in considering the materials collected by the prosecution and placed on record in onder to come to a decision whether charge should be framed against a particular accused or not.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jobaida Rashid Vs. The State, 17BLD (HCD) 366</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CHARGE SHEET</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Charge-sheet</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Submission of charge sheet after expiry of specified and extended time</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Further investigation having not been stopped by the Magistrate after the expiry of specified and extended time, the submission of a subsequent charge sheet can be treated as one as if on the basis of a fresh FIR.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Investigation of a case being a matter of procedure and amendment of the law relating to investigation having retrospective effect, no vested right accrued to the accused petitioner from failure to complete investigation within the specified time.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kitab Ali Sikdar Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD) 482</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 45 DLR 610—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CHEATING</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cheating</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Whenever a loan is taken by one from another on the representation to repay the same dishonestly inducing the person to lend money having no intention to repay, it will be an offence under section 420 B.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Shafluddin Khan Vs. The State and another, 13 BLD (JHJCD) 362</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 13 CWN, 728; 10 DLR 325, 26 DLR 146; 27DLR(AD) 175; 7BLD 164—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cheating</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The question of offence of cheating, does not when there is nothing on record to show that any entrustment of property was made to the accused—Penal Code Sections.406 and 420</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Islam Ali Mia alias Md. Islam Vs. Amal Chandra Mondal and another, 13BLD (AD)28</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cheating</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To justify a conviction for cheating, the initial intention to deceive on the part of the accused must be established. The intention is to be gathered from the surrounding circumstances—Penal Code, Section 415</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Arifur Rahman alias Bablu Vs. Shantosh Kumar Sadhu and another, 14 BLD(AD) 78</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 12 DLR 520; 30 DLR 327</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cheating</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To sustain a charge of cheating the prosecution must prove the initial deception of the accused—Penal Code S. 420</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Karim Vs. Shamsul Alam and another, 14BLD(HCD)167</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> PLD 1950(Lahore)90; 14BLD(1994) 33; l0DLR443—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cheating</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Offence under section 138 of the Negotiable Instruments (Amendment) Act for dishonour of a cheque and the offence of cheating under section 420 of the Penal Code are two distinct offences under two different laws. So, the trial of the petitioner under section 420 of the Penal Code before the Magistrate is not barred under the law. The institution of a money suit for realisation of money in question from the petitioner does not also stand as a bar against the prosecution from proceeding with the trial of the case under section 420 of the Penal Code. Negotiable Instruments (Amendment) Act, 1994 (XIX of 1994), Sections—138 and 141, Penal Code, 1860 (XLV of 1860), Section—420</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Nurul Islam Vs. The State and another, 17BLD (HCD) 498</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cheating</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From the petition of complaint it appears that the transaction in question was normal and routine transaction and as such the liability of the accused persons, if any, is civil liability. As there are no ingredients of the offences under section 406 or 420 of the Penal Code in the petition of complaint, continuation of the proceeding will be an abuse of the process of the Court and as such the proceedings is liable to be quashed.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Asaduzzaman Vs. Md Salamatullah, 19BLD (HCD)461</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cheating</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To constitute an offence of cheating as described in section 415 of the Penal Code, there must be a specific allegation that the accused had initial intention of deceiving the complainant. If there is allegation that goods were delivered on credit on specific promise of repayment within a specific date but the payment was not made within the specific time, it may be inferred that there was initial intention of deception.</p><p style="line-height: 115%;"><strong><em>Md Asaduzzaman Vs. Md Salamatullah, 19BLD (HCD) 461</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 46 DLR (AD) 180; 30 DLR 327; 27 DLR (AD)175; 1954Crl. L. J. (SC)1806; 10 BLD(AD) 168: 42DLR(AD)240—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CHILD</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Child</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Only when it appears to the Court that a person charged with an offence is a child, the Court is required to direct an enquiry to ascertain his age. When an accused is above 16 years of age when charge is framed against him, he is not entitled to the benefit of the Children Act, 1974. Section—66(1)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bimal Das Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD) 218</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Child witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Before examining a child of tender age as a witness, the Court should satisfy itself that the child is intellectually developed enough to comprehend what he has seen and to give an intelligent account of it to the Court. If the child is found sufficiently intelligent to understand the questions put to him and he is capable of giving rational answers to those questions, then he is as good as any other adult witness.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Faziul Haq Sikder Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD)364</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Child witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A child witness who is himself a victim of assaults and saw the accused persons killing his father and testifies in the Court to the said effect and remains unshaken in cross- examination, is a competent and reliable witness.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Forkan alias Farhad and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD)163</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Child witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A child witness is a competent witness to give evidence in Court provided it appears from its deposition that it could understand the question put and give rational answers thereto. Evidence Act, 1872 (I of 1872), Section—118</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State vs. Ali Hossain, 18BLD(HCD) 655</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> AIR 1937 (Patna) 662; 1BLC (1996) 173; 43DLR(AD)(1991)234;A1R1953 (Patna) 246;—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CIRCUMSTANCIAL EVIDENCE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence—</strong>accused presumed to be innocent of the charge till guilt is established by legal evidence—Penal Code, 1860, S.302</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Malai Miah Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD)277</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When admittedly a wife sleeps at night with the husband in a room or hut not approachable by others, the husband is rightly convicted under Section 302 of the Penal Code on the basis of inderbitable evidence on record. S.302 of the Penal Code</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When admittedly a wife sleeps at night with the husband in a room or hut which is not approachable by others and there is no probable circumstances explaining the cause of death of the wife and she is found to have been killed by in a brutal manner by strangulation, the husband is rightly convicted u/s. 302 of the Penal Code on the basis of inderbitable evidence on record against him.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Hamid @ Sofaruddin Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 563</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 38 DLR 235; 40 DLR (AD)139— Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The general principle relating to circumstantial evidence is that the evidence proved in a case must be such as would exclude any other hypothesis than that of the guilt of the accused.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since in the instant case the evidence on record was sufficient and the chain of circumstances was such as would lead to no other conclusion other than that of the guilt of the accused the High Court Division was justified in dismissing the appeal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Rezaul Haque and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(AD)261</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR(AD)139; 16 DLR(SC)261— Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the circumstantial evidence as to demand and receipt of illegal gratification is convincing and conclusive, omission of minor details in the evidence of witnesses does not affect the prosecution case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Rafiqul Islam Vs. The State, 14BLD (AD)248</em></strong></p><p><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To base a conviction upon circumstantial evidence, it must be incompatible with the innocence of the accused and it must exclude all reasonable hypothesis of his innocence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Haji Md. Jamaluddin Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)33</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is complete chain of circumstances that the appellants assaulted deceased victim Biswajit severely and dealt fatal blow causing his death when appellant Gulzar participated in the occurrence most actively and he was found by PW4 for the last time with the deceased victim when Gulzar was chasing him by the eastern side of the khal and the circumstances of the case taken cumulatively are forming a claim so complete that there is no escaped from the conclusion that the murder of victim Biswajit was committed by appellant Guizar and his associates and none else.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Guizar Biswas &amp; ors. Vs. The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 550</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a wife killing case inside the house of the husband it is difficult to find a witness seeing the act of killing. Even the neighbours generally hesitate to come forward to depose in the Court. Under such circumstances, the prosecution as of necessity is to rely on circumstantial evidence. When it is proved that the victim went to bed with her husband in the fateful night and she was subsequently found dead a serious obligation is cast upon the accused to account for the circumstances leading to the death of the wife.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Sukur Miah Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)337</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 40 DLR(HCD) 122; 27 DLR (HCD) 671; 43 DLR(AD) 92—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Circumstantial evidence to form the basis of conviction must be of a conclusive nature from which no other reasonable hypothesis other than the guilt of the accused can be inferred. Suspicion. however strong, is no substitute for legal evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jahur Ali Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)621</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">The plea of alibi being disbelieved, a heavy burden is cast on the husband to explain as to how his wife died with multiple injuries on her person in his room where they were sleeping in the same bed.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gouranga Kumar Saha Vs. The State, 17BLD (AD) 120</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Courts below on proper assessment of the evidence and circumstances of the case clearly found that the petitioner had feigned to have been on casual leave on the false ground of illness and he, in collusion with the co-accused, mis-appropriated the money in question and that there was no dacoity in the Bank as claimed by the defence. Moreover, the defence could not point out any misreading of evidence. There being no missing link in the chain of circumstances against the petitioner and his co-accused, the order of conviction has been rightly maintained.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Badrul Haque Choudhury Vs. The State, 18BLD (AD) 85</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The general principle is that the circumstantial evidence proved in a case must be such as would exclude any other hypothesis than that of the guilt of the accused. Since in the instant case the evidence on record was sufficient and the chain of circumstances was such as would lead to no other conclusion other than that of the guilt of the accused, the High Court Division was justified in dismissing the appeal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Rezaul Haque and others Vs. The State 16BLD(AD)261</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR(AD)139; 16 DLR(SC)261— Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Calling and taking away of the victim by appellant Billal and co-convict Saiful from his residence half an hour before his murder, recovery of the body of the victim from near the Mirpur Bus Stand No. 1, Billal’s offer of love and threat to the P.W.2 Mokseda as to how she would lead conjugal life with the victim before the occurrence and abscondence of Billal immediately after the occurrence till his arrest about one year after the occurrence are circumstances which lead to the irresistible conclusion that he abetted the murder of the victim.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Biltal Vs. The State, 20 BLD (AD 192</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37DLR (AD)193; 29 DLR (AD)1; 29 DLR (AD)269—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CIVIL LIABILITY</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Civil Liability</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is no specific promise for payment by any specific date and as such the High Court Division did not find any existence of initial intention for deception on the part of the accused petitioner. What happened between them was in due course of normal and regular business transaction for which no criminal action lies. At best the informant may go for civil action against the accused petitioner.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mohiuddin Md. Abdul Kader Vs. The State and another 20 BLD (HCD) 499</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CLEMENCY TO CONVICTS</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Clemency to convicts</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">By Acting President’s Order No. 70 of 1990 dated 14.1.1991, the sentence of imprisonment passed against any person prior to 6.12.1990 has been reduced to half.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dilbar Khan and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)337</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em> <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COGNIZANCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> Cognizance</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>“Cognizance” and “Prosecution”</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">“Cognizanc&amp;’ means taking notice of an offence by a “Criminal Court” initiating a judicial proceeding against an accused or taking steps to see whether there is any basis for initiating any proceeding.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">“Prosecution” on the other hand, means bringing specific accusations against the accused before the Court which, if satisfied, frames a formal charge and calls upon the accused to answer the same and proceeds with the trial. If no satisfactory ground for presenting the accused is found, be is discharged by the Court. Prosecution of the accused commences when the court hears the accusations and examines the materials on record for framing a formal charge against the accused.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdur Gafur Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD)604</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cognizance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since there is no requirement of law to record reasons for taking cognizance and as such there is no illegality in taking cognizance of the accused persons without assigning any reasons.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shainpukur Holding Ltd Vs.. Security Exchange Commission, 18BLD (HCD)61</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cognizance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sessions Judge cannot direct the Magistrate to take cognizance of a case. The power of Sessions Judge is strictly limited to directing a further enquiry into the petition of complaint. It will be for the Magistrate concerned to take or not to take cognizance after the result of the further enquiry.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A. Rouf and others Vs. The State and another, 20 BLD (HCD) 162</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cognizance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Neither the Sessions Judge nor the High Court Division has power to direct the Magistrate to take cognizance of a case. Their power is strictly limited to directing a further enquiry into the petition of complaint. It is for the Magistrate to take or not to take cognizance on holding further enquiry—Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898) Sections.203, 436, 439A and 561A</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Yusuf A Hassan Vs K M Rezaul Firdous, 16BLD(AD)55</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 35 DLR 140; 36 DLR (AD)58— Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cognizance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Examination of the complainant under section 200 Cr.P.C. is essential when a complaint is filed before the Magistrate. A ‘naraji’ petition is regarded as a fresh complaint and as such the examination of the complainant under Section 200 Cr.P.C. is also necessary.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abu Bakar and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)283</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37DLR227; 36DLR(AD)58; 19 DLR (SC) 426; 31 DLR (AD) 69; 38 DLR(AD)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">18, 8 BLD 438; 1968 Pakistan Criminal Law Journal, 1867—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cognizance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 27(1) of the Act though empowers a Special Tribunal to take cognizance of an offence triable under this Act without the accused being committed to it for trial, the Magistrate has still then jurisdiction to discharge an accused in an appropriate case despite the fact that the case is triable exclusively by the Special Tribunal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Narayan Chandra Das Vs. The State and another, 16BLD(HCD)421</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cognizance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the incriminating articles allegedly recovered from the accused do not attract the mischief of the Arms Act the Special Tribunal has no jurisdiction to take cognizance of the alleged offence and to try the case—Arms Act, 1878 (XI of 1878) Section—4</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nesar Ahmed @ Babul Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh and another, 17BLD (AD) 151</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cognizance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When it appears to the High Court Division that the dispute between the parties is of civil nature and it should be decided by the civil Court and the contending parties have already filed civil suits for adjudication of their respective claims, the criminal Court should not take cognizance of such a dispute—Cr.P.C Section—561A</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ansarul Haque Vs. Abdur Rahim and 4 others, 17BLD (AD) 143</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cognizance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Only a Sessions Court can take cognizance in respect of the offence regarding antiquities but in the instant case, the cognizance was purportedly taken by the Special Tribunal. Inview of section 28 of the Antiquities Act, such taking of cognizance by the Special Tribunal is without jurisdiction and illegal—Antiquities Act, 1 968(XIV of 1968)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">S.28</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Khalilur Rahman Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 476 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COMMISSION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Commission</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under Section 3(2) of the Act the Security Exchange Commission is a body corporate and shall sue and he sued in its name and under the provision of section 5(5) of the Act the Chairman is the Chief Executive of the Security Exchange Commission. Clause (ga) of section 11 of the Act provides that all the responsibilities and liabilities of the Government under the provisions of the Act shall be the responsibilities and liabilities of the Commission—Securities and Exchange Commission Act, 1993 Sections—3(2),5(5), 11(ga)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shainpukur Holding Ltd Vs. Security Exchange Commission, 18BLD (HCD) 61</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COMMON INTENTION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Common Intention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 34 applies when a criminal act is done by two or more persons in furtherance of the common intention of all of them whereas section 149 applies when any criminal act is committed by any member of an unlawful assembly in prosecution of the common object of that assembly—Penal Code, S.34.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ataur Rahman and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)391</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR 157—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Common Intention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Common intention within the meaning of this section presupposes a prior concert. There must be a prior meeting of the minds leading to a pre-arranged plan to commit the offence. The common intention to commit the offence invites the application of section 34 of the Penal Code. ft is intended to meet a case where the members of a party acted in furtherance of the common intention of all but it was difficult to prove exactly the part played by each of them. When two or more persons do a thing jointly involving criminal liability, it is just the same thing as if each of them had done it individually—Penal Code, 1860, S. 34</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Tajul Islam and others, 15BLD(HCD) 53</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 44 DLR 83; A. 1. R. 1957 (SC) 381; 16 DLR (SC) 94; 16 DLR (Dhaka) 189; 29 DLR (SC) 271; A. I. R. 1978 (SC) 1248; A. I.R. 1939 (Cal) 65; A.I.R. 1929 (Bombay) 327; PLD 1957 (West Pakistan) Lahore, 956; A.I.R. 1946 (Sind) 43; 12 DLR (SC) 217; 27 DLR (AD) 29; 44 DLR (AD) 10; A.I. R.1952 (SC) 474; A. 1. R. 1976 (SC) 2027— Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Common intention</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Section 34 and 149 of the Penal Code are two distinct and separate offences with different ingredients. Section 34 of the Penal Code involves a direct overt act on the part of the accused sharing a ‘common intention’ with others for the commission of an offence while section 149 of the Penal Code is essentially a vicarious liability for being a member of an unlawful assembly with the ‘common object’ of committing the offences. These two offences are of different nature as well— Penal Code, Ss.34 and 149.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abu Talukder Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 225</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Common Intention</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Common intention is an intention to commit the crime actually committed and each accused can be convicted if he shared the common intention. This is anterior to the commission of the crime and it has no reference to the time of the commission of the offence.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Unless a person is found that he carried with him a dangerous weapon he cannot be convicted u/s 148 of the Penal Code on a general statement that the accused persons carried with them dangerous weapons—Penal Code,Ss. 34 and 148</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Haque Mat bar and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)178</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COMMON OBJECT</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Common object</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When it is found that a particular offence has been committed by an individual member of the unlawful assembly, which was neither done in prosecution of the common object of the assembly nor other members of the assembly knew that such an offence would be committed, such members of the assembly cannot be held liable u/s 149 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Sattar and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD) 133</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Common Object</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">For a conviction under section 302/149 of the Penal Code the prosecution must prove and the court must find that the members of the unlawful assembly had the common object of killing the victim. In the absence of a common object for murder, conviction under sections 302/149 of the Penal Code is not sustainable.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Khalilur Rahrnan, 15BLD (HCD) 315</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COMPLAINT</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Complaint by an Attorney</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is a settled principle of criminal law that any person having knowledge of any offence may set the law in motion by making a complaint to the appropriate authority even though he is not personally aggrieved.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The holder of a duly constituted power of attorney is competent to file and prosecute a criminal case on behalf of the grantor before a competent Court and he is entitled to do every necessary thing in that connection on behalf of the grantor—Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898) Section.3(h).</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Tamizul Haque Vs. Anisul Haque, 16 BLD(AD)206</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> Haisbury’s Laws of England (4<sup>th</sup> Ed.) P. 438, Paras-730 and 731; A.I.R. 1916 (Bombay) 155; A.I.R. 1930(Bombay) 511; A.LR. 1959 Bombay 386—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Complaint</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Summons to produce document or other thing</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">For the purpose of enquiry into a complaint no formal case or F.I.R. is necessary before a police officer can apply for permission to the Sessions Judge for production and seizure of any document. But before according permission the Sessions Judge will have to satisfy himself that the police officer is investigating on an alleged offence and is not merely fishing for information to harass, intimidate or coerce innocent persons—Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898) Section—94</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Messrs Hamidia Oil Mills Vs. District Anti-Corruption Officer, Chittagong and ors, 16BLD(AD)220</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Complaint</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Appearance of the accused in a complaint case</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a complaint case, on the first date fixed for appearance of the accused as well as on each subsequent date to which the hearing may be adjourned, the complainant (if he is not a public servant) must personally appear before the Court and that in case of the failure of the complainant to appear in the Court on any such date, it is imperative for the Magistrate to acquit the summoned accused persons unless for some reasons the Magistrate thinks it proper to adjourn the hearing of the case to some other date.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Ayub Ali Bangali Vs. Mia Munir Ahmed, 13BLD(HCD)224</em></strong>.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A.I.R. 1948 (Madras) 45; 36 DLR. 349—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COMPROMISE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Compromise </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">As the wife has compromised the case with her husband, the parties are permitted to compound the offence for enabling them to live peacefully. The accused is acquitted of the charge on compromise—Muslim Family Laws Ordinance, 1961, S. 6(5)(b)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Azizul Hoque Howlader Vs. The State and another, 14BLD(HCD)149</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em>COMMUTATION OF DEATH SENTENCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Commutation of death sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The facts that at the relevant time the appellant was having a parasitical existence on the family of his maternal grandfather and he was at their biddings, he did not take any part in the act of killing Hazera, the real culprits escaped punishment for want of legal evidence and that upon confessing his guilt in court he begged for mercy, were considered extenuating circumstunces for commutation of the sentence of death to imprisonment for life.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Awal Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD) 224</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Commutation of sentence of death</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When before causing the death of his wife the condemned prisoner suffered for some time from a bitter sense of being wronged by his wayward wife, a case for commutation of death sentence is made out. The sentence of death is therefore commutted to one of imprisonment for life.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Commutation of death sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Pangs of separation from the wife, frustrations of the condemned prisoner arising out of the failure of his efforts to get back his wife and the serious mental torments and agonies preceding the unfortunate killing are sufficient mitigating circumstances for commutation of the death sentence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Abul Kalam Azad, 16BLD (HCD)80</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Commutation of death sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The condemned-prisoner is in the condemned-cell for a period of 4 years and 11 months and he has been suffering mental agony of death within the death cell for all these days. Considering the facts and circumstances of the case and also fact that the death sentence remained pending for 4 years and 11 months, the High Court Division held that the sentence of life imprisonment instead of death will meet the ends of justice—Cr.P.C; S. 376</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Md. Monir Ahmed alias Monir Hossain, 18BLD (HCD) 605</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Commutation of death sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the fact that the murder was not committed by a vicious macho male and before causing the death of his wife the condemned prisoner suffered for some time from a bitter sense of being wronged by his wayward wife, a case for commutation of sentence of death is made out. The sentence of death imposed on the condemned prisoner is commuted to one of imprisonment for life.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Zahiruddin Vs. The State,15BLD(AD )85</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 36 Cr.L.J. (1935)683; Inre Perumal Kudumban, AIR 1940 (Madras) 562, 39DLR (AD)(1987) 196; 44DLR (AD) (1992) 225— Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Commutation of sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The condemned prisoner was arrested on 11.12.91, conviction and sentence to death on 20.9.94. He has suffered the agony of death sentence for more than 5 years, moreover he is a man of 25 years. He has an old mother, one wife and two children to support and look after. He is not a hardened criminal. He found his wife (deceased) in illicit connection and in an inappropriate situation with Deiwar and thus suffered from a sense of being wronged by her. He cannot be termed as ‘vicious macho male’ and accordingly the sentence of death is reduce to life imprisonment.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Billal Hossain, 20 BLD (HCD) 45</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CONFESSION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The confession of an accused recorded under section 164 Cr.P.C. being a matter of serious consideration at the trial of a murder case, the Public Prosecutor acted in an irresponsible and negligent manner in not utilisilig the same. The learned Judge was also wrong in hastily rejecting the prosecution’s prayer for examining the Magistrate.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mosammat Amena Khatun Vs. The State and others, 14BLD(HCD) 332</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To be the basis of conviction a confession must be voluntary and true and it must also be inculpatory in nature. A retracted confession requires independent and reliable corroboration before it is accepted and acted upon. Confession of a co-accused is no evidence against other accused persons. It may, however, lend assurance to other evidence— Evidence Act 1872, Ss. 24.30.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dula Miah alias Nurul Islam and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)477</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27 DLR(AD)29; 44DLR(AD) 10, I.L.R. Indian Appeals 147; 12 DLR(SC) 156; 12DLR(SC)217; 36 DLR 185; 16 DLR 147; 45DLR 171; A.I.R. 1939 (PC) 47; 25 DLR 399; A.I.R. 1957(SC)107;6BLD(AD)1—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Extra-judicial confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">By its very nature it is a very weak type of evidence and it is required to be scrutinized with great care and caution. To base a conviction on such confession it must be corroborated by other evidence of unimpeachable character.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Zafar and others Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)280</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 33 DLR 320; 11 D.L.R. (HC) 365; 27 D.L.R.(SC) 1; 38 DLR 289; 41 DLR 11; 50 Cr.L. J. 569</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The unretracted confession and the unqualified admission of guilt by the accused at the trial can legally form the sole basis of his conviction—Cr.P.C; S. 164</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Awal and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)187</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1952 S.C.R. (India )526—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a confessional statement has been properly recorded by the Magistrate in accordance with the requirements of sections 164 and 364 Cr. P.C., the said confessional statement is admissible in evidence under section 80 of the Evidence Act even without examining the recording Magistrate.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mosammat Amena khatun Vs. State and others, 14BLD(HCD)332</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the record shows that the superintendent of the jail hospital reported to the Upazila Magistrate that the appellants were admitted in the jail hospital for treatment with multiple injuries on their person just on the following day of recordings their confessional statements and the conduct of the Investigation Officer is not above board, such confessions must be held to be involuntary— Cr.P.C. 164</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bachhu alias Ekramul Haq and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)588</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It requires that a Magistrate before recording any confession shall have to explain to the confessing accused that if he makes any confession it may be used against him.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In this case the learned Magistrate having not warned the confessing accused in terms of this mandatory provision of law, the confession must be left out of consideration— Cr.P.C. 164(3)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bachhu alias Ekramul Haq and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)588</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 30 of the Evidence Act provides that the confession of a co-accused can be taken into consideration along with other evidence but it does not say that such a confession amounts to proof. In the absence of any substantive evidence on record, either direct or circumstantial, implicating the appellant in the alleged offence, the confessional statement of a co-accused can never be treated as a substantive evidence and no conviction can be based on such confessional statement. Evidence Act, 1872 (I of 1872), Section—30</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ustar Ali Vs. The State, 18BLD (AD) 43</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1LR76 Indian appeals 147; 27DLR 29; 44DLR(AD) 10—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Statement of witness recorded under section 164 Cr.P.C. is never a substantive evidence and it cannot be used against the accused. The prosecution is, however, entitled to impeach the credit of its own witness as it is permissible under section 164 of the Evidence Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mahbubur Rahman and others Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD) 309</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Assurance or caution are necessary in case of long detention of the accused in the police custody before the confessional statement is recorded. In the absence of such assurance or caution rendered the confessional statement untrue and involuntary and as such it cannot be used against the accused-appellant nor against his co-accused—Section—164</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Ali Hossain, 18BLD (HCD) 655</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is an accepted proposition of law that the confession of a co-accused is no evidence against the other accused. Under section 30 of the Evidence Act, the court may take such a confession into consideration only to lend assurance to other evidence which by itself must be sufficient to support a conviction— Evidence Act 1872, Ss. 3 and 30</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Awal and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)187</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1952 S.C.R. (India)526—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Belated retraction of confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Once a confession is found by the court to be true and voluntary, a retraction is of no help to the confessing accused. The necessity of some sort of corroboration in such a case is not a requirement of law although it is usually desired as a rule of prudence. S.164</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Tajul Islam and others, 15BLD(HCD) 53</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Statements made by the witnesses and recorded by a Magistrate under section 164 Cr.P.C. are not confessional statements. The learned Additional Sessions Judge was obviously wrong in treating the said statements as confessional statements and misdirected himself in convicting the appellants thereon.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Muslim and others Vs. The State, 15 BLD(HCD) 298</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">A statement made under section 164 Cr.P.C. is not a substantive evidence. It may be used only for corroborating or contradicting a witness. Consequently, when a witness completely resiles from his earlier statement and gives a different story to the court, his statement under section 164 Cr.P.C. loses all its relevance.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Jiaur Rahman Vs. The State, 15 BLD(HCD) 459</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Confession of a co-accused is no evidence against other accused persons and the same cannot be made the sole basis of their conviction. The court can however consider the confession of a co-accused to lend assurance to other independent evidence—Evidence Act, 1872, S. 3</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Md. Musa, 15BLD (HCD)169</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Under this section there is no necessity of a formal proof of a confession duly recorded by a Magistrate in accordance with the provisions of section 164 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. The examination of the recording Magistrate is not essential in such a case— Evidence Act, 1872, S. 80</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Tajul Islam and others, 15BLD(HCD) 53</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 3 of the Evidence Act the confession of a co-accused is no evidence against other accused persons but under section 30 of the said Act the confession of a co-accused can be taken into consideration against other accused persons only for the limited purpose of lending assurance to other legal evidence on record. Evidence Act, 1872, S.3 and 30</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Tajul Islam and others, 15 BLD(HCD) 53</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">A confessional statement even if it is partly true or partly false or in other words does not disclose the full picture can be used against the maker and there is no legal bar in upholding the conviction on the basis of the confession.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Billal Hossain, 20 BLD (HCD)45</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession of a co-accused, use of</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Even if the confessional statement of one accused is found to be true and voluntary, still his confession cannot be used against those who are co-accused in the case, as the basis for convicting them when there is no other evidence against them.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr Ishaq Ali Vs The State(1993) 13BLD (HCD)236</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 5DLR 369; 18 DLR (West Pakistan) 112—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the statements of the confessing accused are recorded under section 164 Cr. P.C. after 3 days of their arrest by the police and the learned Magistrate fails to ascertain as to where the accused persons had been kept during this period and the prosecution offers no explanation for the same, the confessions become doubtful.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Kashem and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)120</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the fact that the accused was kept in police custody for 3 days preceding the recording of his confession by the Magistrate on a plain piece of paper without properly complying with the provions of section 164 Cr.P.C. and the forwarding report by the police mentioned injuries on the person of the accused, such a confession becomes inadmissible in evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Alaluddin alias Alauddin and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)350</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 43DLR (HC) 389; 913LD (HC) 477; 43DLR (HC) 512—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a confessing accused was kept in police custody for 2 days immediately preceding his production before the Magistrate for recording his confessional statement such a confession must be taken with utmost caution. Section—164.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Jabbar Vs. The State, 16BLD (HCD)552</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provides that the confession of a co-accused is not a substantive evidence but it can be used against the co-accused to ‘lend assurance to other evidence on record—Evidence Act, 1872(1 of 1872) Section.30.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Moslemuddin and another Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)3</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Prolonged police custody immediately preceding the recording of confession, if not otherwise properly explained, is sufficient to brand it as involuntary—Section. 164</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Faruk Mahajan and ors. Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD)15</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Columns 3,4 and 8 of the prescribed form for recording confessions have not been filled up by the Magistrate. Therefore this piece of paper (confessional statement of the petitioner) is highly suspicious and is therefore unworthy of consideration by any Court— Section—164(3)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mrs. Jobaida Rashid Vs. The State, 17 BLD(HCD) 352</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> ‘Principles and Digest of the Law of Evidence—By M. Monir; Queen Vs. Blake, 1844 6.B.126; 17BLD(1997)11; 17 BLD(AD) (1997) 54; 17 BLD (AD) (1997) 163: 2BLC (AD)(1997) 75—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Before recording the confessional statement the recording Magistrate did not assure the confessing accused that he would not be sent to police custody after recording his confession and, in fact, the accused was sent to police custody instead of judicial custody after recording his confession. The procedure adopted by the learned Magistrate is unacceptable to judicial scrutiny. Such a confession leaves enough scope for lurking fear in the mind of the confessing accused and as such it cannot be relied upon—Cr.P.C. Section—164</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nil Ratan Biswas and others Vs. The State,17BLD(HCD) 613</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> PLD1958 (Lahore) 559; PLD 1964 (WP) (Peshawar) 1; PLD 1960 (Karachi) 674—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The evidentiary value of the confession of a co-accused is practically nil against other accused persons in the absence of any independent corroborative evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Faruk Mahajan and ors. Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD) 15</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The judicial consensus is that the provisions of section 164(3) of the Code must be strictly complied with before a confession is admitted into evidence and it is used against the accused. The recording Magistrate is under an obligation to make a real and substantial inquiry to ascertain the voluntariness of the confession. Section—164</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Alauddin Khan Pathan and ors Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 74</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> AIR 1936(P.C) 253, 41DLR 64, 50 DLR 17—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Formalities as provided under sections 164 and 364 of the Code have not been complied with in recording the confessional statement of the accused. It appears that the confessing accused made the confessional statement after coming from the police remand which in no way remove the doubt that the said confessional statement is the product of threat, coercion and physical torture, moreover, there is no independent evidence to corroborate the same. The confessional statement appears to be exculpatory having no active part in the occurrence by the confessing accused and as such the same cannot be used against its maker and as such the conviction cannot be sustained on the basis of said confessional statement.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Akbar Ali Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 268</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">By its very nature it is a very weak type of evidence. It is unsafe to base conviction of an accused on his extra judicial confession alone. The value of such confession as evidence depends on the veracity of witnesses to whom it is made. In a case of such confession material corroboration by evidence of unimpeachable character is required—Evidence Act, 1872 (l of 1872), Section—24</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Hasen Ali, 19BLD (HCD) 418</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 30 of the Act provides that the confession of a co-accused can be taken into consideration to lend assurance to other substantive evidence on record, but it never says that such confession amounts to proof. In the instant case there being no substantive evidence, either direct or circumstantial, implicating Ansar Ali, Montaz, Bhola and Hormuz Ali in the alleged commission of dacoity, the learned Assistant Sessions Judge was wrong in convicting the appellants on the basis of the confessional statement of their two co-accused—Evidence Act, I 872, S. 30</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Ansar Ali Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD)224</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> Ustar Ali Vs. The State, 18BLD (AD) (1998)43—relied upon</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Though there is no requirement of law that the recording Magistrate should invariably assure an accused that he will not be remanded to police custody whether he confesses or not, but it becomes incumbent upon him to do so when the investigating Officer prays for police remand after recording confession. Variation and contradictions between the prosecution case and the confessional statement under the confession unacceptable. S. 164, Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Zakir Hossain Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 671</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 164 sub-section 3, whether speaks of the manner of recording confession of an accused. S.164(3)</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sub-section (3) of section 164 Cr.P.C. speaks of the manner as to how a confession of an accused is to be recorded by a Magistrate and this is a mandatory provision and failure to comply with it shall make the confession invalid and unreliable. This provision of law along with provisions of section 364 of the Code of Criminal Procedure require to be strictly observed and followed to make the confession voluntary and true in the real sense to be fit for reliance for convicting an accused in his confession.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Hossain and others Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 311</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession—confessional statement</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Before a confession is accepted and relied upon it must be found not only to be voluntary but also to be true. Section—164.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Moslemuddin and another Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)3</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession of co-accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 30 of the Act provides that the confession of a co-accused can be taken into consideration to lend assurance to other substantive evidence on record but it never says that such confession amounts to proof. In the instant case, there being no substantive evidence, either direct or circumstantial, implicating the appellant in the alleged dacoity except as to some evidence about the motive of the offence, the trial Court was wrong in treating the confessional statement of the co-accused as substantive evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mojibar Vs The State, 20 BLD (HCD)273</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confessional statement</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The objection on the use of certain confessional statements recorded in connection with another case and used by the investigation officer in the instant case may be raised at the time of trial and the admissibility of these may be decided by the trial Court at that time. The objection on the recording of the statements under section 161 of the Code and the admissibility of the confessional statements can not therefore be decided in this petition.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Major (Retd) Bazlul Huda Vs. The State, 20 BLD (AD)236 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confessional Statement</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Failure to comply with requirement of the form 6, 8 and 9 and giving a certificate in his own hand do not vitiate the confessional statements because at best it will mean violation or non-compliance of some circulars.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">On perusal of column 6 it is found that necessary questions were put to the condemned prisoner and he answered them all in the affirmative. As to column 9 it appears that this column is not applicable since the Magistrate found that the statement made by condemned prisoner was voluntary.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs.
