Billal Hossain, 20 BLD (HCD) 45</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confessional statement of a co-accused— use of</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Confessional statement of one accused, even if found to be true and voluntary, whether can be used against a co-accused.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Even if the confessional statement of one accused is found to be true and voluntary, still his confession cannot be used against those who are co-accused in the case, as the basis for convicting them when there is no other evidence against them.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Ishaq Ali. Vs. The State, 13 BLD(HCD) 236</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 5DLR.369; 18 DLR (West Pakistan) 112—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CONFISCATION OF PROPERTY</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confiscation of property</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In section 409 of the Penal Code there is no provision for confiscation of the property. But the Appellate Division refused to consider this prayer of the petitioner at this stage as this point was not specifically raised before the High Court Division and no ground was taken before the Court.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bibhuti Bushan Talukder Vs. The State, I7BLD (AD,)168</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em>CONTEMPT OF COURT</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Contempt of Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Whether a learned Counsel may bring any matter to the notice of the Court that may, in his view, call for an action under the Contempt of Court Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Whether an Advocate has every right to represent his client and protect his interests by filing an appeal before the appellate forum.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">(i) A learned Counsel may bring any matter to the notice of the Court that may, in his view, call for an action under the Contempt of Court Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">(ii) An Advocate has every right to represent his client and protect his interests by filing an appeal before the appellate forum, but he cannot, while the appeal is pending, ascribing somethings, by publishing a news report in his capacity as a Court reporter, which is not in the judgment.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>M. Saleem Ultah Vs. The State 13BLD (AD)3</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 15 DLR(SC) 355, AIR 1936 (P.C) 141 at pages 145-146; 28DLR(HCD)285; St. James Evening Post Case,(1742) 2ATK649; (1895)11 TIR 1976; (1922) Ich. 276; (1900) 2QB 36(at pp 40); (1899) AC 549(4); 2 EHRR 245—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Contempt of Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">By making absurd comments, uninformed criticism and vituperative innuendoes on the judgments of the Appellate Division, the learned Judges of the High Court Division have placed themselves well within the perimeter of Contempt of Court Act (XII of 1926)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Sher Ali and others Vs. The State and another) 14BLD(AD) 84</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 35 DLR. (AD) 147; 45 DLR(AD) 9 and 175; A.I.R. 1945 (PC) 18.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Contempt of Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The power of the High Court Division to institute a contempt proceeding is a special one. It is competent to deal with the matter summarily and to adopt its own procedure. Contempt of Court Act (XII of 1926) Ss. 2&amp;3</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Badsha Mia and others Vs. Abdul Latif Majumder and others, 14BLD(AD) 237</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A. I. R. 1954 (SC) 186—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Contempt of Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A statement which neither directly nor indirectly imputes any aspersion to a Judge and the Court nor does it contain any scandalous statement against a Judge or the Judiciary to lower down its image in the estimation of the general public but serves as a constructive criticism relating to the mode of appointment of the Judges of the Supreme Court does not constitute contempt of Court. It is more so, when the disputed statement makes no reference to the judgment of any Judge of either Division of the Supreme Court or of any Judge subordinate thereto— Contempt of Courts Act, 1926 (XII of 1926) Section.3</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Dr. Ahmed Husain Vs. Shamsul Huq Chowdhury, 16BLD(HCD)116</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Contempt of Court</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">A bonafide and honest criticism of a judicial exercise is permissible only when it is aimed at improving its quality by way of healthy and constructive criticism. But when a writing is couched in scandalous and offensive language and it brings the Court into disrespect and disrepute in the eye of the general public and castigates its dignity and majesty, the writer concerned does commit contempt of the Court—Contempt of Courts Act, 1926 Section.3</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The Judges of the High Court Division Vs. Ashok Kumar Karmaker and others, 16BLD(HCD)233</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Contempt of Court</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Arms of the High Court Division are long enough to catch and punish any delinquent how high soever—Contempt of Court Act, 1926 (XII of 1926) Sections—2 and 3</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Almas Ali Vs. Sohrab Miah and others, 17BLD (HCD) 150</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Contempt of Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The issuance of the Rule for contempt of Court, appearance of the opposite party before the Court in person as per direction of the Court, expression of sorrow and remorse before the Court by the opposite party together with his past sufferings and the detailed description of prevailing circumstances and the recent happenings are sufficient grounds to take a lenient view towards him in the instant case. It is expected that journalists should be very vigilant, alert and careful in the future in publishing Court proceedings and news and in making comments with regard to the judiciary—Contempt Court Act, 1926; Sections.2 and 3</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Shafique Rehman, 17BLD (HCD) 278</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Contempt of Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Refusal to give a hearing to a contemner is the judicial discretion of the Court. But this discretion has to be exercised sparingly. The Court may, however, refuse to hear a contemner for gross violation of the Court’s order impeding the course of justice. Sections—2 and 3</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Quasem Cotton Mills Ltd. Vs. Bangladesh Shilpa Rin Sangstha, 17BLD (HCD) 625</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1868 L.R. Vol.1V Court of Exchequer, 13; 46DLR617; 82 I.C. 292 and (1990)2 All. E.R. 1; (1952) 2 All E.R. 567 at page 574—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Contempt of Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The jurisdiction of contempt must be taken with utmost care so that it is not used on occasions or in cases to which it is not appropriate. In the instant case, the learned Judges of the High Court Division took a too drastic step for punishing the appellant for contempt of court, being a little touchy and unduly sensative, which was not at all called for—Contempt Court Act, 1926; Sections.2 and 3.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>S.A.M. Iqbal Vs. The State and another, 18BLD (AD) 195 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A.I.R. 1958 (Calcutta) 474; 14DLR (SC)273; 15 DLR(SC)150—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Contempt of Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In both the news items it has not been stated that the borrower would be able to pay on the basis of earlier re-schedulement of the loan and a picture .was deliberately depicted as if this Court stood in the way of the borrower’s repayment of the loan. It appears in both the news items this Court has been maligned and scandalized to lower it in the estimatation of the members of the public. In both the news items name of the reporter has not been published. Therefore the Rule was issued only on the editors and publisher and printer. The High Court Division can very much realies that the contemners have been used as persons by some unseen hands to make the borrower a hero at the cost of honour and prestige of this Court.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is grossest type of contempt to malign and scandalize the highest Court and its Judges by publishing untrue and twisted news and comment in the newspaper. The High Court Division held that the contemners guilty of committing contempt of this Court. Since the contemners have tendered unqualified apology and have thrown themselves to the mercy of this Court considering that they are not the authors of the contemptuous writing, this court decided to accept their apology subject to the direction—Contempt Court Act, 1926; Sections—2 and 3</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shahidul Islam Vs. Mahbubul Alam &amp; ors., 19BLD (HCD) 601</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CONVERSION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Conversion of memo of appeal into a memo of revision</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A time barred appeal was filed under section 30 of the Special Powers Act challenging an order of conviction and sentence. Since the Special Powers Act makes no provision for condonation of delay, section 5 of the Limitation Act not being made applicable to Special Powers Act cases, the convict was left with no remedy for vindication of his grievances. By exercising inherent power under section 561A Cr.P.C. the High Court Division converted the memo of appeal into a memo of revision to examine the legality and propriety of the impugned order of conviction for securing the ends of justice.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sohail Ahmed Chowdhury Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 239</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em> <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COPIES OF STATEMENT U/S. 164 CR.P.C BEFORE FILING POLICE REPORT</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Copies of Statement u/s. 164 Cr.P.C before filing Police Report</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An accused is not entitled to get copies of the statement under section 164 of the Code of Criminal Procedure before filing Police Report under section 173 of the Code.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">To allow an accused an access to documents like the statements under section 164 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, before filing charge-sheet, may prejudice the investigation.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Mobarak Hossain @ Jewel Vs. The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 422</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CORROBORATION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Corroboration</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Corroboration of the statement of the prosecutrix—Whether it has been a long practice for insisting corroboration of the statement of the prosecutrix.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Saidur Rahman Neoton and others Vs. The State 13BLD (AD) 79 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 12 DLR(SC)165; 19 DLR(SC)256; 1952 Supreme Court Reports 377—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Corroboration</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Corroborative evidence is not an imperative component of Judicial credence in every case of rape. Corroboration as a condition for judicial reliance on the restimong of a victim of sex crime is not a requirement of law but merely a guidance of prudence under a given circumstances. The rule is not that corroboration is essential before there can be a conviction. The testimony of the victim of sexual assault is vital and unless there are compelling reasons which necessite looking for corroboration of her statement, the Court should find no difficulty in acting on the testimony of a victim of sex crime alone to convict an accused where res testimony inspires confidence and is found to be reliable.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Al Amin Vs. The State, 19BLD(HCD) 307</em></strong></p><p><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COURT</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Court</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">The deposition of a witness before the Committing Court may be treated as substantive evidence when it is transferred to the Sessions record either under section 288 Cr.P.C. or under the provisions of section 33 of the Evidence Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jalal and 3 others Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD) 588</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COURT OF LAW</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Court of Law</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The petitioner was a professor of Dhaka University and is one of the intellectuals of the country but this does not absolve him of the responsibility of appearing before a Court and asking for bail which is due to him in the facts and circumstances of the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Ahmed Sharif Vs. The State and another, 17BLD (HCD) 235</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COURT OF SESSIONS</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Court of Sessions</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 9(1) of the Code gives Government the power to establish a Court of Sessions for every Sessions Division and appoint a Judge for such Court. Section 9(2) of the Code gives the Government the power to direct at what place or places the Court of Sessions shall hold its sittings—Code of Criminal Procedure, .1898 (V of 1898) Section.9(l)(2)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sayeed Farook Rahman Vs. The Sessions Judge of the Court of Sessions, Dhaka and others, 17BLD (AD) 177</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> AIR 1988 (SC) 1838—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>COURT WITNESS</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Court witness</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Under Section 540 Cr.P.C: the Court has power to re-call any witness and to reexamine him either on his own motion or at the instance of a party.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mainuddin Howlader Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)82</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CRIMINAL APPEAL</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Appeal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If a superior Court disposes a criminal matter, more particularly a criminal appeal of such a great importance in such a light hearted manner without any application of judicial mind then that will have a demoralizing effect on the subordinate judiciary in disposing criminal justice in Bangladesh.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mahmudul Islam @ Ratan Vs. The State), 20 BLD(AD)249</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CRIMINAL CONSPIRACY</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Conspiracy</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Petitioner’s jubilation might be the result of moral support in the activities of her husband but for that alone it cannot be said that she was in the criminal conspiracy—Section.120B</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jobaida Rashid Vs. The State, 17BLD (HCD)366</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27 DLR (AD) 29; 37DLR (AD) 139; 7 BCR (AD) 143—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CRIMINAL INTENTION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="text-align: justify;">The ingredients of section 409 of the Code are misappropriation to commit criminal breach of trust in respect of property over which he had dominion as public servant. The appellant had no criminal intention to commit such criminal breach of trust in respect of the property which was held within his dominion, rather it shows his bonafide intention to help one of the customers of the Bank in tiding over his financial difficulties and as such the appellant is entitled to acquittal as of right.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.K.M. Mohiuddin Vs. The State, 20 BLD(HCD)1 72</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CRIMINAL JURISDICTION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Jurisprudence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a certain fact or circumstance can be interpreted in favour of the accused and also in favour of the prosecution, the well settled principle of criminal jurisprudence is that the interpretation which is favourable to the accused must be accepted.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Asadur Rahrnan alias Asad and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)290</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CRIMINAL MISAPPROPRIATION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Misappropriation</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Audited and approved accounts—No guarantee against criminal prosecution</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Even if the accounts of a company are audited and approved the same cannot exonerate persons in charge of management of the company from facing a criminal trial on allegations of misappropriation of funds of the company by falsification of accounts and concealment of documents, in the absence of any specific bar under any law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Khorshed Alam Vs. Azizur Rahman and another, 15BLD(HCD) 639</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal misappropriation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere inability to pay back a sum of money entrusted, the sale proceeds of a property entrusted will not establish the fact of criminal misappropriation, if mens rea is not establish. The prosecution must establish, apart from entrustment also that the accused had dishonesty misprinted the property entrusted—Penal Code; S.405</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.H.M. Siddique Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD)85</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CRIMINAL PROSECUTION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal prosecution</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Criminal prosecution for breach of rules of statutory bodies and preventive measures taken to maintain peace, where dispute as to immovable property are involved, do not stand on the same footing. But even when criminal prosecution is involved, a suit is not completely barred.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jobeda Khatun vs. Momtaz Begum and others 13BLD(AD)31</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> AIR. 1924(Nag.)80; 23 DLR(SC)14; 36 DLR(SC)14; 36 DLR.(AD)44; In re; N.P. Essappa Chettiar, A1R1942 (Mad) 756; A.I.R. 1924(Cal) 334; AIR 1928 (Cal) 464; ILR 1950 All 543; AIR 1959(SC)960; (1909)21C266; AIR 1938(Pat) 606; 1970 UJ (SC)75; Twenty one year’s Supreme Court Digest, 1950-70 by Sk. Agarwal, volume 2(1972) at page 662—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>  <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CRIMINAL TRESPASS</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trespass</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sections 441/447 provides that whoever enters into or upon property in the possession of another with intent to commit an offence or to intimidate, insult or annoy any person in possession of such property, or having lawfully entered into or upon such property, unlawfully remains there with intent thereby to intimidate, insult or annoy any such person, or with intent to commit an offence, is said to commit “criminal trespass”.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The dominant intention of the appellant was to annoy the complainant who was in possession of the case land. The complainant might not be present at the time of the illegal entry but he came to the scene thereafter and opposed the appellant who, despite his protest, carried on the work of construction. So the ingredients of section 441/447 of the Penal Code have been well-established.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mohammad Ali Member Vs. Abdul Fazul Mia &amp; ano., 19BLD(AD)260</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CRIMINAL TRIAL</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absence of necessary link in evidence— Conviction set aside</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Neither the specimen signature nor the specimen thumb impression of accused Nurun Nabi was taken for comparison with that of the identifier appearing in the disputed document. The mere appearance of the name of Nurun Nabi as the identifier of the disputed document without any evidence to show that accused Nurun Nabi had really identified the purported executant, the conviction of appellant Nurun Nabi is not sustainable.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Majid Sarkar and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD)49</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It required the trial Court to conclude trial within the statutory period from the date the case was received by it and not from the date of framing of the charge—Cr.P.C; Section—339C</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Master Giasuddin and others Vs. The State, 17BLD (AD)35</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Even in the absence of recovery of the deadbody of a victim a conviction can be secured for an offence of murder under section 302 of the Penal Code if there is legal and sufficient evidence on record to prove the commission of murder by the accused. in the face of reliable evidence of eye-witnesses proving murder of the victim by the accused and subsequent removal of the deadbody by them for causing disappearance thereof, conviction under sections 302/34 of the Penal Code is maintainable—Penal Code, 1860 (XLV of 1860) Sections—302/34 and 201/34</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shaha and others Vs. The State, I7BLD (AD) 2.1</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The trial Court failed to apply its judicial mind as to the age of appellant Shiplu, who appears to have been below the age of 16. This makes the order of conviction and sentence in respect of Shiplu illegal and the same is liable to be set aside for want of jurisdiction—Children Act, 1974(XXXIX of 1974) Sections—2(f), 6 and 66</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shiplu and another Vs. The State, 17 BLD(HCD) 243</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 3 BLD (HCD) (1983) 193—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">All incriminating pieces of evidence must be brought to the notice of the accused while examining him under section 342 of the Code of Criminal Procedure for affording him opportunity to offer his explanation.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mizanur Rahman alias Mija alias Mizan Vs. The State,17BLD(HCD) 82</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The offence committed by the appellant finds corroboration from the medical report which found marks of violence on the person of the victim clearly indicating that it is a case of rape and the evidence of the witnesses has clearly established that the appellant committed the crime of rape and as such the trial court has rightly found the appellant guilty of the offence under section 376 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Tafazzal Hossain Khan Vs. The State, 17BLD (HCD) 306</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To sustain a charge of smuggling it must be proved by the prosecution that the seized articles were recovered from the possession of the accused and that those were contraband goods—Special Powers Act, 1974; S.25B</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Tomezuddin Biswas alias Kalu and another Vs. The State, 17BLD (HCD) 174</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Act does not provide for any consequence for failure of the trial Court to conclude the trial within one hundred and twenty days. This limitation of time for disposal of cases is merely directory in nature—Women and Children Oppression (Special Provisions) Act, 1995 (XVIII of 1995), Section—20</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Habibur Rahman Zakir and others Vs. The State, 17BLD (HCD) 440</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Court is competent to proceed with the trial in the absence of any accused person who is either absconding or absenting after his release on bail. Hence, the practice of adjourning criminal trial frequently on the prayer of the defence inspite of appearance of the prosecution witnesses on the ground of absence of any accused over-looking provisions of section 339B Cr.P.C is contrary to law and should be discontinued. S.339B</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mahabubur Rahman Khan @ Tipu Vs. The State, 13BLD(HCD)268</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27 DLR 25; BCR 1990 (HCD) 236— Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Omission or defect in the examination of the accused persons under section 342 Cr.P.C. does not occasions any miscarriage of justice and the same is mere irregularity and curable u/s. 537 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Hafez Munshi alias Hafizur Rahman and others Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 461</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref: </strong>39 DLR 437—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">From the judgment of the trial Court it is found that the learned Sessions Judge has not properly assessed the evidence of the case and failed to consider the gross discrepancies, contradictions and omissions as well as admissions of the PWs on vital points and as such it is not safe to maintain the order of conviction and sentence on the evidence on record of the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shaheb Ali Munshi and another Vs The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 148</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From the evidence of PW4 and the inquest report it appears that Biswajit did not commit suicide rather somebody killed him and then tried to hang the dead body in the cowshed in a sitting position.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gulzar Biswas &amp; ors. Vs. The State, 20 BLD(HCD)550</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a criminal trial determination of fact is the main task before the court and such determination is dependent upon consideration of answers given by prosecution witnesses in their cross-examination.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mahmudul Islam @ Ratan Vs. The State, 20 BLD(AD)249</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Question as to whether following stoppage of proceedings and release of the accused under section 339C(4) of the Code, a fresh complaint/proceeding against the same set of accused persons by the same set of complainants upon the self same allegations is permissible or not.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is no legal bar for instituting a fresh prosecution on the self-same facts a preceding is stopped and accused released under section 339C(4) of the Code of Criminal Procedure.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Niamat Ali sheikh and others Vs. Begum Enayutun Noor and others 13BLD (AD)11</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1981) 33 DLR (HCD) 205 distigushed—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the appellate Court and the High Court Division refused to believe the prosecution case, it is settled law that the Appellate Division, merely because a different view is possible of the evidence, does not interfere with an order of acquittal. Section—439 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abdul Hamid Mollah Vs. Ali Mollah and another 13BLD (AD)127</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> Siraj Din Vs. Kala, 16DLR(SC) 94—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In a criminal case the Court has only to see if the prosecution has been able to prove the guilt of the accused beyond reasonable doubts. Non-putting of a possible accused on trial cannot by itself be a ground for questioning the conviction of an accused whose guilt has been proved satisfactorily.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Afsaruddin Bhuiyan Vs. The State, 18BLD (AD)149</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In criminal matters the accused should get all protection under the law but it is also important that the law should not be stretched too far so that big companies against whom serious allegations of foul play concerning national economy are being made before the Court by a statutory authority, can themselves overtake the law by resourceful enterprise in raising ingenious contentions in order to frustrate the prosecution on the threshold. The Court must strike a balance but the learned Sessions Judge failed to maintain that balance which has rightly been restored by the High Court Division</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shinepukur Holdings Ltd. and ors. Vs.Security and Exchange Commission and another, 18BLD (AD) 189</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Law prescribes a procedure for scrutiny of evidence that may be adduced in a trial. It is fundamental to the rule of law that the procedural law as to holding of a trial must not be interfered with.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Taslima Nasreen Vs. Mohammad Nurul Alam, Officer-in-Charge and another, 18BLD(AD) 249</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Provision of section 342 of the Code being a mandatory provision of procedural law, the departure from the fundamental principles of the said section causes grave prejudice to the accused-appellant. Since the accused- appellant was not given any opportunity to explain the circumstances, the order of conviction appears to have been vitiated and it is liable to be set aside.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Islam alias Nur Islam Vs. The State, I8BLD (HCD) 695</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A conviction under section 302 of the Penal Code and a conviction under section 302/149 of the Penal Code are different kinds of conviction as section 302 of the Penal Code involves direct and personal liability of the accused whereas section 302/149 of the Penal Code involves a vicarious liability. The trial Court convicted the accused-appellant under section 302/149 of the Penal Code and as such the High Court Division was wrong in affirming the order of conviction and sentence under section 302 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Altaf Hossain Vs. The State, 18BLD (AD) 231</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The allegations made in the petition of complaint it clearly shows that the petitioner had initial intention to deceive the complainant and thereby misappropriated the money. So, it cannot be said that it is a case of civil nature. The petition of complaint undoubtedly discloses criminal offence against the accused-petitioner. The Appellate Division held that the High Court Division rightly refused the prayer for quashing the proceeding. Penal Code, 1860 (XLV of 1860) Section — 406 and 420</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abu Baker Siddique Vs. The State &amp; anr., 18BLD (AD) 289</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the findings by the trial Court that the petitioner are not children within the meaning of section 2 of sub-section (f) of the Children Act, the High Court Division rejected the defence plea that the petitioner being children can not be tried by the ordinary criminal Court—Children Act, 1974 (XXXIX of 1974), Sections.2(f) and 66</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mohammad Hossain and others Vs. The State and another, 18BLD (HCD)407</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 47DLR(AD) 96—relied</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the absence of any proof showing that thge quantity of heroin as an incriminating substance exceeded 25 grams, an accused cannot be sentenced to death under Table 1(b) of section 19 of the Act. In the instant case, since the prosecution failed to prove that the quantity of heroin seized exceeded 25 grams, the accused could at best be sentenced under Table 1(a) of section 19 and not under Table 1(b) of section 19 of the Act. Sentence of death was therefore altered to 5 years rigorous imprisonment—Narcotic Control Act, 1990 (XX of 1990), Section—19(1)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Innocent N. Egbunine, 18BLD (HCD)250</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The line of demarcation between the culpable homicide and grievous hurt is rather thin. In the former case injury must be such as is likely to cause death whereas in the latter case it is likely to endanger life. Offence of culpable homicide pre-supposes an intention or knowledge of likelihood of causing death. In the absence of such intention or knowledge the offence committed may be grievous hurt notwithstanding death being caused—Penal Code, 1860 (XLV of 1860), Sections—302, 304 and 326</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Humayun Matubbar Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD) 492</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The accused-appellant Shamsul Huq had no premeditated intention to kill the victim Kastura Bibi and the intention to kill is lacking in the instant case. Although, he had no intention to kill the victim, he must be attributed the knowledge that such throwing of brick was likely to cause her death and the act done by him was both rash and indiscreet and as such the accused appellant Shamsul Huq is liable for commission of offence punishable under section 304 Part II of the Penal Code and he can be convicted thereunder— Penal Code, 1860;Section—304 Part II</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abdul Jabbar and another Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD) 109</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 5BLD(AD)198; 6BLD(AD)318; 10 BLD(AD)210; 5BLD129; 8BLD129; 48 DLR 446—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">A co-sharer in exclusive possession of a parcel of land is entitled to maintain his possession therein. Before effecting partition by metes and bounds co-sharers or co-owners have no right to disturb his possession, not to speak of cutting and taking away his crops. In such a case the bonafide claim of right in the case land is no defence against a charge under section 379 of the Penal Code for cutting and taking away crops grown by the co-sharer in possession—Penal Code; Section 379.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nasiruddin Shah and ors Vs. Nazrul Islam and ors, 18BLD (HCD)634</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To sustain an order of conviction for possessing contraband goods the prosecution is required to prove the exclusive possession or domain of the accused over the goods in question. The prosecution should also produced the seized goods for the scrutiny of the Court when the identity thereof is challenged by the defence—Special Powers act, 1974; Section—25B.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Taher alias Md. Taher Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)691</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since a duly constituted enquiry committee in its report opined that the accused persons contravened the provisions of section 17 of the Securities and Exchange Commission Ordinance, 1983 and the Magistrate on being satisfied about the same has taken cognizance against the accused persons they would get fair chance to defend themselves and as such there is no failure of justice in taking cognizance of the offence against them.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shainpukur Holding Ltd. Vs. Security Exchange Commission, 18BLD (HCD) 61</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27DLR111; 30DLR(SC)58; 29DLR 122; 32DLR(AD)35; (1940) A.C 1014 (at P.1022)—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a case where two persons have been murdered at dead of night it is but natural to inform the police first about the occurrence and as such non-mentioning of any name in the FIR rings a truth in the FIR. Section—302</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shahjahan Sardar and others Vs. The State 13BLD (AD) 58</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Nature of injury caused by ‘Teta’</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If the hook of a ‘Teta enters the body upto its full length and then is brought out, the injury caused may be lacerated but if the hook does not penetrate, there will be hardly any difference between an wound caused by a shorki and that caused by a teta.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State vs. Nuru Mira, 13BLD (AD) 202</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Glaring omissions and material contradictions in the statements of witnesses, particularly of the eye-witnesses, render their evidence unreliable.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Islam and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD) 69</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A conviction under section 19(f) the Arms Act cannot be allowed to stand unless the prosecution has adduced sufficient evidence to testify that the accused person has in his ‘possession’ or under his ‘control’ arms and ammunition in contravention of sections 4, 14, 15 and 19 of the Arms Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sukkur Ali Kha Vs The State, 19BLD (HCD) 585</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">The alleged transaction in between the complainant and the appellant is clearly and admittedly a business transaction. The appellant had already paid a part of the money under the contract to the complainant. The failure on the part of the appellant to pay the complainant the balance amount under the bill does not warrant any criminal proceeding as the obligation under the contract is of civil nature. The High Court Division were not justified in refusing to quash the proceeding in question although the transaction in question between the parties is clearly of a civil nature. Cr.P.C;S. 561A, Penal Code, 1860 (XLV of 1860), S. 420</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dewan Obaidur Rahman Vs. The State and ano., 19BLD (AD) 128</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 45 DLR(AD)27—relied upon</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The trial itself was without jurisdiction and in that view of the matter the delay in filing the appeal was not material. There is no use sending the appeal back to the High Court Division for consideration on merit as the trial was without jurisdiction and as such the order of conviction and sentence is liable to be set aside.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nazrul Islam Chowdhury Vs. The State, 19BLD (AD)18</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">According to section 2(f) of the Children Act a child means a person under the age of 16 years. In the instant case the appellant was a regular student of class IX at the time of commission of the offence and he is aged about 14 years when the court examined him under section 342 of the Code of Criminal Procedure and as such the trial is without jurisdiction.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Shamim Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 542</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Provision of the Anti-Corruption Act can be resorted to for enquire into or for investigation of pecuniary resources or property of any person either he is a public servant or any other else or in other words ‘an ordinary citizen’ and as such the provisions of the Act is not applicable in the case of petitioner, who is ‘an ordinary citizen’ is not correct.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.K.M. Muhituddin Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 455</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A Court while trying an alleged offender should strictly consider the evidence on record without any prenotion because some time, circumstances lie in more dangerous fashion and fortutious concoursse of event may sometime give the air of truth.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mion Samaddar @ Mihir Samadder Vs The State, 19BLD (HCD) 568</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 537 of the Code of Criminal Procedure may now be taken to cover an error, omission or irregularity in the widest sence of those terms provided there has been no failure of justice and that the mere fact that imperative statutory rule of procedure has been broken is not enough to vitiate the trial or proceeding provided there is no failure of justice—Cr.P.C. S. 537</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Ali Asgar Vs. Md Esrail and others, 19BLD (HCD) 517</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37DLR223; 31C.W.N. 27—relied</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Law does not require particular number of witnesses to prove a case and conviction may be well founded even on the testimony of a solitary witness provided his credibility is not shaken by any adverse circumstances appearing on the record against him and the Court, at the same time, is convinced that he is a truthful witness. As a general rule, a Court can act on the testimony of a single witness though uncorroborated and one credible witness outweighs testimony of a number of other witnesses of indifferent character. Evidence on a point is to be judged not by the number of witnesses produced but its inherent truth—Evidence Act; Section— 134</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Al Amin Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 307</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Corroborative evidence is not an imperative component of Judicial credence in every case of rape. Corroboration as a condition for judicial reliance on the testimony of a victim of sex crime is not a requirement of law but merely a guidance of prudence under a given circumstances. The rule is not that corroboration is essential before there can be a conviction. The testimony of the victim of sexual assault is vital and unless there are compelling reasons which necessity looking for corroboration of her statement, the Court should find no difficulty in acting on the testimony of a victim of sex crime alone to convict an accused where her testimony inspires confidence and is found to be reliable.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Al Amin Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 307</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When growing of the case crops by the complainant and the cutting and taking away of the same dishonestly by the accused are proved, the accused is guilty of theft under section 379 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the fact that theft of the case crops by the cutting and taking away of the same and damaging some crops by the accused in the process necessarily involves their entry into the case land and the accused are punished for the offences of theft and mischief, a separate conviction under section 447 of the Penal Code is wholly unwarranted.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Motaleb Sardar and others Vs. The State and another, 19BLD (HCD) 407</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The exercise of power under section 144 of the Code is discretionary while under section 145 Code of Criminal Procedure it is mandatory as the words “Magistrate may” and “he shall” have been used respectively in section 144 and 145 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. Thereafter, whether action has previously been taken under section 144 or not, the Magistrate must act under section 145 if condition as to its application exists and must take action either in suppression or in continuation or in the absence of any order passed under section 144 of the Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Ali Asgar Vs. Md Esrail and others, 19BLD (HCD) 517</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 5DLR162D—Not applicable</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the civil court is already seized with the question of regulating possession of the land between the same parties the criminal court has no jurisdiction to draw any proceeding under section 145 Cr.P.C. and the Magistrates jurisdiction is completely ousted.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Majid Mondal Vs. The State and another, I9BLD (HCD) 534</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 51 DLR (1 999)(AD) 14—relied upon</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Although the stamp and other papers were signed by the complainant in Jeddah but as by using the same in Bangladesh the offence alleged to have been committed in Bangladesh is the consequence of such procurement of signature in Jeddah and as such it comes within the purview of illustration (c) of section 179 of the Code of Criminal Procedure and accordingly the Court before which the instant case is pending has got the jurisdiction to try the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Sattar Vs. The State and another, 19BLD (HCD) 217</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An offence under a particular section if not proved but some other offence is made out by the prosecution, the accused persons can be very well convicted and sentenced for the other offences proved before the Court through legal evidence and the same is permissible in view of the provisions of sections 236 and 237 of the Code.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Al Amin Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD)307</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is settled law that exculpatory statement uncorroborated by any other evidence, cannot be the basis of conviction. Since attention was not drown while the appellant examined under section 342 of the Code either to his confessional statement or to the statement of Dulal, the appellant is obviously prejudiced and as such the trial have been vitiated. Such defect is not curable under section 537 of the Code.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abu Jamal and others Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 411</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 339C Cr.P.C. does not make any provision for exclusion of the days of adjournments secured by the defence or for non-production of the accused in the Court on the ground of their illness. Days of adjournments obtained by the defence or adjournments granted for non-production of the accused for their illness or for any such similar reason cannot therefore be excluded from ‘total working days’.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The trial court having failed to conclude the trial within 270 working days, the accused acquire a right to an order of release when further proceedings of the case should be deemed to have stopped.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Motiar Rahman and others Vs. The State and another 16BLD(HCD)9</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 45 DLR 610—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 339C (4) of the Code makes it incumbent upon Special Tribunal to allow bail to the accused if he fails to complete the trial within the stipulated period envisaged by this Section unless for special reasons it directs otherwise.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Rafiq Hassan alias Biplob Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)198</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To base a conviction on the uncorroborated testimony of a lone witness, it is necessary that his evidence must be of unimpeachable character and it must be free from any doubt</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Siddique Ali and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)138</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When confession is the only evidence of involvement of the accused in the alleged offence and his attention is not drawn to it while examining him under Section 342 Cr.P.C for affording him opportunities to offer his explanation thereon, it must be held that the requirement of law was not fulfilled. The accused has been seriously prejudiced.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Kashem and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)120</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 10 DLR 61—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Character and conduct of a witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A witness who frequently changes his political colours to suit exigencies and is an accused in 8 murder cases and other criminal cases involving grave offences must be received with reservations in assessing the evidentiary value of his statements.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a criminal case the credibility, the character and the conduct of a witness are relevant and these should be considered before accepting his evidence as true.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Hemayetuddin alias Auranga Vs. The State 16BLD(HCD)558</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Material omissions, discrepancies and contradictions in the statements of the witnesses, particularly of the eye-witnesses, render their evidence unreliable.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Islam and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD)69</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Belated disclosure of material facts</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When an eye witness to the occurrence does not disclose the names of the assailants he allegedly recognised, the means of recognition and other material facts to the witnesses who assembled at the scene immediately after the occurrence was over, and there is no cogent reason for such non-disclosure, belated disclosure of these material facts renders his evidence doubtful.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Asadur Rahman alias Asad and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 290</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Recovery of ‘heroin’</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the prosecution case is that grey coloured heroin was seized from the accused and the chemical expert deposed that brown coloured heroin was sent to him for examination, it cannot be held that the alleged incriminating article recovered from the accused was ‘heroin’ at all.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Zulfiqure Ali alias Kazal Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 570</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If a witness in his statement recorded by a Magistrate under section 164 Cr.P.C. does not state some vital facts concerning the overt/acts of the accused persons, his evidence before the trial court on those facts becomes doubtful. The requirement of law is that the attention of the witness will have to be drawn to the relevant facts.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jalal and 3 others Vs. The State, 15 BLD (HCD) 588 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When assault was preceded by an altercation and the condemned prisoner struck only one blow to the victim and restrained himself from repeating blows and there was no premeditation to assault the victim and the occurrence took place on the heat of passion, the offence is not murder but culpable homicide not amounting to murder punishable under section 304 Part I of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Khalilur Rahman, 15BLD (HCD) 315</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Before recording a conviction under section 302 of the Penal Code, the court must be satisfied beyond reasonable doubts about the guilt of the accused persons on careful scrutiny of the evidence on record. A conviction even on high probabilities is not contemplated in law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Jiaur Rahman Vs The State, 15BLD (HCD) 459</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a case of dacoity at dead hours of the night the means of recognition is of vital importance. The court is to scrutinise the evidence on recognition with great care and caution—Penal Code, 1860, S. 395</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kuti alias Bellal and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 9</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In a case of dacoity committed in the darkness of night the question of recognition is of vital importance. When the only eye witness fails to state in his evidence the means of recognition of the accused, the order of conviction can not stand—Penal Code, 1860, S. 395</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Subal Chandra Das Vs. The State, 15 BLD(HCD) 121</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere retention of money by the accused for some time without its actual use or mere delay in its disbursement, if properly explained, does not constitute an offence under Section 409 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.K.M. Hafizuddin Vs. The State, 15 BLD(HCD) 234</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 66 of the Children Act it is for the Court to consider ,whether a person charged with an offence and brought before it for trial appears to be child or not and record a finding thereon after taking such evidence as may be forth coming at the hearing of the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Munem Chowdhury alias Momen Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD) 184</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The whole purpose of unamended section 339C Cr.P.C. was to whip up the prosecution and activise the trial court so as not to delay the trial of a case unnecessarily. It was not intended to give the accused a clean bill of acquittal, even if he was accused of a heinous crime.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under Act 42 of 1992 there is no scope of stopping of a proceeding or of a corresponding revival thereof. The new Act has effected a change in the procedural law but it has not affected any vested right of the accused and the prosecution. By necessary intendment the new Act will be retrospective in operation in so far as sub-section (4) of section 339C of the new Act is concerned.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of repeal of sub-section (4) of section 339C Cr.P.C. followed by reenactment of the said sub-section, the new procedural law will apply to all pending cases although instituted before the amendment.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Wadud Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD) 189</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> Md. Aslam Vs. The State, 19 DLR (SC) 242; Babul and others Vs. The State, 15 BLD (1995XHCD)88—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 6(1) of the Children Act provides that no child shall be charged with, or tried for, any offence together with an adult. A joint trial of a child together with an adult is clearly prohibited by law—Children Act, 1974. S. 6(1)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kawsarun Nessa and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 21</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Statement recorded under section 161 Cr.P.C. is no evidence at all and the prosecution can derive no benefit out of it. It can be utilised by the defence under section 162 Cr.P.C. to contradict the witness in the manner provided by section 145 of the Evidence Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Subhan Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD) 281</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong>PLD1957(Ind)197; 42DLR(AD)186; 17 DLR (SC)40, 35 DLR (AD) 303; 22 DLR 620—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"> </p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Adjournments secured by the accused themselves, the time lost for inability of the Court to function for some time due to circumstances beyond his control and the time spent due to absence of a co-accused are to be excluded in computing the period of 270 working days. Cr.P.C. S. 339C</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Motaleb Shaikh and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD)130</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a trial is held in contravention of the prescribed procedure laid down in the Code, such a trial cannot be held to be in accordance with law and the consequences provided in law must follow.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The High Court Division rightly found that the proceedings of the trial court after the expiry of the prescribed period of 120 days and the order of conviction and sentence pased by the learned Magistrate are illegal. Cr.P.C. S. 339C(4)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Narayan Chandra Goswami Vs. Dinendra Nath Chakraborty and others, 15BLD (AD)118</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Right of private defence of person may extend even to causing death when there is a reasonable apprehension that the intended assault would cause death or grievous hurt but this right is not available against an unarmed man. Penal Code, 1860, Ss. 99 and 100</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dalim and another Vs The State, 15BLD (HCD) 133 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The deposition of a witness before the Committing Court may be treated as substantive evidence when it is transferred to the Sessions record either under section 288 Cr.P.C. or under the provisions of section 33 of the Evidence Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jalal and 3 others Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD) 588</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the appellants were in custody in connection with another criminal case and inspite of the Court’s order for their production they were not produced before the Court for no fault of their own, their trial in absentia is evidently wrong—Cr.P.C; S. 339B</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Muslim and others Vs. The State, 15 BLD(HCD) 298</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 339C(3) Cr.P.C provides that if the trial of a case cannot be concluded within 270 working days, further proceedings in respect of the trial stand stopped and the accused persons released.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 339C ‘(4), which provides for stopping further proceedings in respect of the trial and releasing the accused persons is a mandatory provision of law. The subsequent amendment of law cannot take away the accrued right of the accused persons.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Babul and others Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD) 88</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since section 29 of the Special Powers Act invests the Special Tribunal with all the powers of a Court of Sessions exercising original jurisdiction under the Code of Criminal Procedure, the Special Tribunal is competent under section 517 of the Code to pass necessary orders for disposal of a seized property after the disposal of the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>S.A. Hasnat Khan and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 440</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere possession of counterfeit currency notes is by itself no offence under section 25A of the Special Powers Act. In order to succeed, the prosecution must prove that the accused used the counterfeit currency notes as genuine ones knowing or having reason to believe them to be counterfeit.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abdus Salam alias Abdus Salarn and another Vs. The State,15BLD(HCD) 477</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the accused is not apprehended while smuggling goods from India and he is not arrested with the smuggled goods in the border areas, he cannot be held liable for smuggling punishable under section 25B(l) of the Special Powers Act. If however he holds smuggled goods for sale and these are actually recovered from his possession well inside Bangladesh territory, he is liable for possession of smuggled goods punishable under section 25B(2) of the Act which provides a maximum sentence of 7 years imprisonment.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gopal Chandra Chakraborty Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 224</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the fact that 310 working days have passed after deduction of the holidays, Fridays and the days covered by the absence of the Presiding Judge from the station on account of leave without being relieved by a Judge with power to try the case, it is clear that on the expiry of 270 working days, the proceedings of the case stand stopped and a right of release accrues to the accused persons under sub-section (4) of section 339C of the Code of Criminal Procedure.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Md. Azizul Haque and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 369</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Working days</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The term ‘working days’ used in section 339C means ‘days’ on which a Court actually functions or works for the disposal of a particular case. Only such working days in computing the specified period for conclusion of the trial are to be counted.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The days on which the Judge was on leave, adjournments obtained by the accused and the period of stay of further proceedings granted by the Superior Court are to be deducted in computing ‘working days’.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Zakir Hossain Khan and others Vs.
