The State, 15BLD(HCD) 599</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 8 BLD (1988) (AD)122; 47DLR (HCD)24; 15 BLD(AD)130—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A criminal proceeding cannot be quashed under section 561A Cr.P.C. on the basis of defence materials which are still no part of the record of the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Most. Rahela Khatun Vs. Md. Abul Hassan and others, 16BLD(AD)266</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 265 Cr.P.C provides that a Public Prosecutor or an Assistant Public Prosecutor will conduct the prosecution of a case before a Court of Session. No police officer is thus competent to file any application directly before the Sessions Judge seeking permission for further investigation when the case is pending for trial before the Sessions Court.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Rahamatullh Vs. The State and another,16BLD(AD)88</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The trial Court should draw the attention of the accused to the main incriminating evidence against him, particularly the confessional statement, while examining him under section 342 Cr.P.C. But mere omission to specifically draw the attention of the accused to such evidence does not always prejudice him.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the accused did not retract his confession either by application from jail or by directly filing such application in the Court at any time, no grievance on the unsubstantiated allegations of inducement and torture can be entertained at the last stage of the trial. Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898) Section—342</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mezanur Rahman and others Vs. The State,16BLD(AD)293</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Delivery of Judgment</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A judgment is a final result of a case but it cannot be considered as an integral part of the trial of a criminal case. Staying delivery of judgment of a case at the conclusion of the trial in the name of the disposal of the counter case for unlimited period is highly deprecated—Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898) S.366</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mastansir Billah Vs. Bepari Abdur Rob and others, 16BLD(AD)111</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When in the course of tussels, hot altercations and grappling between the parties the accused dealt only one knife blow to the victim under unexplained circumstances, intention to kill being absent, the offence comes within the mischief of culpable homicide not amounting to murder—Penal Code, 1860 (XLV of 1860) Section -304</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdur Rashid Vs. The State, 16BLD (HCD)183</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a criminal trial determination of fact is the main task before the Court and such determination is dependent upon consideration of answers given by prosecution witnesses in their cross-examination.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Normally in examination-in-chief the prosecution witness speaks which are favourable to the party examining him without disclosing the necessary facts which are in favour of the other side. But in the instant case the cross-examination that was made by the defence only established the presence of the accused persons and their participation in the occurrence. It is really unfortunate that the learned Judges of the High Court Division discarded the evidence of the eye-witnesses and other corroborative evidence on record without any discussion and consideration of any of the testimony of the witnesses. It is very unfortunate that in a criminal appeal such method has been adopted in a very perfunctory manner by the learned Judges of the High Court Division.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mahmudul Islam @ Ratan Vs The State, 20 BLD(AD)249</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From the evidence of PW4 and the inquest report it appears that Biswajit did riot commit suicide rather somebody killed him and then tried to hang the dead body in the cowshed in a sitting position.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gulzar Biswas and others Vs The State, 20 BLD(HCD)550</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From the judgment of the trial Court it is found that the learned Sessions Judge has not properly assessed the evidence of the case and failed to consider the gross discrepancies, contradictions and omissions as well as admissions of the PWs on vital points and as such it is not safe to maintain the, order of conviction and sentence on the evidence on record of the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shaheb Ali Munshi and another Vs The State, 20 BLD(HCD) 148</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em> <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CULPABLE HOMICIDE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> Culpable homicide</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere killing of a person or mere causing person’s death is not murder, culpable homicide or even any criminal offence but it so when caused with certain guilty knowledge. The essential difference between mere “culpable homicide” and “culpable homicide amounting to murder” is the degree of probability of causing death. When death is probable, it is culpable homicide but when death is most probable, it is murder. Penal Code, Ss.299&amp;300</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Ashraf Ali and others, 14BLD(AD)127</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CUSTODY OF A MINOR GIRL</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Custody of a minor girl</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is patently unwarranted in law to dispose of Miscellaneous Case relating to custody of a minor girl while disposing of appeal out of turn without giving any opportunity, to the other side.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Atul Chandra Biswas and another Vs. The Secretary, Ministry of Home Affairs, Government of the people’s Republic of Bangladesh 13BLD (AD)27</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Custody of minor girl</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The real welfare of a minor girl lies with her custody being given to her father, who is her best well-wisher. The High Court Division after having found the victim girl to be a minor was not right in refusing to give her to the lawful custody of her father. The refusal of the minor to go with her father is of no legal consequence—Cr.P.C; Section—491</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sree Mongal Chandra Nandi Vs. Bangladesh and others, 17BLD (AD) 33</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Custody of minor girl</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">For the purpose of custody of the victim girl as prayed for in a pending criminal proceeding, the court has to proceed on the basis that the female is a minor under sixteen years of age as laid down in Section 361 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Wahed Ali Dewan Vs. The State and another, 14BLD(AD)32</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Custody of victim girl</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is entirely unacceptable that a young girl who is an innocent victim of the alleged offence should remain in an wholesome atmosphere of a Jail for an indefinite period. The young girl cannot be allowed to walk away from the prison house of her own, because she had no independent place to stay. The welfare of the girl, should be deciding factor in such a situation. The appellant produced a certificate from the school where the girl was reading which corroborates the statements of the appellant that his daughter was a minor at the relevant time. The radiologist’s opinion also supports the appellants case. From the above circumstances and having considered all aspects of the matter it will be fit and proper and in the best interest of the girl if she is released from custody and given to the care of her father—Cr.P.C.S.491</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bashu Deb Chatterjee Vs Umme Salma and another, 19 BLD (AD) 137.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1035	<h2 class="blog_entry-title"> CRUELTY TO WOMEN (DETERRENT PUNISHMENT) ORDINANCE, 1983</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  14 BLD (HCD) 286, 14BLD(HCD)303, 14BLD (HCD)335,  17BLD (HCD)291.</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  CRUELTY TO WOMEN (DETERRENT PUNISHMENT)</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CRUELTY TO WOMEN (DETERRENT PUNISHMENT) ORDINANCE, 1983</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>(LX OF 1983)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—5</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The possession of a woman obtained through import or export or sale or hire or disposal in any manner with the intent that such woman might be used or employed for the purpose of prostitution or for any immoral purposes, constitutes the offence of trafficking in woman. The two explanations appended to section 5 of the Ordinance make it abundantly clear that the possession of such a woman until the contrary is proved shall be presumed that such possession was for the purpose of prostitution.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In the absence of any evidence to show that the possession of the victim was obtained by the accused persons for the purpose of prostitution or for any immoral purpose, section 5 of the Ordinance is not attracted.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Motaleb and others Vs. The State, 14 BLD (HCD) 286.</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—-6</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To attract of the offence under section 6 of the Ordinance, there must be causing of death or grievous hurt or attempt to cause death or grievous hurt to a woman for dowry. Causing of simple hurt or attempt to cause simple hurt does not bring the offence within the mischief of this section.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Atikur Rahman Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)303</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—6</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">‘Dowry’ under section 6 of the Ordinance not only includes money etc. agreed to be paid before or at the time of the marriage but also money etc. demanded afresh after the marriage for continuing the marriage already solemnized. So, any valuable property, which includes money, demanded by the husband or his relations from the wife or her relations afresh after the marriage is dowry within the meaning of section 6 of the Ordinance.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Ajit Kumar Pramanik and others Vs. Bokul Rani Pramanik and others, 14BLD (HCD)335</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 40 DLR 360—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—9</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From the FIR it is clear that a prima facie case of abetment punishable under section 9 of the Ordinance has been made out. The Special Tribunal on consideration of the available materials on record and on hearing the parties framed charge of abetment against the appellant. The Special Tribunal has not committed any illegality in framing the charge.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Anowara Begum Vs Most. Sultana Jeshmin Khan (Shoapa) and State, 17BLD (HCD)291.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1036	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">ACTING PRESIDENT’S ORDER NO. 70 OF 1990</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  14 BLD (HCD) 337</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  ACTING PRESIDENT’S ORDER </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p> </p><p align="center" style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: center;"><strong> </strong><strong><br clear="all" style="page-break-before: always;"/></strong></p><p align="center" style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: center;"><strong>ACTING PRESIDENT’S ORDER NO. 70 OF 1990</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify;"><strong>Clemency to convicts</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;">By Acting President’s Order No. 70 of 1990- Prison (2) dated 14.1.1991 (Ministry of Home Affairs, Government of Bangladesh), the sentence of imprisonment passed against any person prior to 6.12.1990 has been reduced to half.</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;">In view of the Acting President’s Order No. 70 of 1990-Prison (2) dated 14.1.1991 the sentence of imprisonment passed against the accused on 13.5.1990. Acting President’s order dated 14.1.1991 had been reduced to half the relevant date fixed by Acting President’s said order for calculating the reduction of sentence under the order of clemency.</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify;"><strong><em> Dilbar Khan and others Vs. The State, 14 BLD (HCD) 337.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1037	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">ANTI-CORRUPTION ACT, 1957</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  19BLD (AD)97</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  ANTI-CORRUPTION </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: center;"><strong>ANTI-CORRUPTION ACT, 1957</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in;"><strong>Section—3</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in;"><strong>Anti-Corruption Manual</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in;"><strong>Paragraph—59</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in;"><strong>Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898)</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in;"><strong>Section—561A</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;">In view of the provisions of section 3 of the Anti-Corruption Act, 1957 and paragraph 59 of the Anti-Corruption Manual the investigation held by an Assistant Inspector of the Bureau of the Anti-Corruption was not illegal and without jurisdiction.</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;">The investigation by an Assistant Inspector does not per se become illegal and without jurisdiction. A proceeding cannot also be quashed merely because there is irregularity, if any, in the investigation.</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify;"><strong><em> Md. Abul Hossain Vs The State, 19BLD (AD)97.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1038	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">ANTI-TERRORISM ACT, 1992</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  14BLD(HCD)520, 15 BLD(HCD)93, 14 BLD(HCD)328.</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  ANTI-TERRORISM </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p> </p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: center;"><strong>ANTI-TERRORISM ACT, 1992</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify;"><strong><br/>Section—2 (Ka) to 2 (Chha)</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;">From a reading of the provisions of the Anti-Terrorism Act it is seen that the Anti— Terrorism Tribunal has been invested with the power to try all offences under the Penal Code and other laws along with the offences as defined under section 2 (Ka) to 2 (Chha) of the Act. Since the legislature has purposely, consciously and clearly made this provision for trial of different offences by the Anti-Terrorism Tribunal, no different meaning can be attributed to it while dealing with the question of quashing a proceeding in exercise of the inherent jurisdiction of the High Court Division</p><p>The language of the statute is depository and reservoir of the legislative intent. In order to ascertain and discover the intention the of the legislature, statute must be considered as whole to grasp its true meaning. Where the statute is clear it needs no interpretation. If it is ambiguous of the legislature is to be looked into. The Court is to interpret on the basis of the language and intent and not to enter deep into the matter. The plain meaning of the statute indicates that the offences defined under section 2(ka) to 2(Chha) is to be tried and can be tried alongwith the offences under the Penal Code and the general law of the land. The Tribunal at the time of framing charge shall consider whether the offences under the Anti-Terrorism Act and under the general law of the country is separable or not.<strong><em><br/><br/>Md. Shahidullah and others Vs. The Stateg 14BLD(HCD)520</em></strong><strong><br/>Ref</strong>: A.I.R 1988(SC) 992; 1988 S.C.C. 271; PLD 1990 (Karachi) 448—Cited</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify;"><strong><br/>Section—4</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;">Mere attempt to realise ransom does not constitute any offence under section 4 of the Anti-Terrorism Act. The provision of this section would be attracted only when the attempt is successful and the offence is complete. An attempt to realise money in the form of donation or otherwise has not been made a punishable offence under this special law.</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;"><strong><em>Shadesh Chandra Saha Vs. The State, 15 BLD(HCD)93</em></strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1951 (Assam) 17; A.I.R. 1937 (Cal) 710; A.I.R. 1950(Mad) 599; 33 DLR 379— Cited</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—9(2)</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-indent: .5in;">Simultaneous Trial—Section 9(2) of the Act provides for simultaneous trial of two cases in which offences are so inter-connected that such trials become necessary in the interest of justice.</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;">In the absence of any nexus between the two cases in respect of time, date and place of the occurrences, it cannot be held that the two cases arose out of the self-same occurrence.</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;"><strong><em> Hafiz alias Hafizul Islam and another Vs. The State, 14 BLD(HCD)328.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1039	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">ANTIQUITIES ACT, 1968</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  19BLD(HCD)476</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  ANTIQUITIES</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: center;"><strong><br/>ANTIQUITIES ACT, 1968<br/>(XIV OF 1968)</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify;"><br/><strong>Section—28</strong></p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;">Only a Sessions Court can take cognizance in respect of the offence regarding antiquities but in the instant case, the cognizance was purportedly taken by the Special Tribunal. Inview of section 28 of the Antiquities Act, such taking of cognizance by the special Tribunal is illegal order without jurisdiction.</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-align: justify; text-indent: .5in;">Taking cognizance of the offence by the Special Tribunal under the Special Powers Act is absolutely without jurisdiction and the instant proceeding pending against the accused petitioner is an abuse of the process of the Court and is liable to be quashed.</p><p style="margin-right: -.2in; text-indent: .5in;"><strong><em>Md. Khalilur Rahman alias Ismail Vs The State, 19BLD(HCD)476.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1040	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">BENGAL JAIL CODE,1894</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  20BLD(HCD)204</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  BENGAL JAIL </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BENGAL JAIL CODE,1894</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>(IX OF 1894)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Rules—617, 1051—1058</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Immediately after passing of the judgment awarding the sentence of death, the prisoner shall be confined in a cell apart from all other prisoners and shall be dept under constant guarding, which is required for the purpose of a security of life and also for the safety of the prisoner. But we different picture have been found in the case of an under trial prisoner who is allotted Division I as provided under Rule 617 of the Jail Code. Other facilities to be awarded to the prisoner if he is granted a Division I which are prescribed in Rules 1051-58, cannot be allowed to a condemned prisoner.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The High Court Division cannot pass an order creating anomaly in the subject and procedural law which is being followed according to the Jail Code and the Rules therein iii’ accordance with the provision of Prisoners Act, 1894 by the jail authority. This Court also cannot pass any order which cannot be executed and obeyed.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Major (Retd) Bazlul Huda Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)204.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1041	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">BENGAL POLICE REGULATION, 1943</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  15BLD (HCD) 169</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  BENGAL POLICE </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BENGAL POLICE REGULATION, 1943</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>T.I. Parade</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A belated T.I. Parade provides opportunities to the witnesses to see the accused persons before they are put in the T.I. Parade and as such any unusual delay in holding the T.I.Parade casts doubt regarding the genuineness of the identification of the accused persons.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Md. Musa alias Musaiya alias Shafir Bap, 15BLD (HCD) 169</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>[The above case has been affirmed by the Appellate Division, reported in 21BLD(AD)125]</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1042	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [A]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  14BLD (AD)178, 14BLD (AD)224, 17BLD (HCD) 291, 16BLD(HCD)526, 14BLD (HCD)99,14BLD(HCD)301, 20 BLD(HCD)412, 14BLD(HCD)471, 13BLD (HCD)401,13 BLD (HCD) 277,  13BLD (AD)127, 13BLD (AD)202,15BLD(HCD) 377,15BLD(AD) 133,16BLD (HCD)533,18BLD (HCD) 14, 20 BLD (H</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [A]</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ABETMENT</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Abetment</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is an offence under the Penal Code and a person may be charged for abetting an offence punishable under a special law although ‘abetment may not have been mentioned as an offence under the Special Act— Penal Code. S.109</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>H.M. Ershad Vs. The State, 14BLD (AD)178</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 44DLR(AD)2 15 ;33DLR379; 17DLR (SC)261—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Abetment</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since the condemned prisoner did not take any part in the act of killing his wife Hazera, although he was a silent spectator to the gruesome murder of his wife by others, who were acquitted for want of legal evidence, he cannot be convicted under section 302 of the Penal Code but he should be found guilty for abetment under sections 302/109 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Awal Vs. The State, 14BLD (AD)224</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Abetment</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the fact that the FIR disclosed a prima facie case of abetment punishable under section 9 of the Ordinance, the Special Tribunal committed no illegality in framing charge against the appellant. Cruelty to Women (Deterrent Punishment) Ordinance, 1983; S. 9</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Anowara Begum Vs. Most. Sultana Jeshmin Khan (Shoapa) and State, 17BLD (HCD) 291</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Abetment</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Suicide in consequence of cruelties</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a married woman is dragged to commit suicide in consequence of cruelties perpetrated to her by the husband and other relations, the consequent unfortunate death must be condemned and the abettor or abettors need be punished for protecting the society against such devillish acts.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Unfortunately there is no provision in our laws to punish a person or persons whose cruel behaviour dragged a married woman to commit suicide. Section 306 of the Penal Code provides punishment to a person only in a case where suicide is abetted or intended.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Yahiya alias Thandu and others, 16BLD(HCD)526</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ABSCONDING</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absconding</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Absconding of the accused during the trial may be a circumstance for giving rise to an adverse presumption against him but it can never form the basis of his conviction. The Court acts illegally in asking the accused to disprove the charge brought against him— Evidence Act, 1872, S. 8.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Salam Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)99</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absconding</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since there is no evidence to connect the accused with the alleged offence, the learned Special Judge was wrong in convicting him simply on the ground that the accused was absconding and he did not appear to refute the charges—Evidence Act 1872, S. 8.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Jamsed Ahmed Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)301</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absconding</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 7 of Act relates to the procedure of punishment of an absconder and does not in any way empower the detaining authority to detain a person by an order made more than thirty days prior to the service of the order upon that person.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mosharaf Hossain Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh, 20 BLD(HCD)412</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absconding</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absconding and absconder and failure to appear</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Failure to appear as contemplated by section 339 B (2) Cr.P.C. means default or neglect on the part of the accused to appear in court. So, when an accused is prevented by any genuine cause from appearing in the Court, that by itself cannot constitute neglect or default and for that matter failure to appear in the Court.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mostafizur Rahman Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)471</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1973 Cr.L.J. 137; 34 D.L.R.(AD) 222—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absconding</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Wife Killing—Beating wife by husband</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In our country, beating of the wife by the husband is a common day affair.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Khaleque Vs. The State, 13BLD (HCD)401</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absconding</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absconding of the accused after the occurrence.</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Mere absconsion of the accused after the occurrence, whether is sufficient to hold him guilty of the offence?</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In our country, specially in the villages, beating the wife by the husband on flimsy pretext is a common day affair. Normally such beating is to chastise the wife for unbecoming behavior. Such beating becomes excessive—when the wife provocates the husband.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">It is true, mere absconsion of the accused after the occurrence is not sufficient to hold him guilty of the offence. But conduct of the accused appellant is relevant under section 8 of the Evidence Act. If absconding of the accused is with his guilty mind it may be sufficient to hold him guilty.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Khaleque Vs. The State, 13BLD (HCD)401</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 3DLR(AD) 87—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absconding</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Mere absconding is not a proof of guilt against accused in the absence of legal proof.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Malai Miah Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 277</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ACQUITTAL</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Acquittal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Interference against acquittal— Whether unless it is possible to demonstrate with certainty that none of the grounds upon which the learned Judges purported to acquit is at all acceptable, the Appellate Division will be reluctant to interfere.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Unless it is possible to demonstrate with certainty that none of the grounds upon which the learned Judges of the High Court Division have purported to acquit the accused is at all supportable this court will be reluctant to interfere even though upon the evidence on the record and the facts and circumstances of the case this Court might well have to come to a different conclusion if it was trying the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abdul Hamid Vs. Ali Mollah and another 13BLD (AD)127</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 16DLR(SC) 94—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Acquittal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Ordinarily, an order of acquittal is not interfered with but where the order of acquittal is found to be shockingly unjust, the Appellate Division shall interfere for securing the ends of justice.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Nuru Mira 13BLD (AD)202</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Acquittal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Acquittal of an accused on the default of the prosecution under section 247 Cr.P.C. is not an acquittal within the meaning of section 403 Cr.P.C. and it cannot bar a fresh prosecution.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Godadhar Namadas alias Gadahar Mondal Vs. Most. Joytun Akhtar and others, 15BLD(HCD) 377</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1918) 19 Cr1. L.J. 497—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Acquittal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Interference with an order of acquittal is called for only when the evidence against the accused is unimpeachable and the findings of the court below are perverse.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nagendra Chandra Sarker Vs. Aftabuddin and others, 15BLD(AD) 133</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Acquittal</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Acquittal of non-appealing accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">After the Court finds that the order of conviction as a whole is not maintainable in law it should acquit even the non-appealing accused so that nobody is deprived of the fountain of justice.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Mesbahuddin, 16BLD (HCD)533</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Acquittal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Where the State does not file any appeal against an order of acquittal passed by a Magistrate in a police case the informant, who is vitally interested in the result of the case, is competent to file a revision before the Sessions Judge, who can look into the legality and propriety of the order of acquittal under section 439A Cr.P.C.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere non-issuance of any notice upon the State by the informant by itself is no bar in filing the revisional application against the order of acquittal, although the usual practice in such a case is to serve notice upon the state.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abu Taher and others Vs. Hasina Begum and another, 18BLD (HCD) 14</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Acquittal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Order of acquittal cannot be interfered by the appellate Court unless it is found that the said order is manifestly perverse. A finding cannot be said to be perverse if it is against weight of evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Ms. Shamima Arshad, 20 BLD (HCD) 315</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Acquittal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The language of section 247 of the Code of Criminal Procedure having clearly empowered the concerned Magistrate to acquit the accused for the failure of the complainant to appear in the case on the date fixed for the appearance of the accused, it cannot be said that only the order of acquittal passed upon holding full trial can create a bar under section 403 of the Code of Criminal Procedure from entertaining a second complaint on the self-same allegations. So long as the order of acquittal passed under section 247 Cr.P.C. remains in force the provision of section 403 Cr.P.C. shall stand on the way of entertaining a second complaint on the self-same allegations.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dewan Obaidur Rahman Vs. The State and another, 19BLD (AD) 128</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ADVOCATE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Advocate</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An Advocate has every right to represent his client and protect his interests by filing an appeal before the appellate forum, but he can not, while the appeal is pending, ascribing something, by publishing a news report in his capacity as a Court reporter, which is not in the judgment—Contempt of Court Act; S.2 and 3</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>M. Saleem Ullah Vs. The State. 13BLD(AD)3</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><br/>ALIBI</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><br/>Alibi</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The defence of alibi of the condemned prisoner was not established even then the responsibility of the prosecution does not diminish in establishing its case that it was the accused who was responsible for commission of offence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Azizur Rahman alias Habib, 20 BLD(HCD)467</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 43DLR(AD)92; 43 DLR243, 2BLC (AD) 126; 45DLr287; 1986BLD(AD)174— Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Alibi</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>The plea of alibi and murder of wife</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The plea of alibi having been disbelieved, a great burden lies on the deceased husband to explain as to how his wife died in his room where they were sleeping in the same bed. Existence of multiple injuries on the person of the victim makes this burden heavier.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case the defence plea of alibi having been discarded and the door of the room being kept open and in the absence of any acceptable explanation as to how the wife died in the room of the husband, the only irresistible and natural conclusion will be that it is the husband alone, in the circumstances of the case, who is guilty of committing murder of his wife. The chain of circumstances coupled with the medical evidence on record having been considered by the learned Judges of the High Court Division, we find that the circumstantial evidences admit of no other hypothesis than the guilt of the petitioner.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gourango Kumar Shaha Vs The State, 17BLD(AD)120</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Alibi—wife killing case</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Absence of plea of alibi</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the absence of any plea of alibi the evidence on record is found to be sufficient to hold that the appellant was at home in the fateful night with his deceased wife. Since the defence has failed to succeed in creating a reasonable belief by proving any circumstances that she could take her life by committing suicide the husband cannot absolve himself of the criminal liability for causing death to his deceased wife. The prosecution has been able to prove by evidence beyond reasonable doubt that the husband was with his deceased wife in the night of occurrence and the appellant caused the death of his wife by strangulation and as such the trial Court rightly convicted the appellant.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Salam Vs The State, 19BLD (HCD)98</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Alibi</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Plea of alibi in a wife killing case</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a wife killing case it is very difficult to bring witnesses against the accused person. The evidence of PW4 has got to be taken in the light of this situation. The evidence of PW4 that appellant Shiplu was in the field has been contradicted by the evidence of PW 5, another hostile witness, who saw appellant Shiplu and appellant Khaleda coming out of the house soon after the occurrence. In these circumstances, we do not accept the plea of alibi that appellant Shiplu was absent from the house at the time of the occurrence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shiplu and another Vs The State, 17BLD (HCD)243</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Plea of alibi</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The plea of the accused was that he was not at home during the night of the occurrence as he had been to Faridpur for purchasing clothes and stayed there on the fateful night. The plea of alibi falls to the ground as the defence failed to prove the said facts within the special knowledge of the accused—Evidecne Act Section. 108</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gourang Kumar Saha Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD)259</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>AMENDMENT OF CHARGE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Amendment of charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 227 of the Code provides that the Court may alter or add to any charge at any time before the pronouncement of judgment while section 231 of the Code provides that whenever a charge is altered or added to by the Court, the prosecution and the accused shall have the right to re-call or resummon and examine any witness already examined and also to call any further witness considered by the Court as material.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abu Bakkar alias Bakkar Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 217</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Amendment of charge</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Belated amendment of charge—Whether causes prejudice to the accused?</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the tribunal readily allowed the defence prayer to further cross-examine all the prosecution witnesses after addition of a new charge under section 8A of the Cruelty To Women (Deterrent Punishment) Ordinance, 1983, it cannot be said that the addition of the new charge has prejudiced the accused in any manner. Such a measure is always permissible in law for a proper trial.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abu Bakkar alias Bakkar Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 217</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 31 I.L.R (Bombay) 218; 5DLR(FC) 39; 6 C.W.N. 72; 5 DLR(FC) 24; A.I.R. 1970 (SC) 436 and 21 Cr1. L. J. 57—Cited</p><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ANTICIPATORY BAIL</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Anticipatory Bail</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Anticipatory bail loses its force after submission of charge-sheet and taking of cognizance by the Court concerned.Cr.P.C, S—498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Probir Kumar Chowdhury alias Tinku and ors Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 4</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Anticipatory Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Power of granting anticipatory bail is very sparingly used by this Court to save a citizen from unnecessary harassment and humiliation at the hand of police on flimsy ground or with ulterior motive or out of political designs. This power cannot be exercised in each and every case as a substitute in the exercise of such power by the Court below. A person cannot be enlarged on anticipatory bail how high so ever he may be unless conditions for granting such bail are satisfied. Cr.P.C, Section—498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Islam and ors, Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 173</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 18 BLD (1998) (HCD) 172—relied upon 5BLD( 198 5)(AD) 110 ;46DLR3 15 ;48DLR 18; 47DLR33; 43DLR312—Not applicable</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Anticipatory Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Power of granting anticipatory bail is very sparingly used by the High Court Division. It is used only when it is found necessary to save a citizen from unnecessary harassment and humiliation at the hand of the police on flimsy grounds or with ulterior motives or out of political designs. This power cannot be exercised in each and every case as a substitute to the exercise of such power by the Court below. <strong>[Per Kazi Ebadul Hoque, J]</strong> Cr.P.C, Section—498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Belayet Hossain Sharif Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)172</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 5DLR(FC)143; 15DLR(SC)2; 18 DLR(SC)393; 19DLR(SC)38; PLD 1974 (Lahore) 256; 12BLD440; 12BLD314; 2BLT (HCD)164; 5BLD(AD)1 10; 14BLD(AD)222; 14BLD(AD) 14—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Anticipatory Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the F.I.R. there is no specific allegation against the petitioner. His name was simply mentioned as a former Director of the company. Therefore, prima facie no offence was disclosed against him. If the petitioner was compelled, to go to the trial Court there was a chance of his being unnecessarily harassed. In that view of the matter the High Court Division should allow the petitioner’s prayer for anticipatory bail.<strong>[Per Muhammad Abdul Mannan,J.] </strong>Cr.P.C, Section—498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Belayet Hossain Sharif Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)172</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Anticipatory Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">On going through the application filed by the petitioner and also hearing his engaged Advocate the High Court Division was not satisfied as to the cause of his non- appearance before the Magistrate. Moreover, the offence with which he has been accused of being punishable with death or imprisonment for life anticipatory bail cannot be granted to the petitioner simply because he happens to be an elected Chairman—Cr.P.C, Section—498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdur Rahman Molla Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)247</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 18BLD172; 15DLR(SC)2; 15BLD(AD) 14—Citea</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Anticipatory Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Observations/findings made while granting bail are made with an eye on section 497(1) Cr.P.C. for the purpose of bail only without affecting the merit of the case. Under special circumstances anticipatory bail may be granted even after submission of charge sheet.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. M.A. Malik, 14BLD (AD)220</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Anticipatory Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Anticipatory bail should be granted by the High Court Division for a limited period or till filing of the charge-sheet, whichever is appropriate in the circumstances of the case. After expiry of the period or filing of the chare-sheet, as the case may be, the accused must appear before the Court concerned and obtain fresh bail from the Court on the merit of the case.<strong>(Per Latifur Rahman,J)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Abdul Wahab Shah Chowdhury, I9BLD(AD)189</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Anticipatory Bail—Public Commotion</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The prayer may also be considered may occur if it is proved that on account of some local public commotion or other circumstances it is not possible for the petitioner to appear before the lower Court for seeking bail. The petitioner must, however, remain physically present in Court before his application for bail can be entertained. The Court, if it grants anticipatory bail, must expressly make it a condition that if the petitioner commits any breach of the terms of bail, the Court concerned will be at liberty to cancel his bail and take him into custody. <strong>[Per A.T.M. Afzal, CJ.]</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Abdul Wahab Shah Chowdhury, 19BLD(AD)189</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>APPEAL</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Appeal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the order of conviction and sentence passed by the trial Court is not a proper one, the impugned judgment is to be set aside.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Malai Miah Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD)277</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 33 DLR 320; 38 DLR(AD)311; 38 DLR 235; 41 DLR11</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Appeal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Disposal of a criminal appeal in a summary manner just on an untenable medical ground and without considering the entire evidence on record, particularly the evidence of the eye witnesses, is not sustainable in law. Cr.P.C—Ss. 410 and 423</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Abdul Khaleque alias Abdul Khaleq Howlader, 17BLD (AD) 187</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Appeal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If a superior Court disposes a criminal matter, more particularly a criminal appeal of a great importance in a light hearted manner without any application of judicial mind, then that will have a demoralizing effect on the subordinate judiciary in administering criminal justice in Bangladesh. Cr.P.C—S.410</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Giasuddin and others, 18BLD (AD) 254</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1953 PLD(FC)317; PLD 1966 (Lahore) 8; 39 DLR(AD) 117—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Appeal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">After a complaint case ends in conviction and sentence of the accused, the State comes to the picture as it is the duty of the State to execute the verdict of conviction and sentence imposed upon the accused. The complainant cannot claim any right of audience in an appeal preferred against an order of conviction and sentence passed by the trial Court. Notice of appeal under section 422 Cr. P. C. is required to be given to the appellant or to his lawyer as well as to the State and none else.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Kamal Miah alias Nasim Vs. The State and another, 18BLD (HCD)187</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> AIR 1960(Andra Prodesh)311—relied</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Appeal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">As a matter of practice the High Court Division normally grants bail to the persons who are acquitted after a full fledged trial when the State prefers an appeal against the order of acquittal. The normal order upon admitting the Government appeal is to direct the Deputy Commissioner concerned to take the acquitted persons into custody and release them on bail to the satisfaction of the Deputy Commissioner, Cr.P.C—S. 417</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Hafez Howlader alias Habi bar Rahman and others Vs. The State, 19BLD (AD)1</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Appeal</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dismissal of criminal appeal on default</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">After a criminal appeal is admitted for hearing, it is required to be disposed of only on merit. Law does not contemplate dismissal of a criminal appeal for default.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Kamal Miah alias Nasim Vs. The State and another, 18BLD(HCD)187</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>APPEARANCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Appearance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a complaint case, on the first date fixed for appearance of the accused as well as on each subsequent date, the complainant (if he is not a public servant) must personally appear before the Court.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Ayub Ali Bangali Vs. Mia Munir Ahmed and others, 13 BLD (HCD) 224</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A.I.R. 1948 (Madras) 45; 36 DLR. 349—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>APPELLATE DIVISION—PRACTICE OF</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="text-align: justify;">The submission that having regard to the evidence ‘of the informant that the articles stolen from his house were worth about Tk. 5,000/- only, the Village Court had exclusive jurisdiction to try the case and the learned Magistrate acted illegally and without jurisdiction is an afterthought submission in that neither in the trial Court nor before the High Court Division this question was ever raised. The petitioner is not permitted to raise this question for the first time before the Appellate Division.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Haider Ali Khan Vs. The State, 14BLD (AD)270</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ARMS</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Arms</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">No licence is required to possess a dagger. A person therefore cannot be convicted under the Arms Act for possession of a dagger. Arms Act, 1878 (XI of 1878) Section— 4, Arms Rules, 1994, Rule -30</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mozammel Hossain Vs. The State, 17 BLD (HCD) 635</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Arms</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is well-settled that Rules being subordinate legislation made by the executive authority of the Government cannot override the provisions of the parent Act made by the legislature. “Arms” as defined in section 4 of the Arms Act includes a “dagger” and for possession of a dagger a licence under Rule 33 is necessary. Arms Act, 1872 (XI of 1878), Section—4, ‘Arms’, Arms Rules, Rules—30 and 33</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Maudud Vs. The State, 18BLD(HCD)340</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Arms</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">The prosecution has failed to prove that ‘disco razor’ and ‘spring knife’ are ‘arms’ within the definition Of the Arms Act, 1878 even if they are taken to be ‘daggers’ as such still then conviction of the appellants cannot be sustained under section 19(f) of the Arms Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Saiful Islam Milon and another Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD) 451</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ASSAILANT</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Belated disclosure of the names of the assailant</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">When eye-witnesses witness the occurrence, the assailants depart from the scene and many people assembled at the place of occurrence, followed by officials, it is natural that the eye-witnesses will name the assailants not only to their own kith and kin but also to uninvolved persons. Belated disclosure of the names of the accused makes their evidence doubtful.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nowabul Alam and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD)54</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Ref: 5DLR(SC)65; 14DLR(SC)8 1 ;A.I.R.1965 (SC) 202; 14 DLR (SC) 159; PLD 1960 (SC)387; A.I.R. 1954 (SC)2; AIR 1952(SC) 2; 167; A.I.R. 1973 (SC) 1073—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ASSESSMENT OF EVIDENCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Assessment of evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There are two dying declarations made by deceased Hatem Ali. The second dying declaration being nearer to death, the High Court Division believed the same and acted on it. Two eye-witnesses deposed that the appellants had inflicted fatal injuries on the person of the victim but the High Court Division placed no reliance on these witnesses. There can be no justification for disbelieving these witnesses merely on the ground that they were not disinterested witnesses, although the defence failed to shaken in any way their credibility in cross-examination. Evidence Act, 1872, Section—32(i)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Samad Sikdar alias Somed Sikder Vs. The State, 17BLD (AD) 239</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Assessment of evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the guilt of the accused is proved by the consistent and corroborative evidence of a number of eye-witnesses and their evidence is found to be unblemished, the medical certificate proved by a Doctor appearing for the prosecution showing that during the relevant time the accused was admitted in the Rajshahi Medical College Hospital as an indoor patient cannot be relied upon because of the dubious nature of the certificate.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shaheen and another Vs. The State, 17BLD(AD)282</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Assessment of evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the prosecution fails to examine the natural, probable and disinterested witnesses from the vicinity of the place of occurrence and offers no satisfactory explanation, adverse presumption under section 114(g) of the Evidence Act must be drawn against the prosecution.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Tomezuddin Biswas alias Kalu and another Vs. The State, 17BLD (HCD)174</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 40 DLR 348; 11 BLD (1991)231—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Assessment of evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If the prosecution withholds material witnesses named in the chargesheet and fails to examine natural and probable witnesses from the neighbourhood without any plausible explanation an adverse presumption under section 114 (g) of the Evidence Act, should be drawn against the prosecution.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Momin alias Md. Mominul Huq Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)246</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Assessment of evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the occurrence took place in broad day light on a busy pathway and the prosecution failed to examine any natural and probable witness from the neighbourhood and offered no explanation for that, an adverse presumption under section 114(g) of the Evidence Act is to be drawn against the prosecution</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Alamgir Hossain alias Alamgir Hossain Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)270</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Assessment of Evidence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Statement of a victim girl in custody of the accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a young girl lives with the accused after the alleged abduction she is generally prone to undue influence which is brought to bear upon her by the accused aiming at encountering the case of the lawful guardian. The statement of the victim girl under such circumstances should be received with reservations. The Court should give more importance to the words of the parents to those of a wayward daughter who is currently enamoured with romanticism.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Badiur Rahman Chowdhury Vs. Nazrul Islam and another, 16BLD(AD)263</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Assessment of evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the prosecution withholds material witnesses like the doctor who held the postmortem examination, the Investigation Officer and other charge-sheeted witnesses without satisfactory explanations, the Court has to draw an adverse presumption against the prosecution under section 114(g) of the Evidence Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Munsurul Hossain Vs. The State, 16 BLD(HCD)326</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 36 DLR 333; 28DL R 128—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Assessment of evidence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Evidence False in part may not be false in its entirety</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In our country witnesses have a tendency to exaggerate, embroider and falsely implicate persons in addition to the real offenders. But even then the Court is to scan the entire evidence so as to come to a correct decision as to which part of the evidence is to be accepted and which part to be rejected. Falsehood in one part of the evidence does not necessarily mean falsehood in its entirety.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Masum and others Vs. The State, 16 BLD(HCD)151</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Assessment of witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A witness for the prosecution does not become partisan per se nor an eye-witness can be disregarded merely because he has come to support the prosecution party. It is necessary to consider the whole evidence and then to assess the worth of the witnesses as a whole.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Abdul Khaleque alias Abdul Khaleq Howlader, 17BLD(AD) 188</em></strong></p><p><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ATTEMPT</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Attempt to commit terrorist act</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere attempt to realise ransom does not constitute any offence under section 4 of the Anti-terrorism Act since an attempt to realise money in the form of donation or otherwise has not been made a punishable offence under this special law. Section 4 of the Anti- Terrorism Act will be attracted only when the attempt is successful—Anti-Terrorism Act, 1992, S.4</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shadesh Chandra Saha Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 93.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1043	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [B]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  13BLD(AD)190, 13BLD(AD)1, 13BLD (HCD) 445, 13 BLD (HCD)367, 13BLD(HCD)123, 13 BLD (HCD)585, 13 BLD (HCD)126,14BLD(HCD)501,15BLD (HCD) 167,  15BLD(HCD)376, 15BLD(AD) 14,16BLD(AD)45,  16BLD(AD)200,16BLD(HCD)364, 17BLD (HCD)370, I7BLD (AD)163,17BLD(AD)111, </p><p><b>Subject:</b>  BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [B]</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em><br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BAIL</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The prohibition under the law is that a person accused of any non-bailable offence shall not be released on bail if there is reasonable ground for believing that he is guilty of an offence punishable with death or imprisonment for life—S. 497(1), Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shaikh Shahidul Islam Vs. The State 13BLD(AD)190</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Order of conditional bail is illegal and not proper—S. 498 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.H.M. Siddique Vs. The State, 13BLD(AD)1</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref.</strong> 15DLR(SC)429:Crl. Appeal No. 9D of 1991—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail on conviction</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The section entitles a lower Court to grant bail to a convict for a limited period pending filing of appeal. Section 426, sub-section 2A Cr.P.C is the section which entitles lower Court to grant bail to a convict for a limited period after conviction in order to enable him to prefer an appeal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shaheb Ali Vs The State, 13BLD (HCD) 44</em></strong>5</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 43 DLR (AD) 120—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Bail under section 426(2A) may be granted both by the trial Court and by the appellate Court. But the conviction in such a case must not carry beyond one year. But the very language of the section shows that the power to grant bail under section 426(2A) Cr.P.C. is not available either to the trial Court or the appellate Court when the conviction carries sentence of imprisonment for more than one year.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shaheb Ali Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 445</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An accused who is not under the age of 16 years or any woman or any sick or infirm person, can not be released on bail if there appears reasonable ground for believing that he has been guilty of an offence punishable with death or imprisonment for life. The fact that the accused has become a member of the parliament is no ground at all for releasing him on bail since his case does not come within the purview of section 497(1) Cr.P.C S.497(1) Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Auranga @ K.M. Hemayetuddin, 13 BLD (HCD)367</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Bail in a non-bailable offence is a matter of discretion of the Court which has to be exercised with due care and caution and the facts and circumstances of the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Major (Rtd.) Md. Matiur Rahman and another Vs. the State, 13BLD(HCD)123</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> PLD1972S.C.81(84); (1988)8 BLD (AD)21—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Prosecution witnesses discrepant on material point-bail granted.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Akbor All @ Md. Akbor Ali and others Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD)585</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Petitioners involved in the case for co-lateral purposes in order to harass and humiliate them before the public. More than a year elapsed since the submission of the charge-sheet—Petitioners enlarged on bail.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Major (Rtd.) Md. Matiur Rahman and another Vs The State, 13 BLD (HCD)126</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Bail in a pending appeal involving murder charge—The sole basis for conviction of the appellant-petitioner under sections 302/34 of the Penal Code being the exculpatory confessional statement of the co-accused and there being no chance of an early hearing of the appeal and the petitioner having already suffered imprisonment for more than two and half years, the petitioner is allowed bail pending hearing of the appeal—Cr.P.C; S. 426</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Ayub Ali and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)501</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is the normal practice of the High Court Division to grant bail to the accused respondents in an appeal preferred by the State against an order of acquittal passed after a regular trial. The reason is that in such cases the accused persons have already satisfied the competent Court that there are no reasonable grounds for believing that they have committed the alleged offence. The Appellate Division held that as a measure of punishment bail should not be refused in such a case. Bail is granted to the appellants. Cr.P.C. (Second Amendment) Act, S. 426</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Hafiz Howlader alias Habibur Rahman and others Vs. The State, 15BLD (AD) 144</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When an application for bail is filed before the Sessions Judge before the submission of the charge-sheet under the Special Powers Act, the Judge deciding the bail matter acts as the Sessions Judge and not as the Special Tribunal. In such a case no appeal under section 30 of the Special Powers Act lies to the High Court Division.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abul Kalam Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD) 167</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The absence of any specific overt act in the charge-sheet against the accused, they being in custody for a long time, the coaccused being on bail and there being no prospect of an early trial and the petitioners being fairly old are mitigating circumstances for allowing them bail. Cr.P.C—S.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sultan Ahmed and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)376</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Anticipatory Bail—Section 498 Cr.P.C. gives extended and wider powers to the High Court Division for granting bail in nonbailable offences. But this power must be exercised in a reasonable and judicial manner so that the normal practice founded on justice and equity is not disregarded, unless there be exceptional circumstances.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Atiquallah Khan Masud Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(AD) 14</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> Muhd. Ayub Vs Muhd. Yakub, 19 DLR (SC)38 (54): PLD 1966 (SC) 1003 (1022-1023—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Suo motu cancellation of bail of the accused not before the Court</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">The suo motu cancellation of bail of the accused-petitioners, who were not before the Court and nobody prayed for cancellation of their bail, is not sustainable in law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Harun-ur-Rashid and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(AD)45</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Long delay in holding trial is a good ground for bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since the prosecution has failed to show any cogent reason for not holding the trial for a long time without any fault on the part of the accused and the co-accused are on bail, the accused-appellant is entitled to the privilege of the bail. Special Powers Act, 1974 (XIV of 1974) Section.32</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Amin alias Bada Vs. The State, 16BLD(AD)200</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the F.I.R, shows that co-accused Momtazul Hoque Rafique shot at deceased Kafiluddin and thereby caused his death and the postmortem report shows that the victim had a bullet injury on his body, it prima facie shows that accused Momtazul Hoque is responsible for murder. Mere presence of the petitioner at the place of occurrence is not by itself enough to prove his involvement in the murder. Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898— Section.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ghulam Faruk alias Ovi Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)364</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since there is no early chance as to when the appeal will be disposed of and the trial shall commence and since all the co-accused are on bail, the appellant was allowed bail in the interest of justice—Special Powers Act, 1974—S ection.30</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Liton Vs. The State, 17BLD (HCD)370</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Law permits granting of bail to a woman or to a sick and infirm person even in a case where there are even reasonable grounds for refusing bail under normal circumstances.
