The respondent has not been granted bail upon these considerations but upon the view that there are no reasonable grounds for believing that she has been guilty of the offence alleged. The view taken by the High Court Division does not suffer from any legal infirmity—Cr.P.C. Section.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Jobaida Rashid, I7BLD (AD)163</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Issuance of warrant of arrest without cancellation of the existing bail is neither proper nor legal. Since the accused prayed for time for moving the High Court Division for transfer of the case, judicial courtesy demanded that the learned Sessions Judge ought to have granted at least a short time for enabling the accused to move the High Court Division for transfer of the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Halim and ors. Vs. The State and another, 17BLD(AD)111</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Gravity of the offence by itself is no ground to refuse bail to an accused when the prosecution fails to place any material on record to connect him with the alleged offence—Cr.P.C Section.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Kader Faruque alias Mohd. Faruque Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD)148</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The learned Magistrate ordered for further investigation of the case by the police. It is not certain when such investigation will be completed and the case sent for trial to the competent Court. Trial of the case is likely to be delayed for unlimited period and this provides a special ground for bail—Cr.P.C Section.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shahed Reza Shamim Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD)310 (AD)163</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere incidentally naming the petitioner in the chargesheet without any prima facie material and the mere fact that in the occurrence the Head of the State with members of his family was murdered cannot be considered as good grounds for refusal of bail— Cr.P.C Section.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jobaida Rashid Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD)366</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It appears from the F.I.R. charge-sheet, age certificate, postmortem report that several accused persons threw stone, including petitioner No. 1. The postmortem report shows that there were seven injuries on the victim but it does not show any injury on the chest of the victim. From the school certificate it is found that at the time of the occurrence petitioner No. I was merely 14 years old. Considering the circumstances the. Court held that the ends of justice will be met if the petitioner be enlarged on bail—Cr.P.C Section.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Asik Miah and another Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD) 253</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the fact that the appellant has been in custody for about 2 years and as things stand the conclusion of the trial is going to be delayed, the appellant is entitled to get the benefit of section 339C of the Criminal Procedure Code in securing bail.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Aslam Vs. The State, 18BLD(HCD)163</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is no inherent power of the High Court Division or the Court of Sessions to grant bail at any stage. Therefore status in life, affluence or otherwise are irrelevant while considering the prayer for granting anticipatory bail—Cr.P.C. Sections—496, 497 and 498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>K.M. Jahangir Alam Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)680</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 498 like section 496 or 497 does not require a person to place himself in custodian lagis to be dealt with in accordance with law. Therefore, if he is allowed anticipatory bail then upon furnishing bond he will subject himself really to one form of custody. If the prayer is rejected or adinterim bail is allowed but is subsequently cancelled, then the matter ends there and does not entail the consequence that he has to be given to the police or jail custody. His position cannot be worse than before the refusal of his prayer for/or cancellation of anticipatory bail— Cr.P.C Sections—496, 497 and 498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>K.M. Jahangir Alam Vs. The State, I8BLD(HCD)680</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A man with a high social standing ranks equal before a Court of law with a man sleeping rough on the pavement in front of the Curzon Hall. No one is entitled to claim a privilege for himself nor can he claim a preferential treatment ir a Court of law on account of his wealth or status. So, innocence simpliciter is not a ground for anticipatory bail. <strong><em>[Per Syed Amirul Islam J]—</em></strong>Cr.P.C Section.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Belayet Hossain Sharif Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)172</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Where there is a possibility that the accused may abscond and tamper with the evidence in a case which provides for a sentence of death or imprisonment for life, the accused is not entitled to bail—Cr.P.C Section.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Major (Retd.) M. Khairuzzaman Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)357</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Only after disposal of a Rule confirming the adinterim bail granted by the High Court Division a subordinate Court would be competent to cancel the bail for misusing the privilege of such baii—Cr.P.C Section.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Mominur Rahman alias Zinna and another Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD) 433</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 18BLD(HCD)( 1998)172—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail—Delay in holding trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is an unnecessary delay in holding the trial and there is no certainty when the witnesses will turn up and when the trial can be concluded. In such a position of uncertainty regarding the conclusion of the trial, the High Court Division granted bail to the accused-appellant.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Anowar Hossain @ Mohasin @ Anar Vs The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 103</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail—When prosecution case is vague</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Having considered the vagueness of the prosecution case, the statements of 2 witnesses made under section 14 of the Ain and the mysterious dropping of accused Jalal and Lahauddin from the charge sheet, the High Court Division was inclined to grant bail to the appellant.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bashani @ Shirajuddin Vs. The State, 20 BLD (HCD)575</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 497 of the Code speaks that when there is a reasonable ground for believing that a person is guilty of offence punishable with death or imprisonment for life, he should not be granted bail. Section 497 of the Code is a procedural law and the accused having alleged to have committed a substantive offence of murder his liberty is curtailed and as such the argument with regard to violation of fundamental right cannot be accepted in the facts of the instant case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Faisal Alam Ansari, 20 BLD(AD)289</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail in a grave offence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The accused-petitioner is an architect but he had direct complicity in the commission of offence of the triple murder. The High Court Division held that the accused-petitioner is not entitled to get any bail in the instant case. Cr.P.C. Section—498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shaik Mohammad Aslam alias Md. Aslam alias Sweeden Aslam Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)649</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 23DLR(SC)41; 35 DLR (AD)279; 7 BCR(AD) 143—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In an appeal against a short sentence bail should be ordinarily granted in the exercise of a proper discretion because usually it takes time to hear the appeal and with the passage of the period of sentence the appeal becomes infructuous—Cr.P.C Section.426</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Alal Uddin Vs. The State, 19BLD (AD)202</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 11 BLD(AD)96—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Granting of anticipatory bail is an extraordinary remedy and as such in the case of accusations of non-bailable offences anticipatory bail should not be granted and the person should be directed to seek bail under section 497 of the Code—Cr.P.C Ss.497 and 498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Abdul Wahab Shah Chowdhury, 19BLD (AD)189</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is no dispute that the prayer for pre-arrest or anticipatory bail can be made only under section 498 of the Code and under no other section of the Code. The High Court Division and the Court of Sessions have concurrent power under the section.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">The pre-arrest or anticipatory bail is an extraordinary remedy and an exception to the general law of bail which can be granted only in extraordinary and exceptional circumstances upon a proper and intelligent exercise of discretion—Cr.P.C Section.498</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Abdul Wahab Shah Chowdhury, 19BLD (AD) 189</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Reading the statement of co-accused Taheruddin Thakur recorded under section 164 of the Code and the statement of some of the witnesses recorded under section 161 of the Code it appears that there is a reasonable ground for believing that they may be a party to a criminal conspiracy as alleged by the prosecution and accordingly the prayer for bail is rejected with a direction to the State to take all necessary steps for medical care and attention of the petitioners, who are aged and said to be suffering from various ailments.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>K.M. Obaidur Rahman Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 137</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Court, if it grants anticipatory bail, must expressly make it a condition that if the petitioner commits any breach of the terms of bail, the Court concerned will be at liberty to cancel his bail and take him into custody.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Abdul Wahab Shah Chowdhury, 19BLD (AD)189</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail to a child</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The appellant is in jail hajat since 1.6.1998 in association with other adult criminals in contravention of section 49(1) of the Act and as such in that view of the matter the prayer for bail deserves consideration.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Shamim Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 542</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em>BAIL IN A PENDING APPEAL</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail in a pending appeal</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Where the sentence is of short duration that is for 2 years only and the disposal of the appeal is likely to take time, the High Court Division allowed bail to the appellant following the decision of the Appellate Division.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Yar Mohammad Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 216</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail in appeal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provides that notwithstanding the transfer of a case from one court to another court, the time specified in sub-section (1) or subsection (2), shall be the time for concluding the trial of a case. The provisions of subsection (2B) are not attracted to a transfer made before 1.4.1991—Cr.P.C 339C(2B)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Samad and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD)247</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail in appeal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In an appeal involving short sentence the appellate Court should either dispose of the appeal expeditiously or consider the release of the appellant on ball. It is not necessary for the appellate court to write a long judgment in disposing a bail petition—Cr.P.C S. 426(2)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mahbub and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD)184</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail in appeal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Prayer for bail may be allowed in a case in which there are good grounds for success of the appeal along with other grounds— S.498, Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mizanur Rahman alias Majnu and another Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD)492</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BANKER</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Banker</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The word ‘Banker’ occurring in section 409 of the Penal Code has not been used in the technical sense of the Banking Companies Act. It signifies any person who discharges any of the functions of the customary business of banking and it also includes a firm or company that carries on such business.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mustafizur Rahman Vs. The State and others, 13 BLD (HCD) 287</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1960 Cr. L.J. 188; 44 DLR(AD)192; 23DLR(SC)41—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BENEFIT OF DOUBT</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Witnesses to the case being totally disinterested and they having deposed uniformly on the material question of recovery of the revolver from the possession of the appellant, the appellant is not entitled to get any benefit of doubt</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mahbubur Rahman Khan alias Tipu Vs. The State, 16BLD(AD)268</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">For securing conviction in a criminal case the prosecution must prove beyond all reasonable doubts the individual liability of each and every accused person. Although in the instant case the defence plea fell through and it was found that victim Chapa was murdered by some of the inmates of the house, still then the appellants are entitled to get the benefit of doubt as it could not be ascertained as to which of the 14 inmates of the house had murdered victim Chapa.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Zahirul Alam Kamal and another Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)428</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 39 DLR(AD) 177—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The omission of the only eye-witness to the occurrence to state before the Investigating Officer material facts regarding the involvement of an accused in the alleged murder renders his evidence doubtful.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Ashraf Ali and others, 14BLD (AD) 127</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When from the evidence of the handwriting expert it is clear that the three disputed cheques, on the basis of which moneys were withdrawn, were in the hand-writing of the accused person and he did not challenge that fact and did not explain why he wrote the three cheques belonging to a fictitious account- holders, his guilt is established and he is not entitled to get benefit of doubt out of minor omissions made by the prosecution witnesses.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Ibrahim Hossain Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD)253</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Evidence being contradictory on the question of recognition and there being no circumstantial evidence lending support to the deposition of the sole eye-witness, the prosecution case cannot be found to have been proved beyond reasonable doubt.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul and others Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)221</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In a particular case between the words ‘may be true” and “must be true” there is inevitably a long distance and the whole of this distance must be covered by the prosecution by legal and reliable evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dula Miah alias Nurul Islam and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)477</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27 DLR (AD) 29; 44 DLR(AD) 10, LL.R. Indian Appeals 147; 12 DLR (SC) 156; 12 DLR (SC) 217; 36 DLR 185; 16 DLR 147; 45DLR 171; AIR 1939(PC)47; 25 DLR 399; A.I.R. 1957(SC)107; 6BLD(AD) 1—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The only public witness the baby taxi driver Aslam having failed to recognise the accused persons on dock and he having not named the baby taxi driver nor the number of the baby taxi from which the contraband syrups were recovered and he having not seen from whose physical possession the said syrups were recovered, the baby taxi driver in question neither being an accused nor a witness in the case and no neighbouring shopkeeper having been examined as witness, the prosecution case becomes doubtful and the accused persons are entitled to the benefit of doubt.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Faruque Hossain and 2 others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 162</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The prosecution must prove its case beyond all reasonable doubts by cogent, reliable and sufficient evidence to secure conviction for the accused. The accused is entitled to get all benefits of doubts till the prosecution succeeds in proving its case convincingly.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Md. Musa, 15BLD(HCD)169</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a case where bitter enmity between the parties is proved, some sort of corroboration of the evidence of interested witnesses is necessary. In the absence of such corroboration the accused are entitled to the benefit of doubt.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the F.I.R. mentions gun-shot injuries on the person of the victim but the postmortem report does not mention any such injuries and the Investigating Officer did not find any alamat of firing, the prosecution case becomes shaky.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Serajul Islam and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 613</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><div align="center"><strong>BREACH OF PEACE<br/><br/></strong></div><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Breach of peace</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Satisfaction of the Magistrate regarding the apprehension of the breach of the peace over the possession of a disputed property the jurisdiction of the criminal court to deal with it is ousted.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Taizuddin Mia and ors. Vs. Md. Abdul Kader and another, 18BLD (HCD)77</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Breach of peace</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a civil Court has passed an order regulating the possession of the subject matter of the dispute or when a decree for possession has been passed or a decree for permanent injunction has been granted, the jurisdiction of the Magistrate to act under section 145 Cr.P.C. is ousted.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mozaffar Ahmed Vs. The State and others, 17BLD(HCD) 364</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 45 DLR(AD)31, 35DLR180—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Breach of peace</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Disputes concerning rights of use of immoveable property</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 147(1) Cr.P.C. empowers a First Class Magistrate to draw up a proceeding directing the contending parties to file written statements of their respective claims as to the right of user of any land or water when he is satisfied from a police report or otherwise that there is likelihood of breach of the peace over the dispute in question. But it does not empower him to pass any interim order in respect of user of any such right before holding an enquiry as required by subsections (2)(3) of section 147 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Raquib Ali Vs. The State and others, 16BLD(HCD)170</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Breach of peace</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere non-mentioning of the word ‘satisfaction’ in the order made under section 145 Cr.P.C. does not make the order without jurisdiction but such satisfaction must be present in the grounds stated in the order itself. It must be gathered from the express words used in the order itself and it must not be presumed extrinsic to the order.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When in purported exercise of jurisdiction under section 145 Cr.P.C. without drawing any formal proceeding thereunder, the learned Magistrate illegally attached and sealed the case property from the possession of the petitioner but subsequently he cancelled the said order. He was required to restore to the petitioner the possession of the Cinema Hall in question.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Quddus Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD) 554</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Breach of peace</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a proceeding under section 145 Cr.P.C. the Magistrate is to decide possession of the contending parties on taking evidence. If the finding of the Magistrate as to possession is based on evidene before him from which he could reasonably come to such a finding, the satisfaction of the Magistrate connot be substituted by the satisfaction of the superior court, unless the finding is perverse and shockingly unreasonable.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Soleman Vs. A. Barek Khalifa and others, 15BLD(HCD)198</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Breach of peace</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">When a court is uncertain about the possession in the disputed land under section 145(4) Cr.P.C., it can attach the case land under section 146 Cr.P.C. until a competent civil court determines the rights of the parties thereto, or the person entitled to possession thereof.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shamsuddin alias Shamsudoha Vs. Amjad All, 15BLD(HCD) 196</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Breach of peace</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the Magistrate initiates a proceeding under section 145 of the Code on the basis of a police report called for in connection with an application under section 144 of the Code, such a course is lawful, although it was made beyond 60 days after the passing of the first order under section 144—Cr.P.C. Ss. 144 and 145</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Esrail and others Vs. Md. Ali Ashgar and ors.15BLD(AD)117</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> Rebati Mohan Dey Sarkar Vs. Ansar Ali Mondal, 5DLR 162D—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Breach of peace</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the order of attachment under section 145(1) Cr.P.C. has no rational and legal relationship with any legal proceeding either under section 145 Cr.P.C. or under the Cinemaetography Act, the only measure the learned Magistrate could take in the case was to retrace his irregular steps by giving back the cinema hail to the person from whom it was sealed.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mobarak Hossain (Ratna) Vs. Abdul Quddus and another, 15BLD(AD) 157</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Breach of peace</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The basic requirement for drawing a proceeding under section 145 Cr.P.C. is a dispute over a land likely to cause breach of the peace. If the Magistrate is satisfied that there is an apprehension of breach of peace, he is entitled to draw a proceeding under section 145 Cr.P.C. Mere filing of a civil suit does not oust the jurisdiction of the Magistrate. But if, however, the civil Court has already passed any order regulating the possession of the disputed property, the Magistrate’s jurisdiction is clearly ousted.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Monir Ahmed Vs. Md. Mohan alias Fazle Elahi Mohan and others, 14BLD (AD) 246</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BURDEN OF PROOF</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Burden of proof</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The burden of proving the guilt of the accused lies squarely on the prosecution and it does not ordinarily shift on to the accused. Yet, in anti-corruption cases under the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958 certain burden has been thrust upon the accused to account for the goods and properties entrusted to him—Evidence Act, 1872; Section. 102</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>G.M. Nowsher Ali Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 177</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Burden of proof</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 265B of Cr.P.C lays down that the prosecution ‘shall’ open his case by describing the charge and stating by what evidence it proposes to prove the charge. The word ‘shall’ in the section indicates that the directions stated in the section must be strictly followed, more so in view of section 25A of the Security and Exchange Ordinance which lays down that upon the accused lies the burden of proving that he had not contravened any provision of the Ordinance.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">The burden of proof is neither as heavy as that upon the prosecution nor so onerous and that it is not higher than the burden which rests upon a plaintiff or defendant in civil proceeding.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abu Tayeb, Chairman Vs. The. Security Exchange Commission and anr., 19BLD (HCD) 603</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1936)2 AI1E.R. 1138—relied upon</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Burden of proof</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When it is proved that the, husband and wife were sleeping in the same bed on the fateful night and the wife died due to multiple injuries on her person, a great burden is cast upon the husband to satisfactorily explain as to how his wife died.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gouranga Kumar Saha Vs. The State, 17BLD (HCD)259</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Burden of Proof</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a criminal case the onus of proving the charge against the accused always lies on the prosecution and it must prove its case beyond reasonable doubts. But when the murder of the wife of the appellant takes place in his house while she was living with him in the same house, the accused owes an obligation to explain as to how his wife met with her death.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ali Hossain Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD) 307</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Burden of proof</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The burden of proving the manner of the occurrence lies squarely on the prosecution and it never shifts. When the manner of an incident is not proved, the prosecution case must necessarily fail, no matter the defence version of the case has not also been proved either.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In order to bring a case within the ambit of section 106 of the Evidence Act, “special knowledge” of relevant facts must be aitnbuted to the accused persons in respect of the commission of offence. Evidence Act, 1872, Ss. 101 and 106</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kawsarun Nessa and another Vs. The State,15BLD(HCD) 21</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Burden of proof in matters of detention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The burden of proof to show that the order of detention is lawful lies on the detaining authority. The detaining authority making return to the rule is to place all relevant facts before he Court. Where the respondents do not file any return, the Court cannot satisfy itself as to the justification of detention.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Shameem Vs. Government of Bangladesh and others, 15BLD(AD)138.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1044	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [D]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  16BLD (HCD)533, 13 BLD (HCD)306, 15BLD (HCD)210, 13 BLD (HCD)306, 20 BLD (HCD) 177, 13 BLD (HCD) 152, 14BLD(HCD)201, 18BLD (HCD) 624, 15BLD(HCD)362, 20 BLD (HCD) 72, 13BLD (HCD)277, 15BLD(HCD) 639, 13BLD (AD) 199, 16BLD(HCD)154,16BLD (HCD)589, 15BLD (HCD</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [D]</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DACOITY WITH MURDER</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dacoity with murder</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">When a murder is not committed during commission of the dacoity there can be no conviction under Section 396 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Mesbahuddin, 16BLD (HCD)533</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DEATH SENTENCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Death Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a reference by the learned Sessions Judge/Additional Sessions Judge under section 374 Cr.P.C the proceedings shall be submitted to the High Court Division and the sentence shall not be executed unless it is confirmed by the High Court Division.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Md. Tuku Biswas, 13 BLD (HCD)306</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 21 DLR (SC) 109; PLD 1969 (SC) 89—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Death sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sentence of death passed by a Court of Session shall have to be referred to the High Court Division for confirmation.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">A sentence of death was passed by a Special Martial Law Court and confirmed by the Chief Martial Law Administrator, but the sentence remained unexecuted till the withdrawal of Martial Law. The case must be referred by the Sessions Judge to the High Court Division for its confirmation. In such a case although the sentence of death has not been passed by the Sessions Judge himself, the sentence passed by the Special Martial Law Court having not reached its finality for non-execution, the Sessions Judge is under a legal obligation to refer the case to the High Court Division under section 374 Cr.P.C. for confirmation of the sentence of death.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Baset and another Vs. Government of Bangladesh and others, 15BLD (HCD)210</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 41 DLR 484, 44DLR(AD) 16—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Death Reference</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A Death Reference under u/s. 374 Cr.P.C may be disposed of by this Court even if the condemned accused is absconding.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>The State Vs Md. Tuku Mia Biswas, 13 BLD (HCD)306</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 21 DLR (SC) 109; PLD 1969 (SC) 89—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DEDUCTION OF SENTENCE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Deduction of Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Reduction of sentence cannot be allowed when minimum period of sentence is provided in the law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Habibur Rahman alias Raju Vs. The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 177</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DEFAMATION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Defamation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Imputation, if made by a lawyer in the discharge of his or her professional duty on the character of any person in good faith does not amount to defamation—Penal Code; S. 499/500</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mrs. Sigma Huda @ Sigma Huda vs. Ishfaque Samad and others, 13 BLD (HCD) 152</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1976) 78 Bom. L.R.234; A.I.R.1927 Cal. 823; A.I.R. 1932 Bom. 490—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Defamation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To constitute defamation the words spoken must contain imputation concerning a person intending to harm him in his reputation in the estimation of others—Penal Code; S. 500</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.K.M. Enamul Haque Vs. Md. Mizanur Rahman and others 14BLD(HCD)201</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 28 D.L.R. (AD)38; 1954 Cr.L.J. 800—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Defamation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Complaint made by the petitioners in good faith to the Hon’ble Chief Justice regarding the conduct of the complainant judicial officer is not defamation as the same is covered by the exception—Penal Code; Section. 499, Eighth Exception</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abdun Noor and others Vs. The State and another, 18BLD (HCD) 624</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Defamation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Bringing anything which is unjust or improper to the notice of the public at large is certainly for the public good. In the instant case, the matter being earlier published in other newspapers and the present publication having been made in good faith, it readily comes within the protection provided by the Ninth Exception to Section 499 of the Penal Code. The impugned proceeding is quashed.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Syed Mohammad Afzal Hossain Vs. S.M. Salim Idris, 15BLD(HCD)362</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 19DLR (SC) 198—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DEFENCE MATERIALS</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Defence materials</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The learned Special Judge discharged the accused persons considering the some extraneous materials which were not available either in the complaint petition or in the record and as such the Special Judge acted beyond his jurisdiction be that it under section 241A or 265C of the Code discharging the accused by illegally relying upon some extraneous materials which were not in the record.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abid Hossain Babu Vs. Gazi Mojibul Haq &amp; ors, 20 BLD (HCD) 72</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DEFENCE VERSION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Defence version</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If there is a reasonable possibility of the defence put forward on behalf of the accused being true the accused is entitled to benefit of doubt.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Malai Miah Vs. The State, 13BLD (HCD)277</em></strong></p><p><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DEFINITION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Definition</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>‘public servant’, ‘statutory corporation’ or ‘body corporate’</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">The term ‘body corporate’ as used in section 2(b) of the Act means corporations other than ‘statutory corporations’. The Managing Director of a Company registered under the Companies Act is, therefore, a public servant within the meaning of section 2(b) of the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Khorshed Alam Vs. Azizur Rahman and another, 15BLD(HCD) 639</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DELAY</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Condonation of delay</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the High Court Division is satisfied with the ground for delay in filing the appeal, there is nothing for the Appellate Division to interfere with the discretion.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Huq Vs. The State 13BLD (AD) 199</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Condonation of delay</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Condonation of delay in filing an appeal is essentially the discretion of the appellate Court but such discretion must be exercised judicially. Condonation of delay exparte without hearing the State or the complainant is deprecated</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Minhaz A. Chowdhury Vs. Manzurul Huq and another, 16BLD(HCD)154</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Condonation of unusual delay</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A criminal appeal was filed 1649 days after the prescribed period of the limitation. The petitioner contended that the trial was held in absentia beyond his knowledge and he had no occasion to see the notification in the newspapers. No warrant or proclamation and attachment were executed at his village address.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under these circumstances the High Court Division condoned the unusual delay holding that there was sufficient cause preventing the appellant petitioner from filing the appeal in time and he had no intentional laches—Limitation Act, 1908; Section. 5</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Rahmat Ali Vs. the State, 16BLD (HCD)589</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Condonation of delay</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Poverty, helplessness and absence of any male member in the family to file the appeal earlier were considered mitigating circumstances and compassionate grounds for condonation of delay of 735 days.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shamsul Huq Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD) 276</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Condonation of delay</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Prayer for condonation of delay caused in filing the appeal is required to be allowed-- Limitation Act, 1908, S. 5</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mahirun Nessa Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 170</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Condonation of delay</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 5 of the Limitation Act enables a Court to admit an appeal after the period of limitation prescribed therefore when the appellant satisfies the Court that he had sufficient <br clear="all"/>cause for not preferring it within the prescribed period.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sree Sachindra Chandra Mondal alias Sarker Vs. The Assistant Custodian of Vested and Non-Resident Properties (Land and Building) and Additional Deputy Commissioner (Revenue), Dhaka, and others, 13 BLD (HCD) 583</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1970 SCMR 558 (562); BLD 1986 (AD) 180—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Condonation Delay</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Reasonable explanation must be given for each and every days delay in as much as after the period of limitation, the right of the other party accrues in the matter and as such it should not be lightly dealt with.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bangladesh Inland Water Transport Corporation Vs. M/S. Nazma Transport Company, 13 BLD (HCD) 31</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DELIVERY OF JUDGMENT</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> Delivery of Judgment</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A judgment is a final result of a case but it cannot be considered as an integral part of the trial of a criminal case. Staying delivery of judgment of a case at the conclusion of the trial in the name of the disposal of the counter case for unlimited period is highly deprecated—Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898) S.366</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mastansir Billah Vs. Bepari Abdur Rob and others, 16BLD(AD)111</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DETENTION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Detention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a case in which the Government is unable to produce any material in support of the general and vague type of allegations stated in the order containing grounds that the detenu is a ‘mastan’ and santrashi; the order of detention is illegal—Special Power Act, 1974, S. 3(2)</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abu Sayed Bhuiyan Vs. The Government of the Peoples Republic of Bangladesh and others, 13 BLD (HCD) 164</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 3 1DLR)(AD) 1—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Detention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The necessity of communication of the grounds of detention within 15 days has to be calculated from the date of detention but in view of section 9 of the General Clauses Act, the date of detention is to be excluded while computing this period—Special Powers Act, 1974, S. 8(2).</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mrs. Samirannesa Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh and others, 14BLD(HCD)206</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Detention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 8 of the Special Powers Act providing for furnishing the grounds of detention to the detenu is mandatory. Any detention order made under section 3 of the Act without furnishing the grounds of detention is illegal—Special Powers Act, 1974, Ss. 3 and 8.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bachhu Miah Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)500</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> PLD 1957 (Lahore)497—Cited</p><p> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Detention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Nexus between grounds of detention and order of detention—Want of nexus between the grounds of detention and the order of detention vitiates the entire order of detention.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the absence of any mention about the place, date, time and manner of involvement of the detenu in the alleged prejudicial activities the grounds of detention must be held to be vague, indefinite and unspecified rendering the order of detention illegal—Special Powers Act, 1974, Ss. 3(1) and 8</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Babu Ashok Kumar Baral Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh and others, 14BLD(HCD)363</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Detention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Advisory section of the Special Powers Act requires that the case of a detenu must be placed before the Advisory Board for its consideration within 120 days, failing which the detention becomes illegal. Special Powers Act, 1974, S. 10</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mrs. Samirannesa Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh and others, 14BLD(HCD)206</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 42 DLR 272; 40 DLR 193, 207 and 353—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Detention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 3(3) of the Special Powers Act the Government is not vested with the power to extend the period of the order of detention passed under section 3(2) of the Act by the District Magistrate or the Additional District Magistrate. Independent of the order of detention passed by the District Magistrate, the Government has the power to issue an order of detention under section 3(l)(a) of the Act.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Yeasmin Akhter Vs. Bangladesh and others, 15BLD(HCD) 655</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 47DLR(HCD) 12—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Detention—second order by the Government</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Government is competent to pass a fresh order of detention under sub-section (1) of section 3 of the Act but instead of doing so it passed a second order of detention under sub-section (3) of section 3 of the Act, which is illegal. Consequently the subsequent orders extending the second order of detention passed by the Government are also all illegal and without jurisdiction—Special Powers Act, 1974 (XIV of 1974), Section—3(1)(3)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Samad Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)165</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1 5BLD655; 47 DLR 12—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Detention—exceeding 30 days</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sub-section (3) section 3 of the Special Powers Act provides that no order of detention shall remain in force for more than thirty days after making thereof unless in the meantime it is approved by the Government. Neither in sub-section (2) nor in sub-section (3) there is any provision of passing the order with a condition that the order will come into force on the date of service of the order upon the detenu. Rather, it has been clearly stated that “no such order shall remain in force for more than thirty days after making thereof.” An order is made on the date it is signed.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mosharaf Hossain Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh, 20 BLD (HCD) 412</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Detention of an accused in police custody</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From a combined reading of sections 61 and 167 of the Code of Criminal Procedure it is clear that an accused cannot be detained in police custody for more than 24 hours without an order of remand by a Magistrate. In passing an order of detention touching the liberty of a person the Magistrate must apply his judicial mind for deciding as to whether the circumstances of the case really call for a detention in police custody—Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898) Sections—61 and 167</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Faruk Mahajan and ors. Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD) 15</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 16DLR558;1983BLD325; PLD1960  (WP)Karachi 817; 1 984BLD(AD)193—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DIARY</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Diary of proceedings in investigation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 172 of the Code, a Court is entitled to use a case diary not as evidence but in aid of an enquiry or trial. If on perusal of the case diary or a “naraji” petition, which amounts to a fresh complaint, the Magistrate finds that facts disclosed constitute any offence under the law, it is incumbent upon him to take cognizance against the discharged accused.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Criminal Revision before the Sessions Judge prior to taking of cognizance by the Magistrate against the discharged accused is evidently pre-mature.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Huq Vs. Bazal Ahmed and 3 others, 15BLD (HCD)231</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DISAPPEARANCE OF EVIDENCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Disappearance of Evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To sustain a charge under section 201 of the Penal Code the prosecution must prove that the accused knew or had reason to believe that an offence has been committed and with the requisite knowledge and intent to screen the offender from legal punishment causes the evidence thereof to disappear or intentionally gives any false information in respect of such offence—Penal Code; S. 201</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Khandker Md. Moniruzzaman Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)308</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 35 DLR (AD) 127; 18 DLR (SC)289; 15 DLR (SC) 150; AIR 1952 (SC) 354; 34 DLR, 238; 45 DLR 386; 30 DLR 58; 1984 B.C.R.231; P.L.D. 1978 Lahore 1285—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DISCHARGE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Discharge from custody</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When an accused is discharged in pursuance of a final report by the police, it means that the accused has been discharged from custody under section 500 of the Code and not discharged from the case. In such a case the second prosecution is permissible in law as the order was not passed on merit. A Magistrate is competent to take cognizance on the basis of a naraji petition or a fresh complaint. An order of discharge of the accused from custody at the instance of police does not operate as a bar in taking cognizance against the discharged accused—Cr.P.C. S. 500</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Huq Vs. Bazal Ahmed and 3 others, 15BLD(HCD)231</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 30DLR344; 29 DLR(SC)256—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Discharge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 241A of the Code of Cr.P.C provides that when the accused appears or is brought before the Magistrate and if upon consideration of the records of the case and after giving the prosecution and the accused an opportunity of being heard, the Court considers the charge to be groundless, he shall discharge the accused while section 265C Cr.P.C. provides that in a trial before a Court of Sessions, if upon consideration of the records of the case and after hearing the accused and the prosecution in this behalf, the Court considers that there is no sufficient ground for proceeding against the accused, it shall discharge the accused.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Iftekharul Alam Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD) 188</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Discharge</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">A Magistrate acts in his judicial capacity while discharging an accused on the basis of a final report submitted by the police.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Serajuddowla Vs. Abdul Kader and another 13BLD (AD)94</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A.I.R. 1979(SC)777;AIR. 1941 (Bom) 294; 29DLR(SC) 256—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Discharge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When prima facie there is no material on record to show that the accused was in any way connected with the alleged offence it is to be held that there is no sufficient ground for proceeding against the accused and he should be discharged—Cr.P.C. Sections. 265C and 265D</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Khondker Md. Moniruzzaman, 17BLD (AD) 54</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Discharge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 265C and section 241A of the Cr.P.C are two independent sections which deal with discharge of an accused brought for trial in respect of cases triable by a Court of Sessions and by a Court of Magistrate. These two sections indicate that when an accused is brought for trial before a Court of law, the Court upon hearing the parties and on consideration of the record of the case and the documents may discharge the accused. These two sections have nothing to do with quashing of a proceeding. Section 561A is an independent inherent power of the High Court Division and this power can be exercised in case of abuse of the process of Court and for securing the ends of justice and or to give effect to any order under the Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Latifa Akhter and ors vs. The State and another, 19BLD (AD)20</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Discharge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The prosecution case as set out in the petition of complaint discloses prima facie ingredients of the offence alleged. The exact nature of the offence against the accused petitioners can only be thrashed out upon a trial to scope for discharge—Cr.P.C; Sections— 241A/265C</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gazi Mozibul Huq &amp; ors. Vs. Abid Hossam Babu, 19BLD (AD) 265 </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DISHONOURING OF CHEQUE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dishonouring of cheque</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Dishonouring of the cheque itself does not constitute the offence of cheating. As regards the argument that the accused petitioner issued a cheque knowing full well that he had no money in the account and that conduct amounts to cheating it is held that dishonouring of the cheque itself does not constitute the offence of cheating. On this point reliance was placed on the principle laid down in the case of Md. Asaduzzaman Vs. Salamatullah, reported in 19BLD (1999) (HCD)461</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Motaleb Hossain. Vs. The State, 20 BLD (HCD)573</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dishonouring of cheque</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Dishonouring of the cheque itself cannot be considered as an ingredient of the offence of cheating unless there is evidence to show that after issuing it, the accused has done something more to defraud the payee. Even such a cheque issued with the knowledge that he has not such amount in the Bank account at the moment it will not amount to cheating if he has intention to deposit the money before the cheque is presented for encashment.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mohiuddin Md. Abdul Kader Vs. The State and another, 20 BLD (HCD)499</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DISMISSAL</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dismissal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a complaint case is dismissed under section 203 Cr.P.C. and against such dismissal a revision under sections 435 and 439A Cr.P.C. is filed before the Sessions Judge, the legal and proper course open to the revisional Court, if the order of dismissal is found unjust and improper, is to order for holding a further enquiry under section 436 Cr.P.C. and not to make an order of revival of the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ferdous Mondal and others Vs. The State and another, 17BLD (AD)228</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dismissal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">After a criminal appeal is admitted it is required to be disposed of only on merit. A criminal appeal cannot be dismissed for default.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Kamal Miah alias Nasim Vs. The State and another, 18BLD (HCD)187</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dismissal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the provision of section 423(1)(bb)of the Code in an appeal for enhancement of sentence, the appellate Court may enhance the sentence but such enhancement cannot be made unless the accused is given an opportunity of showing cause against such enhancement.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mizanur Rahman Vs. Mst. Surma Khatun, 18BLD (HCD)512</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DISPOSAL OF PROPERTY</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Disposal of Property</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">For an order to be passed under the provision of section 5 17(1) Cr.P.C for disposal of the goods after the conclusion of an enquiry or trial, the Court has to satisfy itself as to the conditions mentioned therein—Cr.P.C S. 5 17(1)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sompong Vs. The State 13BLD (AD) 121</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DOUBLE JEOPARDY</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Person once convicted or acquitted is not to be tried for the same offence—Principle of double jeopardy</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Reading the provisions of section 403(1)(2) of the Code of Criminal Procedure together with Article 35 of the Constitution it becomes clear that although the present case is in respect of an offence under section 19A of the Arms Act and the previous case was in respect of offences under section 395/397 of the Penal Code, which ended in conviction of the petitioners, the recovery of the rifle during the investigation of the earlier case and the rifle in question being the subject matter of the dacoity case as an alamat and the connected acts forming the same transaction, the subsequent proceeding offends against the provisions of section 403 Cr.P.C. and Article 35 of the Constitution and as such the subsequent case is not maintainable in law..</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdur Rashid alias Rashid and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)113</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> P.L.D.1963(Dacca) 661; 5 BLD (AD) 323—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DOWRY</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dowry</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The payment must be ‘as a consideration for the marriage’ to be considered as dowry. The claim for dowry, where made long after the marriage and not ‘as a consideration for the marriage’ does not come within the mischief of the definition of dowry given in section 2 of the Dowry Prohibition Act, 1980. Dowry Prohibition Act, 1980, Section—2</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Anwara Begum Vs. The State and another, 13 BLD (HCD) 473</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 D.L.R. 227; 20 DLR 590—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dowry</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Dowry’ under section 6 of the Ordinance not only includes money or property agreed to be paid before or at the time of the marriage but also money or property demanded afresh after the marriage for continuing the marriage already solemnized. Cruelty to Women (Deterrent Punishment) Ordinance, 1983 S.6</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ajit Kumar Pramanik and others Vs. Bakul Rani Pramanik and others, 14BLD (HCD)335</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 40 DLR 360—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dowry</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The term <strong>?????</strong> (dowry) has been used in the Ain not only to mean property or valuable security agreed to be given at the time of or before or after the marriage but also property as demanded after the marriage for which there was no previous agreement—Nari-OShishu Nirjatan (Special Provision) Am, 1995 (XVIII of 1995),Sections—2(Cha), 10 and 11</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dipak Kumar Roy alias Kazal Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD) 392</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 46 DLR(AD) 169—relied</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dowry</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To attract the provision of section 6 of the Ordinance there must be causing of death or grievous hurt or attempt to cause death or grievous hurt to a woman for dowry. Causing of simple hurt does not bring the offence within the mischief of section 6 of the Cruelty to Women (Deterrent Punishment) Ordinance, 1983.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr Atikur Rahman Vs The State, 14BLD (HCD)303</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dowry—its ingredients</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">“Dowry” as defined in section 2 of the Act contains four ingredients: (1) it has to be any property or valuable security, (2) it has to be given or agreed to be given either directly or indirectly by the parties mentioned in clauses (a) or (b), (3) it has to be given or agreed to be given at the time of marriage or at any time before or after the marriage and (4) it must be as a consideration for the marriage.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 3 of the Act makes the giving and taking of dowry and the abetment thereof an offence while section 4 makes the demand of dowry an offence.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">If a fresh demand for dowry is made after solemnization of marriage, about which there was no prior agreement and which demand does not fall strictly within the definition of dowry as contemplated in section 2, such a demand becomes an offence under section 4 of the Act—Dowry Prohibition Act, 1980, Ss. 2—4</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Bashar Howlader Vs. The State and another, 14BLD(AD) 185</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR 227; 40 DLR 360; 1983 Criminal Law Journal, 888; 46 DLR. 290; 1983 Cr1 L.J, 269; AIR 1983 (SC) 1219: 1983 Crl. L.J. 1501—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DUTY OF JOURNALISTS</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Duty of Journalists</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The journalists must maintain the norms in publishing a news, particularly the news with regard to the judiciary and they must be very careful in publishing the same.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Shafique Rehman, 17BLD (HCD)278</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em> <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DUTY OF PUBLIC</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> Duty of Public</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 44 of the Code of Criminal Procedure imposes a legal duty upon every person aware of the commission or of the intention of any other person to commit any of the offences specified therein to give information thereof forthwith to the nearest Magistrate or Police Officer.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Shafique Miah Vs. The State, 17 BLD(AD) 284</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>DYING DECLARATION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dying declaration</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Three conditions are required to be fulfilled before a dying declaration is acted upon. The first condition is whether the victim had the physical capability to make the statement. Secondly, whether the witnesses heard the statement correctly and reproduced the names of the assailants correctly. Thirdly, whether the maker of the dying declaration had opportunity to correctly recognise the assailants—Evidence Act, 1872, S. 32(1)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Abul Khashem Vs The State, 15BLD (HCD) 205</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 42 DLR (AD) 200—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dying Declaration—its value</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case the dying declaration was recorded by a Magistrate in presence of the doctor attending upon victim Nasiruddin. Inspite of the fact that P.Ws. I and 2 stated that the victim was senseless althroughout and P.W. 2 made a confused statement regarding the time of death of the victim, the High Court Division was right in relying on the dying statement in view of the consistent evidence of disinterested witnesses proving its genuineness—Evidence Act, 1872 (I of 1872) Section—32(l)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Lal Mia Howlader alias Lalu Howlader and another Vs. The State, 16BLD (AD)258</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dying declaration</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is alleged by the defence that P.W.1 admittedly not a witness of the occurrence implicated the accused persons out of enmity at the instance of P.W.4 who during cross-examination himself stated categorically that before going to the Thana for lodging the F.I.R. he consulted the matter with the informant P.W.l, which lends support to the defence version that the accused have been falsely implicated in the case in collusion with him. It is also in the evidence of P.W.5 that the dying declaration through alleged to have been made by the deceased on query to her wife, Mobessera did not say that she reported the matter to P.W. 1 before going to Thana for lodging the F.LR. Moreover the daroga deposed that she did not state to him that her husband the victim told her the names of the accused persons or she saw them running away. These statements are also absent in the first information report. Moreover the principal accused Kamrul Islam has been acquitted by another Bench of the High Court Division disbelieving the prosecution story in Criminal Appeal No. 605 of 1995.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Liton Vs.
