The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 219</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dying declaration</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Merely because the victim died some days after recording the dying declaration will not render the dying declaration inadmissible under section 32 of the Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Akkel Ali and others, 20 BLD (HCD) 484.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1045	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [E]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  17BLD(HCD)236, 15BLD(HCD) 188, 13 BLD)(HCD) 296, 19BLD (HCD) 259, 14BLD(HCD)509, 16BLD(HCD)526, 14BLD(HCD)80,  14BLD(AD)163,14BLD(HCD)167, 14BLD (HCD)280,15BLD (HCD) 234,  15 BLD (HCD) 449,20 BLD (HCD)168,14BLD(HCD)383, 14BLD(AD) 36, 14BLD(HCD)391, 18BLD</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [E]</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ENIS OF JUSTICE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ends of Justice</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The petitioner is an old man and Professor of a University suffering from ailments and is not able to go to Gopalganj. Considering the nature of the case we direct that the case may be withdrawn from the Magistrate Court, Gopalganj to the Court of Chief Metropolitan Magistrate, Dhaka where the petitioner must surrender and pray for bail.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Ahmed Sharif Vs The State and another, 17BLD(HCD)236</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>End of Justice</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">It is well-settled that the High Court Division has the widest and most comprehensive power under this section to pass any order or orders either on an application by a party or suo motu to prevent an abuse of the process of the Court or otherwise to secure the ends of justice.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">An accused can, however, prefer an application under section 561A Cr.P.C. only after he becomes previously unsuccessful in his application either under section 241A or 265C Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Iftekharul Alam Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD) 188</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>ENHANCEMENT OF SENTENCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Enhancement of Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If the sentence of fine is to be enhanced, then a Rule should be issued upon the appellant to show cause as why the sentence to pay a fine imposed upon him under the impugned judgment should not be enhanced.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.M.A. Wazedul Islam Vs. The State, 13 BLD)(HCD) 296</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 32 DLR(AD)100; A.I.R. (32) 1945 (PC) 48; 25 DLR 192—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Enhancement of sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In an appeal from a conviction, sentence may be reduced by an appellate Court but sentence cannot be enhanced. From subsection (1)(bb) of section 423 Cr.P.Code it is also clear that sentence can be enhanced only in an appeal for enhancement of sentence and that can be done after giving the accused an opportunity of showing cause against such enhancement.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant appeal, the appellate Court had no power to enhance the sentence of the appellants who filed the appeal under section 408 of the Cr.P.Code against their conviction by the trial Court. Obviously, the enhancement of sentence of the appellants was illegal and without jurisdiction.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Moktar Ali Bepari Vs. The State and another, 19BLD (HCD) 259</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Enhancement of sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sentence can be enhanced by the High Court Division after giving a reasonable opportunity to the accused of showing cause against such enhancement when an appeal is filed under section 417A of the Cr.P.Code by a complainant against any sentence on the ground of its inadequacy. In view of the provisions of section 404 of the Code no appeal shall lie from any judgment or order of a criminal Court except as provided by the Code. So, except under the provisions of section 417A of the Code, there is no other provisions for filing any appeal for enhancement of any sentence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Moktar Ali Bepari Vs. The State and another, 19BLD (HCD) 259</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>EVIDENCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Evidence—when unacceptable?</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the prosecution made out a case in the Court totally different from the F.I.R. case and the statements of the witnesses recorded under section 161 Cr.P.C. and the witnesses suppressed material evidence, the prosecution case becomes totally unacceptable—Evidence Act, 1872 S. 155</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Zakir Hossain @ Jakir Hossain and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)509</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Evidence of the Doctor and the two nurses who attended victim Monowara in the hospital as to the request of a stomach-wash made by persons who brought her there is admissible in evidence as res gastae under Sections 6 and 7 of the Evidence Act as an exception to the rule of hearsay evidence. Evidence Act, 1872(1 of 1872) Ss. 6 and 7.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Yahiya alias Thandu and others, 16BLD(HCD)526</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Evidence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Misreading of Evidence—Making out new cases</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The trial court as well as the appellate court misread the material evidence on record and committed illegality in making out new cases—Order of conviction set aside.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Driver Md. Hasan Ali Howlader Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)80</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Evidence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Re-assessment of evidence—When not permissible</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the absence of any misreading or non-consideration of any material evidence by the Courts below, the Appellate Division refuses to re-assess the evidence afresh.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bellal and another Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD)163</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>EXAMINATION OF ACCUSED</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Examination of accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">This section is meant for giving the accused an opportunity to explain the circumstances appearing against him in the evidence. This is entirely for the benefit of the accused—The Penal Code; S. 420</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Karim Vs. Shamsul Alam and another, 14BLD(HCD)167</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 42 DLR (AD) 31; 27 DLR (AD)175; 36 DLR (AD) 14—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Examination of accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It has been intended as a protection for and benefit of the accused and it imposes a duty upon the Court to examine the accused properly and fairly by bringing all the incriminating materials to the notice of the accused for furnishing reasonable explanations. S. 342</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Zafar and others Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)280</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 33 DLR 320; 11 D.L.R. (HC) 365; 27 D.L.R. (SC) 1; 38 DLR 289; 41 DLR 11; 50 Cr.L. J. 569</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Examination of accused is necessary</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It requires the Court to consider the statement of the accused in the light of the facts and circumstances of the case and the evidence on record. The Court is never justified in ignoring the statement of the accused explaining his position—Cr.P.C S. 342</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.K.M Hafizuddin Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD) 234</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 8DLR(WP)64; 9DLR(SC) 14; 14 DLR 292; 25DLR (SC)73; 5 DLR (WP)67 (old 106)—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Examination of accused must be proper</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It requires that the attention of the accused must be drawn to all incriminating circumstances appearing in the evidence against them so that they may explain these circumstances. Failure to do so renders the conviction illegal—Cr.P.C S. 342</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kamala and others Vs. The State, 15 BLD (HCD) 449</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Examination of accused—prejudice to accused</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">This provision of law is intended for the benefit of the accused. The trial Court is under an obligation to properly comply with the requirement of law so as to avoid any possible prejudice to the accused on this count.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Al-haj Nurul Islam Chowdhury and another Vs. The State, 20 BLD (HCD)168</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><div align="center"><strong>EXAMINATION OF COMPLAIANT<br/><br/></strong></div><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Examination of Complainant</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Examination of the informant under section 200 Cr.P.C. is necessary before sending the case for judicial enquiry and taking of cognizance. When there is a complaint case and a police case over the same occurrence, the complaint case should be tried first and the police case thereafter. There should be simultaneous trail of the two cases.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Kashem and others Vs. Sanjida Begum and another, 14BLD(HCD)383</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref: </strong>37 DLR 227; 37 DIR 335; 28 DLR 359; 18 DLR (SC) 474—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Examination of complainant</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The purpose of examination of the complainant under the section 200 Cr.P.C is to see whether there is sufficient ground for proceeding and not to see whether there is sufficient ground for conviction. Adequacy or sufficiency of evidence can be determined at the trial—Cr.P.C, 5. 200.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>S. A. Sultan Vs. The State and another, 14BLD(AD) 36</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>EXAMINATION OF DOCTOR</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Examination of Doctor</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere filing of an application by the Public Prosecutor that the doctor concerned is away from the country cannot be permissible substitute for legal evidence as it does not fulfil any of the conditions laid down in law— Cr.P.C S. 509(A)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ataur Rahman and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)391</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR 157—Cited</p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>EXPERT’S OPINION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Expert’s Opinion</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The expert’s opinion is not a substantive piece of evidence. The Courts below were quite competent to arrive at their own conclusion as to whether or not the signatures of the petitioner in Ext.3 series were of the petitioner.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Huq alias Md. Nurul Hoque Vs. The State, 18BLD (AD) 83</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>EXTENSION OF DETENTION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Extension of Detention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The order of extension of detention requires to be made with proper application of mind of the detaining authority and it must not be a mechanical routine work by the office staff made much ahead of the expiry of detention.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mrs Samirannesa Vs Govt. of Bangladesh and others, 14BLD(HCD)206.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1046	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [F]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  14BLD (HCD)122, 14BLD(HCD)127, 19BLD (AD) 290, 19BLD (HD)307,14BLD(HCD)33, 14BLD(HCD)94, 14BLD(HCD)308,15BLD(AD)163, 15BLD(AD)54, 15BLD(HCD)53, 15BLD (HCD)133, 15BLD(HCD)205, 15BLD (HCD)281,15BLD(HCD) 449,15BLD(HCD)53,16BLD(HCD)326,  17BLD(HCD)295, 20 BL</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [F]</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>FALSE BELIEF</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>False belief</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A mere promise of marriage made by the accused to a woman intending never to fulfil his promise does not lead to a conclusion that a false belief was caused to her mind that she was the lawfully married wife of the accused—Penal Code, S. 493</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Makhan alias Putu Vs The State, 14BLD (HCD)122</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 34 DLR 366—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>FALSE COMPLAINT</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Recommendation for appropriate action for false complaint</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since the informant Naik Md. Rafiqul Islam of the Bangladesh Rifles (B.D.R.) started this case against the accused with false, frivolous and fabricated allegations, a copy of the judgment was transmitted to the Ministry of Home Affairs, Government of Bangladesh, recommending appropriate action against the delinquent.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mokhlesur Rahman and another Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)127</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>FALSE EVIDENCE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>False evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It appeared to the Sessions Judge that the petitioner was deposing falsely by suppressing certain material facts and as such the complaint was made to the Magistrate for taking appropriate action against him. The Magistrate took cognizance. The matter for investigation is whether the accused-petitioner deposed falsely or not before the Court. The matter will be decided on merit and as such the question of previous false statement on record is wholly irrelevant—Cr.P.C, S. 561A, Penal Code, S. 193</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mawlana Abdul Hoque Vs. Md. Shakhwat Hossain and another, 19BLD (AD) 290</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>  <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is neither the beginning nor the ending of every case. It is only a complaint to get the law or order in motion. It is only an initiative to move the machinery and to investigate into a cognizable offence. It is only at the investigation stage that all the details can be gathered and filled up. The first information report cannot be trçated as the first and the last word of a prosecution case—Cr.P.C, 5. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Al Amin Vs. The State, 19BLD (HD)307</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Embellishment of FIR case—The prosecution case becomes shaky due to inconsistencies and embellishments in the FIR case— Cr.P.C, S. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Haji Md. Jamaluddin and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)33</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Questionable delay in lodging the F.I.R. without any satisfactory explanation makes the prosecution case suspicious—Cr.P.C. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Latif alias Budu and others. Vs.The State, 14BLD(HCD)94</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An F.I.R. may not always contain the details of the occurrence. Omission to mention in it some material facts does not render it a false information. Under section 265C Cr. P.C. a duty has been cast upon the Court of Sessions, upon consideration of the materials on record and after hearing the parties, to discharge those accused against whom there is no ground for proceeding so that frivolous cases do not occupy the time of the Court and cause unnecessary harassment and injury to an innocent person.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In exercising power u/s 439 Cr.P.C. the High Court Division does not generally go into questions of fact. But this Court having paternal and supervisory jurisdiction over all subordinate Courts can certainly, in an appropriate case, scrutinise facts and examine the legality or propriety of any order passed by the Courts below for preventing an imperding injustice,</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Khandkar Md. Moniruzzaman Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)308</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 35DLR (AD) 127; 18 DLR(SC)289; 15 DLR (SC) 150; AIR 1952 (SC) 354; 34 DLR, 238; 45 DLR 386; 30 DLR 58; 1984 B.C.R. 231; P.L.D. 1978 Lahore 1285—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Non-mentioning of the names of the accused persons or some other material facts in the F.I.R. lodged by a person not an eye witness to the occurrence is not always fatal to the prosecution when such absence is satisfactorily explained—Cr.P.C. S.154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Forkan alias Farhad and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD)163</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the investigation of a case has already started prior to the lodging of the ejahar, such an ejahar or the first information report is not admissible in evidence and it must be kept out of consideration. Cr.P.C. S. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nowabul Alam and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD)54</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>F.I.R—Its use and importance—</strong>It being the first recorded version of the prosecution case, it is of great importance. But it is never a substantive evidence. It can be used to corroborate the statements of the informant under section 157 or to contradict him under section 145 of the Evidence Act but it cannot be used to contradict the evidence of any other witness—Cr.P.C. S. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Tajul Islam and others, 15BLD(HCD)53</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is consistent to the safe administration of justice to rely on the testimony of witnesses whose presence is mentioned in the F.I.R— Cr.P.C. S. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dalim and another Vs The State, 15BLD (HCD)133</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">F.I.R. lodged after 35 hours of the occurrence, although the Thana is only half a mile from the hospital where the victim was being treated, is a belated F.I.R. with scope for embellishments for the prosecution—Cr.P.C. S. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abul Khashem Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)205</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">F.I.R. is never a substantive evidence. It can at best be used to contradict or corroborate its maker and none else. Of course, F.I.R. being the earliest recorded version of the prosecution case, it can be looked into to assess the whole prosecution case in the light of the facts and the attending circumstances— Cr.P.C. S. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Subhan Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD)281</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>F.I.R. Its object and importance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The object of the F.I.R. is to make known at the earliest opportunity what the case of the prosecution is against the alleged offenders. It is always viewed with grave suspicion when the story made at the trial differs in material particulars from the story given in the F.I.R—Cr.P.C. S. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kamala and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 449</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Delay in Lodging the FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Unreasonable delay in lodging the F.I.R. inevitably gives rise to suspicion which requires the Court to look for the possible motive and the explanation for the delay for considering its effect on the prosecution version of the case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Tajul Islam and others, 15BLD(HCD)53</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A belated F.I.R. without any satisfactory explanation makes the prosecution case shaky and doubtful—Cr.P.C Section. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Munsurul Hossain Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)326</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The First Information Report is not a substantive piece of evidence and it can be used only for the purpose of corroborating or contradicting the maker thereof. Its value lies in being the earliest recorded version of the prosecution story—Cr.P.C. Section. 154</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Siraj Miah Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD)</em>295</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1952 SCA 40; 143 Indian Cases 479; 11 DLR (Dhaka) 338; 25 DLR 41—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The very purpose of recording a first information report is to indicate what was the manner in which the occurrence was related when the case was first started and also to show what were the facts given out immediately after the occurrence and reported to the police at the earliest available opportunity. The Court is entitled to note the conflict between the first recorded version of the prosecution case and the story that is made out in course of the trial. A comparison between two such versions of the case is not only permissible but imperative in the context of the circumstances.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Syed Habibur Rahman @ Rocket, 20 BLD (HCD)26</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">The vital facts have not been mentioned in the first information report although the FIR was lodged after a lapse of 13 days without any explanation. This belies the evidence of the PWs and hence the condemned prisoner is entitled to get benefit of doubt.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Syed Habibur Rahman @ Rocket, 20 BLD(HCD)26</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>FIR</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From the FIR itself as well as from the deposition of the informant that the FIR was lodged long after 16 hours from the time of alleged recovery of the incriminating articles, though it has been proved that the place of occurrence is three kilometres off from the police station and that the informant and his party went to the place of occurrence in a jeep. So, the delay of 15 hours in lodging the FIR is, no doubt, inordinate and in the absence of any explantion for such inordinate delay, it is very difficult to accept the genuineness of the FIR in question.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Aslam Jahangir Vs. The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 426.</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>FORFEITURE OF PUBLICATION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Forfeiture of Publication</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Power of the Govermnent to forfeit publications and power of the High Court Division to set aside order of forfeiture</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The order of forfeiture is essentially a preventive action so that the author or the publisher of the disputed book does not continue to commit the offence of outraging the religious feelings of any class of citizens and as such no show cause notice is necessary before passing the order of forfeiture. The objective satisfaction of the Government gives the order of forfeiture the sanction of legality which is only subject to judicial review—Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898) Sections. 99A and 99B</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. (Homeo) Baba Jahangir Beiman-alShureswari Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD140</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Forfeiture of publication</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To forfeit a publication the Government is required to state by notification in the official gazette the grounds of its opinion and not its satisfaction for formation of opinion—Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898) Section—99A( 1)(C)</p><p style="text-align: justify;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>FREE FIGHT</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Free Fight</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When it is clear from the evidence that the free fight between the parties took place following an altercation, it stands out that death of the victim was caused without any premeditation in a sudden fight in the heat of the passion and the accused having not acted in a cruel or unusual manner, this readily brings the case under section 304 Part I of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Kalam Azad Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)401</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> PLD 1950(Lahore)90; 14BLD(1994) 33; 40 DLR 443—Cited</p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> FUGITIVE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Fugitive</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Fugitive from Law and Fugitive from Justice</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is well-settled that a fugitive from justice is not entitled to the protection of law but when he is charged with an offence punishable with death, he has a right to be defended by a lawyer appointed by the State—Cr.P.C S. 340</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurun Nahar Zaman Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD) 537</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Fugitive from Law</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Surrender and custody of the accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the accused after his conviction surrenders in the Court and the Court after refusing his prayer for bail orders for sending him to jail and he is held up in custody in the process of being sent to the jail and under such circumstances he executed a vokalatnama duly attested by the Court police on the strength of which an appeal is filed before the Sessions Judge, who grants him bail after condoning the delay, it cannot be held that he was a fugitive from justice.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Minhaz A. Chowdhuiy Vs. Manzurul Huq and another, 16BLD(HCD)154</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> FURTHER INVESTIGATION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Further Investigation Re-investigation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Reinvestigation in the name of further investigation is not contemplated in law. There cannot be any re-investigation into a case in which charge-sheet has already been submitted—Cr.P.C. 173</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mubashwir Ali and others Vs. The state, 14BLD(HCD)566</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR (HCD) 185; 27 DLR (HCD) 342; 31 DLR (AD) 69; 35 DLR (AD) 127; 36 DLR (AD) 14; 37 DLR (HCD) 182; 34 DLR (AD) 222; 39 DLR (AD) 1; 38 DLR (AD) 41—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Further investigation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The police has power to make further investigation in respect of an offence even after submission of a charge-sheet in respect of an accused against whom fresh evidence has been collected in the course of further investigation. But the Magistrate has no power to direct further investigation in respect of the accused persons against whom charge-sheet has already been submitted.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the name of a further investigation the police has no power to cancel a charge-sheet in order to submit a final report in respect of an already charge-sheeted accused person— Cr.P.C. 173(3B)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Golam Mostafa and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)581</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 36 DLR 63; 27 DLR 342—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Further investigation</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Further investigation and supplementary charge sheet</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A superior police officer appointed under section 158 of the Code may direct further investigation, pending the order of the Magistrate, under sub-section (2) of section 173 Cr.P.C. Further investigation may also be held under sub-section (3B) of section 173 after submission of police report under sub-section (1) of section 173 of the Code, if the Officer in-Charge of the police station obtains further evidence in the case. Further investigation on the prayer of the accused persons is not contemplated in law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sukhil Kumar Sarkar Vs. The State and ors., 15BLD(HCD) 311</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Further investigation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From a reading of section 156 together with section 190 (1) Cr.P.C it appears that a Magistrate instead of taking cognizance himself of an offence upon receiving a complaint, may send the same to the police for investigation by treating it as the F.I.R. The final report submitted by police under section 173 Cr.P.C. is never binding upon the Magistrate. Instead of accepting the final report, the Magistrate may, either on his own motion or on receipt of a naraji petition from any affected person, direct the police to make further investigation.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sukhil Kumar Sarkar Vs. The State and ors., 15BLD(HCD) 311</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27DLR (HCD) 342; 37 DLR (HCD) 185—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Further investigation</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Re-investigation under the garb of further investigation is not contemplated in law</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since the police after usual investigation of the case submitted chargesheet against the accused persons the order of the Government, at the instance of the accused, for further investigation of the case by the C.I.D. designed to set at naught the already submitted charge-sheet under the garb of further investigation is not contemplated in law.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abu Talukder Vs. The Secretary, Ministry of Home Affairs and others, 16BLD (HCD)615</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Further investigation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 173(3B) of the Code empowers the police officer to hold further investigation in a case where a report has already been submitted and the subsequent report will be treated as a police report within the meaning of section 173(1) of the Code of Criminal Procedure.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Samad Khan and others Vs. The State and another, 17BLD(HCD) 436 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Further investigation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Although evidence was forthcoming regarding the complicity of some accused persons in the alleged murder but the investigating officer (1.0.) wrongly excluded them from the charge sheet. The High Court Division directed the police to hold further investigation into their case. Section—173(3B)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Monwara Begum, 18BLD (HCD) 102.</em></strong><span id="pastemarkerend"><br/></span></p></div></div>
1047	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [L, M, N, O]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  19BLD (HCD)307, 20 BLD(AD)102, 13BLD (HCD)260,14 BLD(AD)171, 15 BLD(AD)153,19BLD (HCD) 404,19BLD (HCD) 404, 13BLD (HCD) 579, 17BLD (AD) 123, 17 BLD (AD) 43,13 BLD (HCD)277, 14BLD(HCD)467, 15BLD(HCD) 545, 16BLD(HCD)181, 17BLD(HCD) 563,14BLD(HCD)566, 13BLD</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [L, M, N, O]</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>  <br/></em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>LEGAL EVIDENCE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Legal Evidence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Legal evidence is that evidence which is adduced by a witness before the court at the time of trial and not the statement made in the first information report.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Al Amin Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD)307</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Legal evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere placing no reliance upon confessional statement of the accused and non- examination of the Magistrate who held T.I. Parade are no ground for acquittal where the order of conviction is based on other sufficient and reliable legal evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abdul Hashem Vs. The State, 20 BLD(AD)102</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>LEGAL REMEMBRANCES MANUAL</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Legal Remembrances Manual Paragraph 6</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An Advocate to defend an undefended accused charged with capital offence should be appointed in time and provided with necessary papers for arranging a proper and meaningful defence papers.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">An Advocate to defend an undefended accused charged with capital offence should be appointed well in time of the commencement of trial to enable him to study the case and the lawyers should be sufficient standing and able to reader assistance. The lawyer should be provided with the papers similar of the Public Prosecutor and be supplied free of costs the copies of the papers of which an accused person is ordinarily allowed copies.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Hanif Gani, 13BLD (HCD)260</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27 DLR(AD) 1—Cited</p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>LENIENCY IN PUNISHMENT</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Leniency in punishment</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An unblemished record of 22 years of service may provide a good ground for awarding a less harsh sentence in the form of compulsory retirement in place of dismissal from service. But it can never be a good ground for reinstating him in the service when the authority has lost confidence in him.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sonali Bank Vs. Ruhul Amin Khan, 14 BLD(AD)171</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>LICENCE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cancellation and suspension of licence and confiscation of arms</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 18 of the Arms Act speaks only of cancellation or suspension of licence of arms by different authorities while section 24 of the Act empowers the Court on conviction of the accused to confiscate the arms or ammunition in question.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case the petitioner having not being convicted in the case, the direction of the tribunal for taking steps for confiscation of the petitioner’s pistol is neither legal nor proper. The petitioner’s licence can, however, be always cancelled by the appropriate authority under section 18 of the Arms Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abdus Samad Vs. The State, 15 BLD(AD)153</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cancellation of licence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 18 of the Arms Act provides that any gun licence may be cancelled or suspended by the authority by whom the same was granted provided that there are materials showing that there is a threat to security or public peace. Such authority can cancel licence after recording reasons in writing.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Major (Rtd.) M. Asaduzzaman Vs. The District Magistrate, Jessore &amp; others, 19BLD (HCD) 404</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cancellation of licence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 26 of the Arms Act provides that the Government may at any time order or cause to be seized any arms ammunition or military stores in the possession of any person, notwithstanding that such person is licensed to possess the same and may detain the same for such time as it thinks necessary for the public safety.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Major (Rtd.) M Asaduzzaman Vs. The District Magistrate, Jessore &amp; others, 19BLD (HCD) 404</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>LIMITATION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Limitation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a case where limitation has not been specifically prescribed, the pronouncements by superior Courts, particularly by the Supreme Court are that as a rule nobody can be made to suffer any harm to his rights including those relating to property, person and reputation, without giving him opportunity to show cause.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. SWdique Vs. The State, 13BLD (HCD) 579</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 39DLR(1987)164; 42DLR15; PLD 1970 (Lahore)—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Limitation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">As a matter of long standing practice in the High Court Division 60 days limitation as provided under Article 155 of the First Schedule of the Limitation Act for appeal, is being followed for revision. But there is no legal bar to entertain in the interest of justice such revisional application even after the aforesaid period of 60 days provided the applicant can show that he was prevented by sufficient cause to move the High Court Division within the said period of 60 days.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Reazuddin Ahined Vs. The State and another, 17BLD (AD) 123</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Limitation</strong></p><p>Section 14 of the Limitation Act is not applicable to proceedings before the Administrative Tribunal.<strong><em><br/>Abdus Sukkur Vs. Chairman, National Board of Revenue and others, 17 BLD (AD) 43.</em><br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>LITIGATION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Litigation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Litigation existing between accused and some of the witnesses is not enough to bear grudge by the accused so as to commit the offence of murder, rather such litigation sometime can be taken as a cause for false implication.</p><p><strong><em>Malai Miah Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD)277.</em> <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>MAINTENANCE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Maintenance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Family Courts Ordinance, 1985 has not ousted the jurisdiction of a Magistrate to exercise power under section 488 Cr.P.C. The provision of the Family Courts Ordinance, 1985 are applicable not only to the Muslim Community but also to other communities as well.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Meher Negar Vs. Md. Mojibur Rahman, 14BLD(HCD)467</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 42DLR(HCD)450; PLD 1969(SC) 187, SCMR(Vol. IV) 1971 Page 150; 14 BLD (HCD) 41 5—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Maintenance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 5 of the Family Courts Ordinance,  1985 gives exclusive jurisdiction to the Family Court to try any suit for maintenance. An application for maintenance under section 488 of the Code of Criminal Procedure is not a suit and as such the provisions of the Family Courts have not taken away the power of the Magistrate to order for maintenance under Section 488 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Zinnatan Nessa Vs. Md. Kaloo Mia, 15BLD(HCD) 545</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 14 BLD 467= 47 DLR 18—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Maintenance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 3 of the Family Courts Ordinance, 1985 envisages that notwithstanding anything contained in any other law the provisions of this Ordinance shall apply to cases filed under this Ordinance.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">A relief provided in an Act cannot be taken away by implication simply because similar relief has been provided in a subsequent Act without repealing the provision for relief in the previous Act. The power of the Magistrate to act under section 488 Cr.P.C. has not been taken away by promulgation of the Family Courts Ordinance.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Rezaul Karim Vs. Rashida Begum and another, 16BLD(HCD)181</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 47DLR 18; 1971 SCMR 150; PLD 1969 (SC) 187; 42 DLR 450—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Maintenance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The combined effect of the provisions of sections 3, 4, 5 and 27 of the Family Courts Ordinance, 1985 is that the jurisdiction of the Magistrates in dealing with matters contained in section 488 of the Code is ousted after the coming into operation of the Family Courts Ordinance, 1985</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Pachan Rissi Das Vs. Khuku Rani Dasi and others, 17BLD(HCD) 563</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>Ref:</strong> AIR 1961 (SC) 1152; 14BLD415; 14BLD413; 47 DLR18; 21DLR (1969)123; PLD 1984 (SC) 95: PLD 1984 (SC) 95; PLD 1971 (Karachi) 887; 42 DLR 450; PLD 1969 (SC) 187—Cited.<strong> <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>MALAFIDE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> Malafide vitiates everything</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Even a malafide investigation cannot be allowed to stand.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mubashwir All and others Vs. The State and another, 14BLD(HCD)566</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR (HCD) 185; 27 DLR (HCD) 342; 31 DLR (AD) 69; 35 DLR(AD) 127; 36 DLR (AD) 14; 37 DLR (HCD) 182; 34 DLR (AD) 222; 39 DLR (AD) 1; 38 DLR (AD) 41—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>MARTIAL LAW</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence of Martial Law Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A judgment; sentence or order of a Martial Law Court so long it is not reviewed, an accused cannot be said to have derived any vested right from such an order or judgment. The provision that all proceedings of Special Martial Law Courts to be submitted to the C.M.L.A. for review is merely tentative in nature.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Helaluddin Ahmed alias Swapan Vs. Bangladesh and others 13BLD (AD)17</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 43 D.L.R.(AD) 104; 33D.L.R.(1981) 233 and 358; 92 Commonwealth Law Reports, 526 (528); 33 DLR(AD)154; PLD 1978 (SC) 49; PLD 1974(SC) 1513; BLD 1989 (SC)26; 32 DLR(AD)1 10—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>MARTIAL LAW REGULATION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Martial Law Regulation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a judgment of acquittal is under review and settled legal criterion has not been followed and the reviewing authority acted in excess of its power conferred under Regulation 3(4) of the Martial Law Regulation No. I of 1982 in converting the judgment of acquittal into one of conviction, it must be declared to have been passed without any lawful authority Article—102.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdur Rahman Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh and ors., 17BLD (HCD) 332</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>MAXIM</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> Maxim</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Deminimis Non Curat Lax—</strong>The maxim means that law does not take account of trifles. This is intended to prevent penalties of negligible wrongs or of offence of trivial character.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.K.M. Enamul Haque Vs Md. Mizanur Rahman and others, 14BLD (HCD)201</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Maxim</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>‘Justice hurried, justice buried’—</strong>It is well settled that justice should not only be done but should be manifestly seen to have been done. Otherwise, the common proverb that ‘Justice hurried, justice buried’ may become true.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mostafizur Rahman Vs. The State. 14BLD(HCD)471.</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1973 Cr.L.J. 137; 34 D.L.R.(AD)222—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>MOTIVE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Motive</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Motive of Crime</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Although in a criminal case the prosecution is not generally required to prove the motive of the crime, yet in a case which is essentially based on circumstantial evidence, motive becomes an important consideration.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Zafar and others vs. the State, 14BLD(HCD)280</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Motive</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Motive of Murder —When relevant?</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In a case of murder involving unnatural brutality, motive of the crime becomes a relevant fact and in such a case motive is required to be proved by the prosecution.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jalal and 3 others Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD) 588</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 16 DLR 189; 31 DLR 16; 1 1DLR 258; 7BLD (AD) 1, 11 DLR (SC) 38; 35 DLR 339; 2DLR 30; 9 BLD (HCD) 474; 1 1BLD (HCD) 295; 10 BLD (HCD) 236; 10 BLD (AD) 259; 43DLR373; 3DLR(FC)372—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Motive</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Motive is not a necessary ingredient of an offence under section 302 of the Penal Code. The Court will see if sufficient direct evidence is there or not. If not, motive may be a matter for consideration, specially when the case is based on circumstantial evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Giasuddin and others, 18BLD (AD) 254</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em> <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>MURDER</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Murder</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In cases where allegations that a husband had murdered his wife and then absconded, the husband in such a situation had a duty to explain how his wife was murdered and by whom she was murdered. In case of non-explanation by the husband or his silence in the matter or the having absconded immediately after the murder, would be considered to be a good ground for a findings that the husband is guilty of murder of his wife if, however, there is no suggestion or circumstances to show to the contrary that other inmates of the house also used to beat her and killing her in the process—Penal Code S.302.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Nurul Huq, 13 BLD (HCD) 99</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 172 I.C.374; 39 Cr.L.J. 123; A.I.R. 1977(SC)1 116; 126 I.C.689; 62 I.C. 545; PLD 1964 (SC) 813; 31 DLR 312; 16 DLR (Dacca) 598; A.I.R. 1973(SC)2474—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>NON-COMPLIANCE OF THE MANDATORY PROVISION OF LAW </strong></p><p align="center" style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Non-compliance of the mandatory provision of law</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Non-compliance of the procedures laid down in section 27(6) of the Special Powers Act strikes at the root of the trial and the trial stands vitiated from its very inception. Section 537 Cr.P.C. cannot cure this basic infirmity. Notification regarding the attendance of the accused though published in the newspapers by the Magistrate cannot be construed to be a sufficient compliance with the requirement of law as envisaged by section 27(6) of the Special Powers Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Sadeque Ali Vs. Judge, Senior Special Tribunal, Thakurgaon, and others, 14BLD(HCD)369</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1 BCR (AD) 117; 31 DLR (AD) 301; 26 C. L.J.(1925)1336—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>NOTICE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Notice to the accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Notice to the accused is not necessary in revision under section 436 Cr.P.C. for holding further enquiry against the dismissal of complaint.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sirajuddowla and others Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD) 607</em></strong></p><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>NUISANCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Nuisance.—public nuisance and private nuisance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The term nuisance’ has not been given any specific definition in the statute. Following the accepted judicial pronouncements it may be described as an unlawful interference with a person’s use or enjoyment of land/building or some right over it or in connection with it.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">There are two kinds of nuisances-public and private. A public nuisance is one which affects the public generally as an annoyance while a private nuisance is one which injures a private person which the law regards as in excess of the annoyance suffered by the public at large. To constitute of a private nuisance it is enough to show that there is a material interference with the physical comfort of human existence according to plain, sober and simple notions among the common people.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Wahid Mia alias Abdul Wahid Bhuiyan Vs. Dr. Rafiqul Islam and others, 16BLD (HCD)255</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>OBSERVATION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Observation</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>High Court Division comments on the trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Court—When to be expunged?</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">After going through the order sheet of the Court of the Magistrate and copies of the confessional statements the High Court Division took the view, perhaps reasonably, that the confessional statements must have been with the case record. Since the provisions of sections 164 and 364 Cr. P.C. were substantially complied with, the learned Judge ought to have taken this evidence under section 80 of the Evidence Act even without examining the recording Magistrate. The Appellate Division held that the High Court Division did not make any harsh comment against the petitioner on this account. The comment of the High Court Division that the petitioner rejected the petition of the Public Prosecutor for examining the recording Magistrate and the Police Officer who recorded the F.I.R. of the case “rather hastily”, was rather harsh and this could have been couched in a softer manner but nevertheless the expression of disappointment of the High Court Division on this score is natural in the circumstances of the case and the comments made in the impugned judgment, except being harsh, cannot for that matter attract the extreme measure of expungement.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mr. Munshi Mohammad All Vs. The State and another, 16BLD(AD)130</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong>14 BLD 332—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>OMISSION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Omission</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An omission by witnesses to mention about dying declaration, when examined under section 161 of the Code of Criminal Procedure before the Sessions Court is a glaring one and, as it such, amounts to contradiction which makes the statements of the witnesses worthless.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kuti Meah and others Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 572</em></strong></p><p>  <span id="pastemarkerend"> </span> <span id="pastemarkerend"><br/></span></p></div></div>
1048	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [P]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  17BLD (AD) 177, 17 BLD (HCD) 291,15BLD(HCD)117,15BLD(HCD) 501, 20 BLD(AD)236,17BLD (AD) 297,16BLD(HCD)283, 15BLD(HCD) 357, 15BLD(HCD)485, 14 BLD(HCD)286, 14BLD(AD) 270, 19BLD(AD)20,  19BLD(AD)131, 19BLD(AD)299, 16BLD(AD)118, 17BLD (AD) 232,18BLD (HCD) 61</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BLD' 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [P]</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PLACE OF TRIAL</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Place of trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If there is a special order to try a particular case by the Sessions Judge at a particular place the original place of sitting continues to remain the place of sitting of the Court of Sessions and the new place indicated in the special order is meant for trial of only that case or class of cases which the special order specifically provides—Cr.P.C Section. 9(2)</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Sayeed Farook Rahman Vs. The Sessions Judge of the Court of Sessions, Dhaka and others, 17BLD (AD) 177</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Place of trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From the FIR it prima facie shows that the occurrence took place in Dhaka and then it continued and culminated in Sylhet. In such circumstances, the Tribunal at Syihet has not committed any illegality, in taking cognizance of the offence and in framing charge—Cr.P.C Sections—179 and 180</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Anowara Begum Vs. Most. Sultana Jeshmin Khan (Shoapa) and State, 17 BLD (HCD) 291.</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PLACE OF INQUIRY AND TRIAL</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Place of Inquiry and Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Liability for offences committed outside Bangladesh and prosecution for offences against the State.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When an offence is committed outside Bangladesh, the trial of the case cannot proceed without sanction of the Government as ordained by proviso to section 188 Cr.P.C. Sanction obtained under section 196 Cr.P.C, cannot do away with the requirement of the proviso to section 188 of the Code. In view of the proviso to section 188 Cr.P.C., inspite of due cognizance of the case, the Court is unable to try the case without the sanction from the Government.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Taslima Nasrin Vs. Md. Nurul Alam and another, 15BLD(HCD)117</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1980 Crl.L.J. (Noc) 57(Cal); (1979)1 Cal. H.N. 414—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Place at Inquiry and Trial</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ordinary place of inquiry and trial and offences committed on a journey or voyage</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case the offence started when M.V. Emirate illegally entered into the territorial waters of Bangladesh and it was arrested by the Bangladesh Naval Ship ‘Tamzi’ about 5 miles of the Maheshkhali Sea Shore within the territorial jurisdiction of the judgeship of Cox’s Bazar District but the offence continued while the ship passed into the local limits of the Naval Jetty at Chittagong. Under such circumstances, both the Special Tribunals at Chittagong and Cox’s Bazar were competent to try the case. Cr.P.C. Ss. 177 and 183</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Master Osman Gani and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 501</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>POLICE REPORT</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Police Report</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The prosecution is required under the provisions of section 173(3A) of the Code of Criminal Procedure to send the Court the report together with the statement recorded under sections 161 and 164 Cr.P.C. The accused shall be entitled to get the copies thereof before the hearing under Section 265B of the Code. Non-compliance with this requirement of law causes prejudice to the defence. The prosecution must not play hide and seek. However when the copies of statements under sections 161 and 164 of the Code are supplied to the accused before hearing under section 265B Cr.P.C. the defect is cured.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Major (Retd) Bazlul Huda Vs The State, 20 BLD(AD)236</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Police Report</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">While considering the police report (FRT) the Special Tribunal heard the learned Advocates of the parties and on perusal of the case record and the case diary took cognizance of the case on the finding that a prima facie case was made out by the prosecution against the accused persons. Since the Tribunal could not agree with the opinion of the Investigating Officer and took cognizance of the case on the basis of materials before him, the learned Tribunal acted within his competence. No exception can be taken to it.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bilkish Miah Vs. The State, 17BLD (AD) 297</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Police Report</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Magistrate is not bound to accept a police report submitted under section 173 Cr.P.C. recommending discharge of the accused persons. If the Magistrate finds that there are prima facie materials on record to proceed against the accused, he may reject the recommendation of the police and take cognizance against accused persons under section 190(b) Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abu Bakar and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)283</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Police Report</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 173 (3B) of the Code of Criminal Procedure the Police has a right to file supplementary report or reports, known as a supplementary chargesheet, when it obtains further evidence, oral or documentary, after submission of his report under sub-section I of section 173 Cr.P.C For such a report, he is not required to obtain any permission from the Court.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Rahmat Ullah Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD) 357</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>POSSESSION OF ARMS</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Possession of Arms</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In absence of any evidence or circumstances to show that the accused had exclusive possession over the spot pointed out by him and none else had access to it, mere knowledge of the accused about the existence of arms and ammunition as a particular spot does not constitute any offence under the Arms Act, 1878, S. 19A</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shahadat Hossain Babul Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)485</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>POSSESSION OF WOMEN</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Possession of women</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The two explanations appended to section 5 of the Ordinance make it abundantly clear that a possession of a woman obtained through import or export or sale or hire with the intent that such woman might be employed or used for the purpose of prostitution, shall be presumed, until the contrary, is proved, that such possession was for the purpose of prostitution—Cruelty to Women (Deterrent Punishment) Ordinance, 1983 S.5</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Motaleb and others Vs. The State, 14 BLD(HCD)286</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PRACTICE AND PROCEDURE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and Procedure—new question before the Appellate Division</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The submission that since the informant in his evidence stated that the articles stolen from his house were worth about Tk. 5,0001- only, the Village Court had exclusive jurisdiction to try the case is an after thought one in that neither in the trial Court nor before the High Court Division this question was ever raised. The petitioner cannot be permitted to raise this question for the first time before the Appellate Division.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Haider Ali Khan Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD) 270</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and Procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is very surprising that the High Court Division enunciated a wrong proposition of law without entering into the merit of the case. This is not only illegal but also not a proper exercise of jurisdiction vested in the court itself.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Latifa Akhter and others Vs The State and another, 19BLD(AD)20</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and Procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Appellate Division is loathe to hear a petitioner who has neglected to appear before the High Court Division inspite of opportunities offered.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dil Mohammad Vs Abdul Motaleb Khan and another, 19BLD(AD)131</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and Procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Appellate Division does not encourage making of submission which was not made before the High Court Division and which for its resolution requires examination of facts by the Appellate Division.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Miah Hossain and others Vs The State, 19BLD(AD)299</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and Procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a complaint is filed within one year of the last demand for dowry there is no violation of the provision of section 7(b) of the Dowry Prohibition Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Nurun Nabi Sheikh and others Vs. Mst. Fatema Khatun, 16BLD(AD)118</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and Procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When Magistrate dismisses a complaint under section 203 Cr.P.C. and stops further enquiry he obviously passes a judicial order. Unless that order is got rid of, the DAB cannot continue the same enquiry in violation of the said order.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Md. Raihan Ali Khandker and others, 17BLD (AD) 232</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and Procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Column 4 of the Schedule II of the Code relating to the offences against other laws provides that warrant of arrest shall be issued against the accused persons if the offence is punishable with imprisonment for not less than two years and not more than five years.
