Since the offence in question is punishable with imprisonment upto 5 years there is no illegality in passing an order of issuance of warrant of arrest against the accused-persons.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shainpukur Holding Ltd. Vs Security Exchange Commission, 18BLD (HCD) 61</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and Procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 94 of the Code of Criminal Procedure authorises the Court or any competent police officer to ask any person to produce any document or thing under his custody, which is necessary for the purpose of an investigation, enquiry, trial or other legal proceedings. But there is no provision for seizure of such document for facilitating investigation.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Arab Bangladesh Bank Ltd. Vs. Md. Shahiduzzaman, 18BLD (HCD)167</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the provisions of section 476 Cr.P.C a Court cannot proceed directly against any person for giving false evidence or fabricating evidence. The Court cannot also impose any fine upon any witness or direct him to pay any compensation to the accused. A Tribunal constituted under section 26 of the Special Powers Act is required to follow the provisions of section 476 Cr. P.C if it wants to proceed against any witness for commission of an offence under section 193 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Idris Miah Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)288</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Before exhausting the legal formalities as required by sections 87 and 88 of the Code of Criminal Procedure a Court is not competent to resort to the provision of section 20(5)(Kha) of <strong>???? ? ???? ???????? (????? ?????) ???</strong>, 1995 the trial Court acted illegally and without jurisdiction in publishing the impugned notification in a daily newspaper before obtaining service return of the orders of arrest of the accused and of proclamation and attachment of his properties.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The expression “notification” in at least one daily Bengali newspaper used in section 20(5)(Kha) of the Act obviously meant publication of such notification in a widely circulated national daily and to a district based daily newspaper with very limited circulation.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Belayet Howlader Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD) 81</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and Procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Excise and Salt Act and the rules framed thereunder do not authorise the police to seize manually-manufactured cigarettes (bins) on licence from the appropriate authority on the suspicion that those were being marketed by avoiding necessary excise duty as being without the requisite band-rolls thereon. The police have no authority to arrest and prosecute any person directly for the alleged violation of the provisions of the Act. The police can act in aid of the excise authority only when called upon to do so. Excise and Salt Act, 1944 (I of 1944), Section—37, Punishment for violation of the provisions of the Act</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Idris Miah alias Idris All and others Vs. The State and others, 18BLD (HCD) 54</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 23 of the Act provisions of the Cr.P.C. have been made applicable to cases coming under this Act in matters of investigation and trial. Although time limit for completing investigation has been fixed in section 18 of the Act, no provision has been made as to what legal consequences shall follow if the investigation is not completed within the specified time. So, the amended provision of section 167(5) Cr.P.C. shall apply in this regard.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Anwar Hossain Vs. The State, 18BLD(HCD) 18</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Practice and procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Non-publication of notice under section 27(6) of the Special Powers Act, 1974 directing the accused-appellant to answer the charge is illegal and without jurisdiction and as such the same is not curable under section 537 of the Code of Criminal Procedure.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Samiul Haider alias Kuiba Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD) 515</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PRESUMPTION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Presumption</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the evidence on record, the observations made by the Special Judge against the informant in the judgment under appeal are not unjustified and call for no interference—Evidence Act S. 114(g)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The Solicitor, Government of Bangladesh Vs. Feroz Mahmud and another, 14 BLD(HCD)160</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Presumption</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Non-production of the ‘requisition slip’ by the prosecution leads to an adverse presumption against it under section 1 14(g) of the Evidence Act that if the slip was produced it would show that the Director himself obtained the blank Accession Register and made over the same to accused Respondent No. 2 for preparation of a new Register.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The Solicitor, Government of Bangladesh Vs. Feroz Mahmud and another, 14 BLD(HCD)160</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Presumption</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Non-examination of important witnesses, particularly some of the neighbours, without reasonable explanation gives rise to an adverse presumption against the prosecution— Evidence Act 5. 114(g)</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Dula Miah alias Nurul Islam and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)477</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27 DLR(AD)29; 44DLR(AD) 10, I.L.R. Indian Appeals 147; 12 DLR(SC) 156; 12DLR(SC)217; 36 DLR 185; 16 DLR 147; 45DLR 171; A.I.R. 1939 (PC) 47; 25 DLR 399; AIR 1957(SC) 107; 6BLD(AD)1—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PRISONER—DEATH SENTENCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Prisoner—Death sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 30 sub-section 1 of the Act provides that every prisoner under sentence death shall, immediately on his arrival in the prison after sentence, be searched by, or order of, the Jailer and all articles shall taken from him which the Jailer deems it dangerous or inexpedient to leave in his possession. Sub-section (2) of Section 30 of the Act further provides that every such prisoner shall be confined in a cell apart from all other prisoners, and shall be placed by day and by night under the charge of a guard.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Major (Retd.) Bazlul Huda Vs. The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 204</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PRIVILEGE OF A LAWYER</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Privilege of a lawyer</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A lawyer, while acting under the instructions of his client has a qualified professional privilege. While acting as such, the lawyer does not, if not otherwise implicated, come within the ambit of defamation unless and until there is an express malice on his part. Mere knowledge in such case can not be equated with express malice or malice in fact. The privilege enjoyed by the lawyer is not only a qualified privilege. He will not come within the bound of the offence of defamation unless and until there is an “express malice” or malice in fact on his part.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mrs. Sigma Huda @ Sigma Huda Vs. Ishfaque Samad and others, 13 BLD (HCD) 152</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1976)78 Born. L.R.234; A.I.R. 1927 Cal. 823; A.I.R. 1932 Born. 490—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Privilege of a lawyer</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If an Advocate is called in question in respect of defamatory statements made by him or her in course of his or her duties as an Advocate, the Court ought to presume that he or she acted in good faith upon instructions of the client and ought to require the party to prove the express malice.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ms. Sigma Huda @ Sigma Huda Vs. Ishfaque Samad &amp; others, 13BLD(HCD) 152</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1976)78 Born. L.R.234; A.I.R. 1927 Cal. 823; A.I.R. 1932 Born. 490—Cited</p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PROCEDURAL LAW</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Retrospective effect of procedural law</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Procedural laws are generally retrospective in nature—When a statute deals merely with procedures that do not affect the rights of the parties, it is to be held that changes in procedural laws apply to all actions, pending as well as future. In view of the insertion of Section 2B in section 339C by Act No. XVI of 1991, the cases of transfer made before 1.4. 1991, a fresh period of 270 days will be available to the transferee Court. Since 1.4.1991 a total period of 270 days only irrespective of the transfers of the case to different Courts, will be available for conclusion of trial.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Ana Miah and another, 14BLD(AD)143</em></strong></p><p><strong>Ref:</strong> 7 BLD250 ; 8 BED 340; 40 DLR (AD) 97; 19 DLR(SC) 242; 20 DLR (SC) 315—Cited</p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PROCEEDING</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Proceeding</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Proceeding before a court starts after the Magistrate takes cognizance of an offence on police report or on complaint. Before cognizance is taken there is no proceeding that may be quashed under section 561A. Quashing of a proceeding can be made even at the initial stage when facts and circumstances of a case demand it for securing the ends of justice—Cr.P.C. Ss. 4(m) and 561A</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mubashwir Ali and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)566</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Proceeding</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">During the pendency of police investigation of a cognizable case the steps taken before the Magistrate in relation to production of the accused granting of bail etc. have been held to be proceeding before a Court and the functioning of the Magistrate in his judicial capacity. Section—195(1)(b), Cr.P.C</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Serajuddowla Vs. Abdul Kader and another I3BLD (AD)94</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> AIR 1979(SC)777; AIR 1941(Bom) 294; 29 DLR(SC)256—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PROCLAMATION AND ATTACHMENT</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Proclamation and attachment—</strong>Under Section 87 Cr.P.C. a Magistrate is competent to publish a written proclamation requiring the accused to appear before him within a specified time when he has reason to believe that the wanted person has absconded or is concealing himself to avoid the execution of the warrant. The prerequisites for the proclamation are the issuance of an warrant and the abscondence or concealment of the accused.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Attachment under section 88 Cr.P.C. of property belonging to the proclaimed person can be made only after the order of’ proclamation under section 87 Cr.P.C. has been duly made.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Maulana M.A. Mannan and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)151</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PRODUCTION OF DOCUMENT</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Production of document</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 94(1) of the Code speaks of production of document or thing in the possession or in the power of the persons to whom the summons or the order is issued. The words ‘production’ and ‘possession’ clearly indicate that what is to be produced must be a tangible thing. So far as the word ‘document’ is concerned section 3(16) of the Act defines that ‘document’ shall include any matter written, expressed or described upon any substance by means of letters, figures or marks or by more than one of those which is intended to be used, or which may be used, for the purpose of recording the matter.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case the impugned order Annexure-B asked the petitioner under section 94 of the Code of Criminal Procedure and Articles 31 and 50 of the Anti-corruption Manual to provide information of the names of all the cases which he handled from 1.3.93 to 20.3.94 and the position of those cases. Such information as asked for does not constitute a document.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mohammad Imtiazur Rahman Farooqui Vs. Bureau of Anti-Corruption, 19BLD (HCD)382</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 42 DLR (HCD) 43; 49 DLR(HCD) 599—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PROPERTY</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Property</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The expression “property” occurring section 405 of the Criminal Procedure Code, should not be given a narrow construction. Blank forms of tickets are also property when the same are converted into ticket.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.H.M. Siddique Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 85</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PROSECUTION</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Prosecution for offences against the State</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 196 of the Code of Criminal Procedure provides that no Court shall take cognizance of any offence punishable under Chapter VI or IXA of the Penal Code (except section 127), or punishable under section 108A, or section 153A, or section 294A, or section 295A or section 505 of the same Code, unless upon complaint made by order of, or under authority from, the Government, or some officers empowered in the behalf by the Government.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shamsuddin Ahmed and others Vs. The State and another, 20 BLD(HCD) 268</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><div align="center"><strong>PUBLICATION OF NOTIFICATION<br/><br/></strong></div><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Publication of Notification</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In the absence of any notification in respect of the absconding accused in any news paper, the Special Judge acted illegally in proceeding with the trial in violation of the express provision of law—Cr.P.C S. 339B(1)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Jamsed Ahmed Vs. The State,14BLD(HCD)301</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>PUBLIC PROSECUTOR<em> <br/></em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Public Prosecutor</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Public Prosecutor is supposed to know the law and has a responsibility to work with utmost devotion keeping in mind that he is representing not a party but the people in the administration of criminal justice. If he fails in his duty, a sessions exception is taken by the Court.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Nurul Huq, I3BLD (HCD) 99</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 172 I.C.374; 39 Cr.L.J. 123; A.I.R. 1977 (SC)1116; 126 LC.689; 62 LC. 545; P.L.D. 1964 (SC)813; 31DLR. 312; 16 D.L.R. (Dacca) 598; A.I.R. 1 973(SC)2474—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PUBLIC SERVANTS</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Public Servants</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Public Servants shall mean and include only those persons who serve as public servants at the tise of commission of the offence and remain so when cognizance is taken. Since the petitioners were not public servants at the time of commission of the alleged offence, sanction of the Government for prosecuting them is not required—Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958 (XL of 1958), Section—6(5)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mohor Ranjan Pal and others Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)86</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 49 DLR(AD)(1997)51; 19 DLR(SC) 33; 17DLR(SC)26;29DLR( 1977)219—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Public servant</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Clause 12 of section 21 of the Penal Code provides that every person in the service or pay of the Government or remunerated by the Government by fees or commission for the performance of public duty is a public servant.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Public duty is one which is created and conferred by law by which an individual is vested with some portion of the sovereign function of the Government to be exercised by him for the performance of the duty for the term and tenure prescribed by law. There is no such law in the instant case and as such the petitioner cannot be said to perform public duty and called a public servant.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Matiur Rahman Vs. The State, 19BLD(HCD) 607</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 30DLR(SC)127; PLD 1964 Dhaka 330; A1R1957(SC)13 and A1R1918 Lahore 152—distinguishable.</p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1049	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">BLD'S 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [S]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  13BLD(HCD)344, 16BLD(AD)215, 14BLD (HCD)102,18BLD (HCD) 399, 18BLD(HCD)551, 15 BLD(HCD) 469, 17BLD (HCD) 155,14BLD(HCD)126, 14BLD(HCD)317, 15BLD(HCD) 162, 15BLD(HCD) 129, 15BLD(HCD)570, 15BLD(HCD) 486,13 BLD (HCD)483,13 BLD(HCD)430, 14BLD(HCD)94, 18BLD (</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  BLD'S 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000)</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>BLD'S 8-YEARS CRIMINAL DIGEST (1993-2000) INDEX – [S]</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SANCTION</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sanction</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sanction is necessary for prosecution of a public servant. Sanction is a mandatory provision and a prosecution not sanctioned by the sanctioning authority of an accused who is a public servant is untenable in law—Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958, S. 6(5)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Serajul Haque and another Vs. The State, 13BLD(HCD)344</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1971P.Cr.L.J(Vol.2)Page 1173—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sanction</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The proviso to section 6 (5) of the Criminal Law Amendment Act requires the Special Judge to immediately address the Government in case of sanctions not being accompanied with the petition of complaint before taking any step in the matter. In the present case the learned Special Judge failed to act with sound discretion in asking the A.S.P. to hold an enquiry when admittedly officers much above his rank were made accused in the case. Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958 (XL of 1958) Section-6 (5) Proviso</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mr. Abdur Rahim alias Md. Abdur Rahim Vs. The State, 16BLD(AD)215</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 35 DLR(AD) (1983) 324; PLD 1962 (WP) Karachi 738; PLR (1964) 14 (Dacca) Dacca Series 557; 35 DLR (1983)249; 9 DLR (Dacca)) 109; 10 DLR(Dhaka) 321; (1945) 7F.C. Reports 90 and (1945) F.C. Reports 93; 10 DLR(Dacca)370; 29Cr.L.J. 1928,958—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sanction</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sanction to prosecute is a pre-condition for initiation of a criminal proceeding against a public servant. Absence of sanction from the appropriate authority vitiates the entire proceeding and renders the conviction untenable—Prevention of Corruption Act, 1947 S. 5(2)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Ali Hossain Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)102</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sanction</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sanction of the Government for prosecution of a public servant as required under section 6 of the Act is intended to save innocent persons from unnecessary harassments by unscrupulous officers—Anti Coruption Act, 1957 Section.6</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Khair Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD) 399</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sanction</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When an offence is alleged to have been committed by a public servant not in discharge of his official duty, but as a private citizen and he is not charged under the provision of the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958, but under the Penal Code, he is not entitled to the protection under section 197 Cr.P.C. and as such no sanction is necessary for his prosecution</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abdul Awal and another Vs. The State, 18BLD(HCD)551</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sanction</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sanction of the Government for prosecution for offences committed outside Bangladesh</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the letter of sanction shows that the requirements of section 188 Cr.P.C. have been duly fulfilled and the satisfaction of the Government for prosecuting the accused is apparent, such a sanction must be held to be valid.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Taslima Nasreen Vs. The State, 15 BLD(HCD) 469</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sanction</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">For prosecuting a public servant for an offence under the Criminal Law -Amendment Act previous sanction of the Government is essential under Section 6(5) of the Act if the concerned public servant continues to be a public servant at the time of taking cognizance of the offence—Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958, S.6(5)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Fakir Md. Tariqul Islam Vs. The State and others, 17BLD (HCD) 155</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 6 DLR 152; 2 BCR 1982 (HCD) 4; 52 C.W.N. 567; A.I.R. 1939 (Sind) 148; 42 DLR (1990) 252—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SEARCH AND SEIZURE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Search and seizure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Search and seizure without strictly following the provisions of law cannot be held to be legal—Cr.P.C. Ss. 102 and 103</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Moklesur Rahman and another Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)126</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 26DLR297</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Search and Seizure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the pipe guns in question were recovered from a sugarcane field and the place was accessible to the public and the 3 seizure list witnesses did not prove the recovery of the fire arms from the possession of the accused, his exclusive possession of the fire arms becomes doubtful—Cr.P.C. 103</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Golam Mostafa (Bara) Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)317</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 21 D.L.R. 684; 44 DLR159—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Search and seizure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In conducting search for recovery of contraband phensidil syrups the conducting officer is required to comply with the provisions of section 103 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Faruque Hossain and 2 others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 162</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Search and seizure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The provisions relating to search and seizure provided in subsections (1) and (2) of section 103 Cr.P.C. are mandatory. Any search and seizure without strictly complying with the aforesaid provisions of law is illegal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Habibur Rahman alias Jane Alam Vs.The State, 15BLD(HCD) 129</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Search and Seizure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The police must follow the laws and rules relating to search and seizure. Noncompliance of the mandatory provision of section 103 Cr.P.C. and Regulation No. 280 of the Police Regulations makes search and seizure illegal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Zulfiqure Ali alias Kazal Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)570</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Search and seizure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the accused admits that he has arms in his possession and arms are actually recovered at his instance, provisions of Sections 103 and 165 Cr.P.C. are of little relevance.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kamruzzaman alias Babul Sikdar Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 486</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong><em> </em> <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SEARCH WARRANT</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Search Warrant</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 100 of Cr.P.C empowers a Magistrate to issue search warrant for the recovery of the child who has been kidnapped and wrongfully confined. A direction issued by a Magistrate to the same effect is not in confirmity with the provision of section 100 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. G.M. Enamul Hoque Vs. Mosammat Rafia Begum and another, 13 BLD (HCD)483</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Search Warrant</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The provisions of section 103 Cr.P.C. are designed to create a safeguard against possible chicanery and concoction on the part of the Investigating Officer and it is obligatory on the officer about to execute a search warrant to call on and get two or more respectable inhabitants of the locality to witness the search to ensure that the search is conducted honestly—Cr.P.C S.103</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Subodh Ranjan and others Vs. The State, 13 BLD(HCD)430</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> PLD 1979 (Lahore) 279; 26 DLR 297; PLD 1975 (SC) 607, 609—Cited</p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>  <br/></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SEIZURE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Seizure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Non—seizure of the torch light—</strong>Non- seizure by the Investigating Officer of the torch light by which the witnesses allegedly recognised the accused renders the prosecution case unacceptable</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Latif alias Budu and others Vs.The State, 14BLD(HCD)94</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Seizure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under Section 26 of the Arms Act only the Government and not the District Magistrate is empowered to seize arms of any person and detain the same for such time as it thinks necessary for the public safety.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mokbul Hossain Santu Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh and others, 18BLD (HCD) 509</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><div align="center"><strong>SENTENCE</strong></div><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The sentence of 4 years imprisonment is not very short but ordinarily the disposal of appeals take a long time in view of heavy accumulation of appeals in the High Court Division. The learned Advocate for the appellant submits that he will take necessary steps for early disposal of the appeal. In the circumstances, appeal should be allowed but with direction for early disposal of the appeal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Malek Vs. The State 13BLD (AD) 63</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Awarding sentence of fine along with sentence of imprisonment for life can be said to be illegal in view of section 409 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.M.A. Wazedul Islam Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 296</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Awarding sentence is the discretion of the Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In consideration of the fact that the appellants applied for registration of their trade mark and manufactured goods under a bonafide belief that their application would be allowed, the Appellate Division modified the sentence to the period already undergone.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Hazi Oziullah and another Vs. The State, 16BLD(AD) 59</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sentence is a complex matter which needs special considerations in the circumstances of a particular case. In the instant case there were hot altercations and exchange of hot words between the parties immediately preceding the occurrence and there was grappling by Salam and 2 others on one side and victim Jalal on the other. In the course of such quarrel and on the hit of passion the condemned prisoner gave dagger blows to the victim. Under such circumstances, it cannot be said that the condemned prisoner had premeditation for killing Jalal and as such the sentence of death is commuted to one of imprisonment for life—Penal Code, 1860(XLV of 1860) Section .302</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Abdul Aziz Mina and others, 16BLD(HCD)183</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sentence is essentially a matter of discretion of the trial Court. Where no minimum is set by law, the Court is required to exercise the discretion judicially and not fancifully.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Al-haj Nurul Islam Chowdhury and another Vs. The State, 20 BLD (HCD)168</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">For the offence under section 471 of the Penal Code the appellant could at best be punished, as provided in section 465 of the Penal Code, upto 2 years rigorous imprisonment or with fine or with both. The imposition of 4 years rigorous imprisonment under section 471 of the Penal Code is not legal and it cannot be sustained.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Hossain Mollah alias Abu Mollah Vs. The State, 17BLD(AD)170</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Reduction of sentence on compassionate grounds</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The appellants were convicted under section 467 of the Penal Code and were sentenced to suffer R.I. for 2 years and to pay a fine of Tk.5,000/- for committing forgery involving a sum of Tk. 561/- only. As the forgery was detected before payment of the bills in question the accused could not actually misappropriate the money nor could they make any wrongful gain to themselves. In view of the fact that a small amount of money was involved in the deal and in view of the further fact that the delinquent official is old man of about 70 years of age, the Appellate Division took a lenient view in the matter and reduced the substantive sentence of 2 years  R.L to the period already undergone on compassionate grounds.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Khandker Md. Shamsul Islam Vs. Mohammad Hossain, 18BLD (AD)4</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the trial Court did not impose fine, the appellate Court for the first time, in addition to a sentence of imprisonment, cannot impose a sentence of fine. So, the order of sentence of fine passed by the appellate Court in addition to the sentence of imprisonment is illegal and without jurisdiction—Muslim Family Laws Ordinance, 1961, S. 6(5)(b)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mizanur Rahman Vs. Mst. Surma Khatun, 18BLD (HCD)512</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1BLD(198 1)(HCD)165—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A sentence must not be lenient vis-a-vis the nature of the offence committed and at the same time it must not be harsh either so that the offender is sent to a point of no return turning him vindictive to the society thinking that he has been treated too harshly instead of repentant thinking.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Husain Mohammad Ershad Vs The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 446</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence in a lump</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sentence passed in a lump without specifying as to what is the sentence under each of the sections i.e; 448/380 of the Penal Code is only an irregularity and not an illegality and it does not affect the competence of the learned Magistrate to pass the order of conviction and sentence.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Haider Ali Khan Vs. The State, 14BLD (AD)270</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Leniency in the matter of Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">While setting aside the order of acquittal passed by the High Court Division, the Appellate Division took a lenient view in the matter of sentence and reduced it to the period already undergone on the ground that the Respondent faced two trials and he had to take two appeals until he was acquitted by the impugned judgment.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Abdid Muttaleb Khan, 14BLD(AD)12</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Reduction of sentence on compassionate ground</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the age of appellant No. I is recorded to be 90 years in his statement under section 342 Cr.P.C. and there is no material to hold to the contrary, his sentence of R.I. for one year is reduced to the period undergone.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Hasan Ali and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD) 37</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SMUGGLING</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Smuggling</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To prove a charge of smuggling it is essential to establish that the goods in question were brought into Bangladesh in violation of any prohibition or restriction imposed by or under any law or by evading customs duties or other taxes. In the absence of any special mark of identification to brand the cattle heads as Indian and similar cattle-heads being available in the local market, the appellants cannot be held liable for smuggling—Special Powers Act, 1974; 5. 25B</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kabil Mia and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)432</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Smuggling</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The statement in writing made by the accused before respectable persons who are not Police Officers admitting therein the bringing into Bangladesh of a huge quantity of gold without any valid authority though may not be confession proper, but it clearly amounts to an admission by the accused of his misdeed under Section 17 of the Evidence Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Shafi Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)322</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 10 DLR(WP)55; 45DLR(AD)1 13— Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Smuggling</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Smuggling is punishable under section 156(8) of the Customs Act and subsequently it has been made punishable under section 25B of the Special Powers Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Shafi Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)322</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 10 DLR (WP) 55; 45 DLR (AD) 113—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>STATE—DEFENCE</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>State Defence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Appointment and cancellation of State defence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">After the District Magistrate appoints a defence lawyer to defend an undefended accused and the Court accepts it and allows the defence lawyer so appointed to act for the accused on a number of occasions, abrupt cancellation of such appointment by the Court without assigning any reason is illegal and arbitrary.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurun Nahar Zaman Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD) 537</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>STATEMENT OF A DEAD PERSON</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Statement of a dead person</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Statement, written or verbal of relevant facts, made by a person is relevant when the statement is made by a person as to the cause of his death in cases in which the cause of that person’ s death comes into question—Evidence Act, 1872 S.32(1)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Abdul Khaleque, 13 BLD (HCD) 354</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Ref: PLD. 1956 (FC)43=8 DLR (FC) 24; BCR 1982 (Special Bench )344; 12 BLD 49; 40 DLR(AD)281; 21DLR(SC)89=PLD 1969 SC 89; (1939)56 Indian Appeals 66 (76) ; 27 DLR (AD) 29—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>STATEMENT OF VICTIM</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Value of the statement of a victim of abduction</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the school certificate and the doctors opinion show that the victim is a minor girl below the age of 18 years, prima facie there is no reason to disbelieve the mothers statement that she is a minor and she is a victim of abduction. In a case like this, it is difficult to appreciate how can a wards statement be preferred to that of his or her parents on the question of his or her age.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Khairunnessa Vs. lily Begum and another, 16BLD(AD)124</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Value of the statement of the victim on her age</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the school certificate and the doctor’s opinion show that the victim is a minor girl below the age of 18 years, there is no reason prima facie to disbelieve the mother’s statement that she is a minor and she is a victim of abduction or kidnapping. In such a case, to give credit to the girl for her statement is to assume that she must have been born before her mother. It will have to be assumed further that the mother is an enemy of her daughter and the mother was falsely claiming her to be a minor and further that the welfare of the victim girl would be safe and better in her own hands than in the hands of her parents. In a case like this, it is difficult to appreciate how can a ward’s statement be preferred to that of his or her parents when the question is about his or her age.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Khairunnessa Vs. Illy Begum and another, 16BLD(AD) 124</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>STATEMENT OF WITNESSES BEFORE POLICE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Statement of witnesses before police</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Inordinate delay in recording statement of a witness u/s 161 Cr.P.C.—the weight of the evidence of such witness is diminished.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Haji Md. Jamaluddin &amp; others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)33</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Statement before Police</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Omission of vital fact by the witnesses recorded by the investigating officer that he saw the condemned prisoner and his wife in the night of occurrence of going inside the hut and that they slept inside the hut in the night preceding the morning of which condemned prisoner’s wife was found dead is unreliable.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The witnesses having not stated at the earliest point of time, the said evidence cannot be relied upon in Court.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Azizur Rahman alias Habib, 20 BLD (HCD)467</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Statement of witnesses before police</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Inordinate delay in recording the statement of witnesses by the I.O. under section 161 Cr.P.C. renders their evidence shaky.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Zafar and others vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)280</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 33 DLR 320; 11 D.L.R. (HC) 365; 27 D.L.R. (SC) 1; 38 DLR 289; 41 DLR 11; 50 Cr.L. 3. 569</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>STOLEN PROPERTY</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dishonestly retaining or receiving stolen property</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In order to sustain a conviction under section 411 of the Code the prosecution must prove affirmatively by reliable evidence that the accused had exclusive possession and effective control or domain over the stolen property or he received or retained the same knowing or having reason to believe it to be a stolen property.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Afsar Ali Pramanik Vs The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 356</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SUMMARY TRIAL</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Summary trial</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Where the evidence of all witnesses was recorded in full together with their full cross- examination, it could not be taken to mean that the summary procedure of trial was actually adopted—Special Powers Act, 1972; S. 27(4)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Humayun Kabir Vs. The State, 13 BLD (HCD) 406</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 45 C.W.N. 139—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SUO MOTU</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Suo motu rule by the High Court Division</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Under Section 439 of the Cr.P.C, the High Court Division may suo motu call for the record of the Courts subordinate to it and set aside any order passed by such Courts in any legal proceeding which has caused miscarriage of justice.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Reazuddin Ahmed Vs. The State and another, 17BLD (AD) 123</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Suo motu Rule</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A suo motu Rule was issued by the High Court Division under section 476 of the Code upon delinquent Abdul Majid to show cause as to why an inquiry should not be made to ascertain as to whether he gave false evidence as PW 2 before a Bar Council Tribunal in a complaint case and thereby committed an offence punishable under section 193 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Hamid Advocate Vs. Bangladesh Bar Council and others, 17BLD(HCD) 547.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1050	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">CHILDREN ACT, 1974</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  15BLD(AD) 163, 15BLD (HCD)364, 14BLD(HCD) 266, 17BLD (HCD)243, 15BLD(HCD)21, 18BLD(HCD)407, 19BLD (HCD) 542, 15BLD(AD)184, 14BLD(AD) 218, 14 BLD (HCD)381. </p><p><b>Subject:</b>  CHILDREN </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CHILDREN ACT, 1974</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>(XXXIX OF 1974)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—2(f)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Child Witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A child witness who is himself a victim of assaults by the assassins of his father and saw the accused persons killing him and he testifies in details before the Court about the occurrence and remains unshaken in the face of cross-examination is a competent witness and he can be safely relied upon.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Forkan alias Farhad and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD) 163</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—2(f)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Child witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The law requires that before examining a child of tender age as a witness the Court should satisfy itself that the child is intellectually developed enough to comprehend what he has seen and to give an intelligent account of it to the Court. If the Court finds otherwise, it should decline to examine him as a witness. On the other hand, if the child is sufficiently intelligent to understand the questions put to him and he is capable of giving rational answers to those questions, then his capacity to give evidence is on the same footing as that of any other adult witness.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Fazlul Haq Sikder Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD)364</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—2(f), 6 and 8</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 2(f) provides that a boy under the age of 16 is a child. Section 6 provides that there can be no joint trial of the child and the adult and as such no child is to be charged with or tried for any offence together with an adult. The child must be tried separately in the Juvenile Court and not in the ordinary Court. Only the Junveile Court is competent to take cognizance against the juvenile offenders.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Deputy Commissioner, Satkhira, and others, 14BLD(HCD) 266</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1980 A.I.R (SC) 1579 and 1986 A.I.R. (SC) 1773—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—2(f), 6 and 66</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The trial Court failed to apply its judicial mind to find if appellant Shiplu, who appears to below the age of 16 years at the time of trial is a child. This makes the order of conviction and sentence passed by the trial Court upon appellant Shiplu illegal The impugned judgment and order are set aside for want of jurisdiction.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Shiplu and another Vs The State, 17BLD (HCD)243</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 3 BLD (HCD) (1983)193—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—2(f) and 6(1)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 2(f) a ‘Child’ means a person under the age of 16 years and when used with reference to a child sent to a certified institute or approved home or committed by a Court to the custody of a relative or other fit person means that he is a child during the whole period of his detention notwithstanding that he may have attained the age of 16 years during that period.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 6(1) of the Act provides that no child shall be charged with, or tried for, any offence together with an adult. In the instant case the trial of the minor accused appellant Kawsarun-nessa together with adult Bahera Khatun must be held to be illegal. Such a trial is without jurisdiction and as such the impugned order of conviction cannot be sustained.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Kawsarun Nessa and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)21</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—2(1) and 66</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the finding by the learned Additional Sessions Judge as the trial Court that tie petitioners are not children within the meaning of section 2 sub-section (1) of the Children Act, the High Court Division rejected the defence plea that the petitioners are children and they cannot be tried by the ordinary criminal Court.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mohammad Hossain &amp; ors. Vs. The State &amp; anT. 18BLD(HCD)407</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 47DLR(AD) 96—relied</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—5</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The appellant is aged about 14 years and as such the trial of the appellant must be held by the Juvenile Court according to section 5 of the Act and not by any other Court. The trial held by Special Tribunal in respect of the appellant is without jurisdiction.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md Shamim Vs The State, 19BLD</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>(HCD)542</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 17BLD(1997)(HCD) 243:49 DLR53—relied upon</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—49(1)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The appellant has been in jail hajat since 1.6.1998 in association with other adult criminals in contravention of section 49(1) of the Act and in such view of the matter the prayer for bail deserves consideration.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md Shamim Vs The State, 19BLD (HCD) 542</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—66</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Presumption and determination of age</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under Section 66 of the Children Act it is for the Court to consider whether a person charged with an offence and brought before it for trial appears to be a child or not and then to proceed accordingly.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case the learned Additional Sessions Judge observed that the age of the petitioner could not be less then 16 years. Hç also noticed that there was sign of interpolation in the registraiion card and the certificate issued from the school. The learned Sessions Judge did not rely on those documents. From the materials on record the learned Additional Sessions Judge found it difficult to hold the petitioner to be a child on the date of framing of charges. The learned trial Court committed no illegality.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abdul Munem Chowdhury alias Momen Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD)184</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—66(1)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provide that when it appears to the Court that a person charged with an offence is a child, the Court is required to direct an enquiry to ascertain the age of the accused. When an accused is above 16 years of age at the time of framing of the charge he is not entitled to get the benefit of the Children Act</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Bimal Das Vs. The State 14BLD(AD) 218</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—66(1)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provides that when an accused is brought before any criminal court for facing trial and it appears that he is a child, the Court shall make an enquiry as to his age by taking such evidence as may be forthcoming and shall record a finding thereon stating his age as nearly as possible. When such a plea is taken by the accused, a duty is cast upon a court to determine the age of the accused by holding an enquiry.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Baktiadr Hossain Vs. The State, 14 BLD (HCD)381.</em></strong></p><p><strong><em><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span><br clear="all" style="page-break-before: always;"/></em></strong></p></div></div>
1051	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">CODE OF CRIMINAL PROCEDURE, 1898 [SECTION 1 - 170]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  18BLD (HCD) 61, 14BLD (HCD)204, 16 BLD(AD)206, 17 BLD(AD)284, 18BLD(HCD)61, 14BLD(HCD)566, 7BLD(AD)177, 15BLD(AD)l08,19BLD(HCD)291,20BLD(HCD )177,17BLD(HCD)15, 15BLD(HCD) 151,16BLD (AD)220, 18BLD(HCD)167, 19BLD (HCD) 382,13BLD(HCD)45,18BLDC4D.)210, 13BLD</p><p><b>Subject:</b>   CRIMINAL PROCEDURE</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CODE OF CRIMINAL PROCEDURE, 1898</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Schedule II, Colum, 4</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Column 4 of the Schedule II of the Code relating to the offences against other laws provides that warrant of arrest shall be issued against the accused persons if the offence is punishable with imprisonment for not less than two years and not more than five years. Since the offence in question is punishable with imprisonment upto 5 years there is no illegality in passing an order of issuance of warrant of arrest against the accused-persons.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Shainpukur Holding Ltd. Vs Security Exchange Commission, 18BLD (HCD) 61</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—1</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provides the procedure for trial of criminal cases in Bangladesh and lays down that the Code of Criminal Procedure extends only to Bangladesh.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abdul Haque Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)204</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—3(h)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Complaint—Complaint by an Attorney</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is a settled principle of criminal law that any person having knowledge of any criminal offence may set the law in motion by making a complaint to the appropriate authority even though he may not be personally injured or affected by the commission of the offence.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Where there is a General Power of Attorney authorising the power-of-attorney-holder to sign the complaint and appear and act on behalf of the grantor in all Courts, the attorney is entitled to do every necessary thing in that connection on behalf of the grantor. In the instant case the signing of the petition of complaint by and examination under section 200 Cr.P.C. of Anisul Hoque on behalf of his father Shamsul Haque were thus perfectly valid and proper.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Tamizul Haque Vs. Anisul Haque, 16 BLD(AD)206</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—4</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Information of certain offence obligatory</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 44 of the Code of Criminal Procedure imposes a legal duty upon every person aware of the commission or of the intention of any other person to commit any of the offences specified therein to give information thereof forthwith to the nearest Magistrate or Police Officer. Such provisions have been made so that the perpetrators of specified crimes are brought to book and are not suppressed by the persons knowing about them.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Shafique Miah Vs. The State, 17 BLD(AD)284</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—4(h)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>‘Complaint’</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The word ‘report’ in section 25 of the Securities and Exchange Commission Ordinance instead of the word ‘complaint’ signifies that cognizance of an offence under the Ordinance can be taken only on the basis of a ‘complaint’ as defined in section 4(h) of the Code. In the instant case cognizance was taken on the basis of extracts of a report submitted by an enquiry committee and not on the basis of any report submitted by the Executive Director who merely presented the extracts of that report before the C.M.M.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Shainpukur Holding Limited Vs. Security Exchange Commission, 18BLD(HCD)61</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—4(m) and 561A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>‘Judicial Proceeding</strong>—Proceeding ‘before a Court starts after the Magistrate takes cognizance of an offence on police report or on complaint. Before such cognizance there is no proceeding that may be quashed under section 561A Cr. P. C.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Quashing of a proceeding can be made even at the initial stage just upon taking of cognizance by the Magistrate when facts and circumstances of a case justify it.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mubashwir Ali and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)566</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—9(1)(2)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Court of Sessions</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 9(1) of the Code gives the Government power to establish a Court of Sessions for every Sessions Division and appoint a Judge of such Court. This Judge is none else than the Sessions Judge which is an office. Section 9(2) of the Code gives the Government the power to direct at what place or places the Court of Sessions shall hold its sitting. Therefore the power of the Government is pervasive over: (1) the establishment of a Court of Sessions, (2) appointment of a Judge of a Court of Sessions and (3) determination of the place or places where the Court of Sessions shall hold its sitting. This is a flexible power and cannot be restricted to particular situations.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Sayeed Farook Rahman Vs The Sessions Judge of the Court of Sessions, Dhaka and others, I 7BLD(AD)177</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—9(2)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If there is a special order to try a particular case at a particular place the original place of sitting continues to remain the place of sitting of the Court of Sessions and the new place indicated in the special order is meant for trial of oniy that case or class of cases which the special order specifically provides.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Sayeed Farook Rahman Vs The Sessions Judge of the Court of Sessions, Dhaka and others, 17BLD(AD)177</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> AIR 1988 (SC) 1838 Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—18(2)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Appointment of Additional Chief Metropolitan Magistrates</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When Government allocated function under sub-section (7A) of section 167 Cr.P.C. to the Additional Chief Metropolitan Magistrate and he passed the impugned order of revival, such an order cannot be held to be illegal.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Government of Bangladesh Vs. Shah Alam, 15BLD(AD)l08</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—21</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From a perusal of the provisions of section 21 of the Code of Criminal Procedure it appears that the functions performed by C.M.M. Dhaka, are mainly judicial functions in addition to some administrative works, which are incidental to the post and therefore the post of C.M.M. Dhaka is essentially meant, for performing judicial functions.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Idrisur Rahman Vs Md. Shahiduddin Ahmed and others, 19BLD(HCD)291</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—35A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The proviso of the section provides that deduction from the sentence cannot be allowed when minimum period of sentence as provided in the law.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Habibur Rahman alias Raju Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD )177</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—61 and 167</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From a combined reading of sections 61 and 167 of the Code it is clear that an accused cannot be detained in the custody of the police for a longer period than under the circumstances of the case is found reasonable and such period must not, without an order of remand by a Magistrate, exceed 24 hours, exclusive of the time necessary for journey from the place of arrest to the Court of the Magistrate. In passing an order of detention touching the liberty of a person, the Magistrate should apply his judicial mind into the facts and circumstances of the case in determining whether circumstances of the case really call for a detention in police custody.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Faruk Mahajan and others Vs The State, 17BLD(HCD)15</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—87 and 88</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Proclamation for an absconding person and attachment of his property</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 87 the Magistrate is competent to publish a written proclamation requiring the accused to appear before him in connection with a case within a specified time when he has reason to believe that the person against whom a warrant has been issued by him has absconded or is concealing himself so that the warrant cannot be executed. The prerequisites for the publication of a proclamation are the issuance of an warrant and absconsion or concealment of the accused to evade the execution of the warrant.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Attachment under section 88 Cr. P. C. of the property, moveable or immovable, or both, belonging to the proclaimed person can be made only after the order of proclamation issued under section 87 of the Code has been duly made. In the absence of any material for invocation of the provisions of sections 87 or 88 Cr.P.C. the order of proclamation and attachment is illegal.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Maulana M. A. Manna and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 151</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—94</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Summons to produce document or other thing</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is not necessary for the purpose of enquiry into a complaint that a formal case upon lodging an F.I.R. will have to be filed before a police officer or an Anti-Corruption Officer, who can apply for permission to the Sessions Judge for production and seizure of any document. But before according permission, the Sessions Judge is to satisfy himself that the Police Officer or Anti Corruption Officer is investigating on an alleged crime and is not merely fishing for information to harass, intimidate or coerce innocent persons. In the present case, there being a specific information in respect of alleged defalcation of a huge amount of money by several importers in collusion with Bank officials and in the face of reference that Bank’s own investigation has revealed that there has been a defalcation, the impugned order of the Sessions Judge directing the Bank to deposit to the Bureau of AntiCorruption, Chittagong all connected records regarding the removal of goods imported on obtaining loan from the said Bank, which were pledged with the Bank, calls for no interference.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Messrs Hamidia Oil Mills Vs. District Anti Corruption Officer, Chittagong, 16BLD (AD)220</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—94</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It authorises the Court or any competent police officer to ask a person, in whose possession or power certain document or thing is believed to be, which is necessary for the purpose of any investigation, enquiry, trial or other leaI proceedings, requiring such person to produce it at the time and place mentioned in the summons or order. But there is no provision for seizure of such document for facilitating an investigation when no such case is pending before any Court. The learned Sessions Judge committed an illegality in passing the impugned order according permission to the police to seize records from the Bank’s custody without finding a prima facie case.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Arab Bangladesh Bank Ltd. Vs. Md. Shahiduzzaman, Deputy Director (Ta: Ka:4), Bureau of Anti-Corruption, Dhaka and others, 18BLD(HCD)167</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—94</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>General Clauses Act, 1897</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—3(16)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Anti-corruption Manual</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Articles—31 and 50</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 94(1) of the Code speaks of production of document or thing in the possession or in the power of the persons to whom the summons or the order is issued. The words ‘production’ and ‘possession’ clearly indicate that what is to be produced must be a tangible thing. So far as the word ‘document’ is concerned section 3(16) of the Act defines that ‘document’ shall include any matter written, expressed or described upon any substance by means of letters, figures or marks or by more than one of those which is intended to be used, or which may be used, for the purpose of recording the matter.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case the impugned order Annexure-B asked the petitioner under section 94 of the Code of Criminal Procedure and Articles 31 and 50 of the Anti-corruption Manual to provide information of the names of all the cases which he handled from 1.3.93 to 20.3.94 and the position of those cases. Such information as asked for does not constitute a document.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mohammad Imtiazur Rahman Farooqui Vs. Bureau of Anti-Corruption, 19BLD (HCD) 382</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 42DLR(HCD)43; 49DLR (HCD) 599—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—94(1)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>General Clauses Act, 1897 (X of 1897)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—3(16)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Anti-Corruption Manual</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Articles—31 and 50</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 94(1) of the Code speaks of production of document or thing in the possession or in the power of the persons to whom the summons or the order is issued. The words ‘production’ and ‘possession’ clearly indicate that what is to be produced must be a tangible thing. So far as the word ‘document’ is concerned section 3(16) of the Act defines that ‘document’ shall include any matter written, expressed or described upon any substance by means of letters, figures or marks or by more than one of those which is intended to be used, or which may be used, for the purpose of recording the matter.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case the impugned order Annexure-B asked the petitioner under section 94 of the Code of Criminal Procedure and Articles 31 and 50 of the Anti-Corruption Manual to provide information of the names of all the cases which he handled from 1.3.93 to 20.3.94 and the position of those cases. Such information as asked for does not constitute a document.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mohammad Imtiazur Rahman Farooqui (M.I. Farooqui) Vs.
