Abdul Kader (1993) 13BLD(AD)94</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A.I.R 1979 (SC) 777; A.I.R. 1941 (Bom)294; 29 DLR(SC) 256—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—196</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Prosecution for offences against the State</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 196 of the Code provides that no Court shall take cognizance of any offence punishable under Chapter VI or IXA of the Penal Code (except section 127), or punishable under section 108A, or section 153A, or section 294A, or section 295A or section 505 of the same Code, unless upon complaint made by order of, or under authority from, the Government, or some officers empowered in this behalf by the Government.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case it appears that the Officer-in-Charge of Motijheel P.S. who lodged the complaint is an authorised person under section 196 Cr.P.C. So it does not come to rescue the publisher petitioners.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Shamsuddin Ahmed and others Vs The State and another, 20BLD(HCD)268</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—200</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>The purpose of examination under the sec</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Held : </strong>The purpose of examination of the complainant under the section is to see whether there is sufficient ground for proceeding and not to see whether there is sufficient ground for conviction. Whether the evidence is adequate for supporting a conviction can only be determined at the trial and not at the stage of enquiry.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>S.A. Sultan Vs. The State and another, 14BLD(AD)36</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—200</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 200 of the Code does not provide for recording reasons for taking cognizance but section 203 of .the Code provides for recording reasons for dismissing a complaint. From the order dated 2.4.1997 passed by the C.M.M. no reasons were recorded for taking the cognizance. Since there is no requirement of law to record reasons for taking cognizance, no illegality can be found in those orders on that count.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Shainpukur Holding Limited Vs. Security Exchange Commission, 18BLD(HCD)61</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—200</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Cognizance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Examination of the complainant under section 200 Cr.P.C. is essential when a complaint is filed before the Magistrate. A ‘naraji petition is regarded as a fresh complaint and as such the examination of the complainant under Section 200 Cr.P.C. is also necessary.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abu Bakar and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)283</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR 227; 36 DLR(AD)58; 19 DLR(SC) 426; 31 DLR (AD) 69; 38 DLR (AD) 18, 8BLD 438; 1968 Pakistan Criminal Law Journal, 1867—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—200 and 205D</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Examination of the complainant under section 200 Cr.P.C. is essential before sending the case even for judicial enquiry. Noncompliance with this requirement of law can not be sustained</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Police case and complaint case when there is a complaint case and a police case over the same occurrence, the complaint case should be tried first and the police case will remain pending till the disposal of the complaint case.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abut Kashem and others Vs. Sanjida Be- gum and another, 14BLD(HCD)383</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR 227; 37 DLR 335; 28 DLR 359; 18 DLR (SC) 474—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—203</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a Magistrate dismisses a case under section 203 Cr.P.C. and stops further enquiry into the same he obviously passes a judicial order. Unless that order is got rid of, the DAB, in violation of the said order cannot continue the same inquiry and proceed with the case for eventual submission of charge- sheet.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Md. Raihan Ali Khandker, 17BLD(AD)232</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—203, 205(1), 436 and 561A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sessions Judge cannot direct the Magistrate to take cognizance of a case. The power of Sessions Judge is strictly limited to directing a further enquiry into the petition of complaint. It will be for the Magistrate concerned to take or not to take cognizance after there- suit of the further enquiry.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A. Rouf and others Vs The State and another, 20BLD(HCD)162</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—203,436, 439A and 561A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Neither the Sessions Judge nor the High Court Division is invested with any power to direct the Magistrate to take cognizance of a case. Their power is strictly limited to directing further enquiry into the petition of complaint. It will be for the Magistrate concerned to take or not to take cognizance after holding further enquiry.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The order of the High Court Division in directing the Chief Metropolitan Magistrate to take cognizance of the offence and to issue process in accordance with section 205(1) Cr.P.C. is not sustainable in law.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Yusuf A. Hassan Vs. K.M. Rezaul Firdous, 16BLD(AD)55</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 35DLR140; 36 DLR (AD)58—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—203, 204 (3) and 436</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An order dismissing a complaint under sections 203 and 204(3) of the Code does not amount to a discharge of the accused. Therefore, no notice to the accused is necessary in such a case for holding further enquiry u/s 436 Cr,P.C. An accused can be discharged only when a charge has been framed and such a discharge could be made under sections 209,253 or 259 Cr.P.C. In such a case a notice upon the accused persons would be necessary for holding further enquiry. An order of discharge can now be made under the amended provision of Section 241A Cr.P.C.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Sirajuddowla and others Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD)607</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—204(1A)(1B)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sub-section (1A) of section 204 o the Code provides ‘no summons or warrant’ should be issued against the accused under sub-section (1) until a list of prosecution witnesses has been filed’. Sub-section (IB) of section 204 of the Code provides: ‘in a proceeding instituted upon a complaint made in writing, every summons or warrant issued under sub-section (1) shall be accompanied by a copy of such complaint’.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case admittedly no list of witnesses nor copy of the complaint (report) was filed before the C.M.M. on 2.4.1997. The High Court Division also noticed from the records of the C.M.M. Court that, in fact, processes of warrant of arrest were not issued at all against the accused persons. So there was no violation of the aforesaid provisions in these cases.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Shainpukur Holding Limited Vs. Security Exchange Commission, 18BLD(HCD)61</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—221</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Charge and its object</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A charge is a formal accusation formed against the accused persons facing trial before a court.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The object of framing charge is to enable the accused persons to know the substantive allegations they will have to meet at the trial. When evidence is admitted on a distinct criminal offence beyond the charge framed, there is no way but to infer that the accused persons have been misled and prejudiced in their defence, because the accused understood a charge which was different from the one they were required to meet at the trial.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kamala and others Vs. The State, 15 BLD (HCD)449</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—221 and 222</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When any section of any law is quoted at the time of framing of charge, the section must be quoted in the language in which the law is framed.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Habibur Rahman alias Raju Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)177 </em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—222</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Particulars of a charge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Law requires that a charge must contain particulars as to the time and place of the alleged offence, which should reasonably be sufficient to give the accused notice of the matter he is charged with.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The time included between the first and last dates of the commission of the offence of criminal breach of trust or dishonest misappropriation of money cannot, however, exceed one year. In the instant case no date or dates of the commission of the alleged offence having been mentioned in the charge, the requirements of section 222 of the Code can not be said to have been complied with.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Nizamuddin Dhali Vs. The State 16BLD(HCD)580</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—222(2)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Proviso</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Proviso to section 222(2) of the Code enjoins that the first and last date of the charge of criminal breach of trust or dishonest misappropriation of money shall not exceed one year.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case, charge was framed for misappropriation of money between 10.11.1979 and 25.11.1982 that is for a period of over 2 years. Evidently, the trial was held by adopting a mode which is prohibited by law and as such the same is illegal, not curable under section 537 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Abdul Ban Molla Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD)223</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 7 BLD 413; 39 DLR 184; 45 DLR 352—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—227</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Court may at any time alter or amend any charge during the trial in accordance with section 227 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Hussain Mohammad Ershad Vs.The State, 14BLD(AD)161</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Sections—227 and 231</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 227 of the Code of Criminal Procedure the Court may alter or add any charge at any time before judgment is pronounced. Every such alteration or addition shall be read and explained to the accused.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 231 of the Code provides that whenever a charge is altered or added to by the Court after the commencement of the trial, the prosecutor and the accused shall be allowed to re-call or re-summon and examine with reference to such alteration or addition any witness who may have been examined and also to call any further witness whom the Court may think to be material.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abu Bakkar alias Bakkar Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)217</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—229B(2)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>“Absonding and absconder” and “failure to appear”</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An “absconder” may be said to be one who hides conceals or makes himself inaccessible or does not come in public in order to defeat the process of the Court or his arrest. But an absentee accused should not be readily assumed to be an absconder without due inquiry and notice.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Failure to appear’ as contemplated by section 339B(2) Cr.P.C. means default or neglect on the part of the accused to appear in Court. So, if an accused is prevented by any genuine or reasonable cause from appearing in Court, that by itself does not amount to neglect or default and for that matter a failure to appear in Court.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—236 and 237</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">An offence under a particular section if not proved but some other offence is made out by the prosecution. the accused can very well be convicted and sentenced for the other offences proved before the Court through legal evidence and the same is permissible in view of the provisions of sections 236 and 237 of the Code.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Al-Amim Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD)307</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—241A/265C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The prosecution case as set out in the petition of complaint has got prime facie ingredients of the offence alleged. The exact nature of the offence against the accused petitioners can only be thrashed out at the trial.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Gazi Mozibul Huq and others Vs Abid Hossain Babu, 19BLD(AD)265</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—241A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Discharge of an accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">With the discharge of an accused person the proceeding against him comes to an end and the Magistrate has no power to revive the said proceeding against him, although a second prosecution may be competent on a fresh complaint or a fresh police report in respect of the same offence. In the case of an order of discharge passed not on merit, a fresh complaint or a fresh police report may furnish the basis of a second prosecution in respect of the same offence.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Maulana M. A. Manna and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 151</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—241A and 265C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When accused shall be discharged— Section 241A Cr.P.C. provides that when the accused appears or is brought before the Magistrate, and if the Magistrate, upon consideration of the records of the case and the documents submitted therewith and after giving the prosecution and the accused an opportunity of being heard, considers the charge to be groundless, he shall discharge the accused on recording his reasons for so doing.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 265C Cr.P.C. provides that in a case trial before a Court of Session if upon consideration of records of the case and the documents submitted therewith, and after hearing the submissions of the accused and the prosecution in this behalf, the Court considers that there is no sufficient ground for proceeding against the accused, it shall discharge the accused on recording the reasons for so doing.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Iftekharul Alam Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD) 188</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—24lA and 561A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 241A of the Code the Magistrate is to consider only the documents submitted with the case record. At this stage the Magistrate will consider only the documents of the prosecution and not of the defence. Documents of the accused may form part of the record after charge is framed and trial begins.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The learned Additional Sessions Judge while exercising revisional jurisdiction was wrong in finding the charge groundless on consideration of the documents filed before him for the first time in support of the defence case, although these were not filed before the learned Magistrate before framing the charge. The order of discharge passed by learned Additional Sessions Judge is thus illegal and the same is quashed.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mahbttha Akter and others Vs. Md. Mozammel Hoque and others, 15BLD(HCD)339</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—241A, 265C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The learned Special Judge discharged the accused persons considering the some extraneous materials which were not available either in the complaint petition or in the record and as such the Special Judge acted beyond his jurisdiction be that it under section 241A or 265C of the Code discharging the accused by illegally relying upon some extraneous materials which were not in the record</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abid Hossain Babu Vs Gazi Mojibul Haq and or, 20BLD(HCD)72</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—241A, 265C and 561A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The usual and well-settled practice is that a criminal proceeding can only be quashed after cognizance has been taken and process issued thereupon to prevent the abuse of the process of the Court but there may be a rare case where the High Court Division may be justified in interfering even at the initial stage before taking of cognizance. The view expressed by the High Court Division that an application under section 561A Cr.P.C. will be premature unless the accused petitioner had gone through the stages under section 241A or 265C of the Code is not consistent with the principles of law already settled.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Syed Mohaminad Hashem alias Hashim Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD) 115</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A.I.R (32)1945 P.C18; 1984BLD (AD) 97:1984(36)DLR(AD)14; 28DLR(AD) 39—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—247 and 403</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The language of section 247 of the Code having clearly empowered the concerned Magistrate to acquit the accused for the failure of the complainant to appear in the case on the date fixed for the appearance of the accused, it cannot be said that only the order of acquittal passed upon holding full trial can create a bar under section 403 of the Code from entertaining a second complaint on the self same allegation. So long as the order of acquittal, passed under section 247 Cr.P.C. remains in force the provision of section 403 Cr.P.C. shall stand on the way of entertaining a second complaint on the self-same allegations.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Dewan Obaidur Rahman Vs The State and another, 19BLD(AD)128</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—247 and 403</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Acquittal</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Acquittal of an accused on the default of the prosecution under section 247 Cr.P.C. is not an acquittal within the meaning of section 403 Cr.P.C. and it cannot bar a fresh prosecution.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Godadhar Nainadas alias Gadahar Mondal Vs. Most. Joytun Akhtar and others, 15BLD(HCD) 377</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1918) 19 Cr1. L.J. 497—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—247</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Acquittal of an accused on the default of the prosecution under section 247 Cr.P.C. does not entitle the person acquitted to plead autrefois acquit on a fresh prosecution on the same facts, it being not an acquittal within the meaning of section 403 Cr.P.C. Such an acquittal does not operate as a bar against a fresh prosecution.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Gadadhar Namadas alias Gadadhar Mondal Vs. Most. Joviun Akhtar and other, 15 BLD (HCD)377</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1918) 19 Cr1. L.J. 497—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—249/339C, 265/241A and 403</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Release on bail—265/241A—”acquitted” and convicted’—person once convicted or acquitted not to be tried for the same offence—double jeopardy—terminologies such as ‘release’. released on bail’ discharged’, ‘acquitted’ and ‘convicted’—whether bear separate meanings with separate consequences and denote different connotations?</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Held :</strong> Reading the Code as a whole it appears that in relation to an accused- petitioner, several terminologies have been used in the Code denoting different connotations. Each of the words has separate meanings and each word is attended with different consequences. When a proceeding is stopped either under section 249 or 339C of the Code, without pronouncing any judgment either of acquittal or of conviction and the accused is released, it does not operate either as a discharge or as acquittal of the accused, so as to attract in the latter case the protection of section 403 of the Code barring trial for the same offence once conviced or acquitted. Stoppage of a proceeding and release of the accused stand short of a discharge. It is half-way to discharge, but not discharge proper. It is certainly not an acquittal. The proceeding is not brought to a close. It does not fade into a past and closed transaction. The use of the words “further proceedings in respect of the trial shall stand stopped and the accused person released” in fact mean that the proceeding in question has come to a stop and the same proceeding can only be revived if the legislature gives sanction to it, but the words quoted do not mean that fresh proceeding cannot be started on the same allegations. A ‘fresh proceeding’ and ‘further proceedings in respect of the trial’ do not mean the same thing. The legal effect of the above quoted words in section 339C and the provisions of section 249 remain and the same proceeding can be revived by a legislative mandate or in the alternative a fresh proceeding can be instituted on the same allegations.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is no legal bar for instituting a fresh prosecution on the self-same facts after a proceeding is stopped and accused released under Section 339C(4)</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Niamat Ali Sheikh and ors Vs Begum Enayetur Noor &amp; others 13BLD(AD)11</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1981) 33DLR (HCD)205 distingushed—Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—265A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A public prosecutor has the right to conduct every trial before a Court of Sessions. In such a case, the role of an Investigation Officer is that of a witness. If any application is to be filed before the Court, it has to he filed through the Public Prosecutor. In the instant case, the learned Sessions Judge was manifestly wrong in entertaining a petition submitted directly by an Assistant Superintendent of Police and in passing order thereon. The learned Sessions Judge ought to have been vigilant in guarding the legal sanctity and independence of the Courts.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Rahmat Ulllah Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD)357</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—265C and 439</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In exercising revisional power U/s 439 Cr.P.C. the High Court Division generally does not go into questions of fact. But this Court having paternal and supervisory jurisdiction over the subordinate Courts can certainly scrutinise facts whether in determining any question of fact onus is wrongly placed on a party or an incorrect principle is applied in determining the question of fact or any material piece of evidence is ignored by the Court below due to misconception of law. Under these circumstances the High Court Division examines the legality or propriety of any order passed by a Subordinate Court for rectification of an impending injustice.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under Section 265C Cr.P.C. it is the duty of the Court of Sessions, upon consideration of the materials on record and after hearing the parties, to discharge those accused persons against whom it appears to the Court that there is no ground for proceeding so that frivolous cases and cases of no evidence do hot occupy the time of the Court and innocent persons are subjected to the rigours and expenses of a full-scale trial.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Khandkar Md. Moniruzzainan Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)308</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section —265C and 265D</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Discharge of accused —Duty upon the Sessions Judge</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sections 265C and 265D are abridged substitution of the now repealed Chapter XVIII of the Code and these cast a duty upon the Sessions Judge to apply his judicial mind in considering the materials collected by the prosecution and placed on record in order to come to a decision whether charge should be framed against a particular accused or not. If the Court of Sessions fails to discharge its duty in this regard properly the High Court Division in exercise of its revisional jurisdiction shall interfere in order to prevent the abuse of the process of the Court and vexatious trial and thereby save innocent people from being harassed.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mrs. Jobaida Rashid Vs The State, 17(BLD) (HCD)352</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—265C and 265D</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the statements of witnesses recorded under section 161 of the Code none of the witnesses stated that the accused-respondent was in any way connected with the occurrence and he committed any act of abetment. Under such circumstances, the High Court Division was justified in holding that there was no sufficient ground for proceeding against the accused on the charge of abetment for murder and rightly discharged the accused.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs Khondker Md. Moniruzzainan, 17BLD(AD)54</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—265C and 265D</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Discharge of accused—Framing of charge against the accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It casts a duty upon the Court to consider on the materials on record if thera is sufficient ground for proceeding against the accused. Its object is to enquire into the materials on record for prima facie satisfaction of the Court as to whether the accused. should be discharged or proceeded against so that innocent persons may not be harassed on false and vexatious allegations.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">If after consideration of the materials on record and hearing the parties the Court is of the opinion that there is prima facie ground for presuming that the accused has committed an offence, it shall frame necessary charges against him under section 265D of the Code. It is settled by judicial pronouncements that the High Court Division acting under section 439 of the Code and having supervisory jurisdiction can scrutinise necessary facts to examine the propriety of any order passed by the trial Court under section 265C or 265D of the Code of Criminal Procedure. In the instant case the trial Court committed a clear illegality in framing charges against the accused petitioner without any material on record to prove his complicity in the alleged offences.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Khondker Maniruzzainan Vs The State, 17BLD(HCD)11</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 45 DLR (HCD) 386—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—265C, 241A and 561A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 265C in Chapter XXIII speaks of, discharge of an accused in a trial before Court of Sessions, while section 241A in Chapter XX speaks of discharge of an accused in a trial by a Magistrate. These are two independent sections of the Code which deal with discharge of an accused brought for trial in respect of cases triable by a Court of Sessions and by a Court of Magistrate. These two sections indicate that when an accused is brought for trial before a Court of law the Court upon hearing the parties and on consideration of the record of the case and the documents may discharge the accused. These two sections have nothing to do with quashing of a proceeding. Section 561A is an independent inherent power of the High Court Division and this power can be exercised in case of abuse of process of the Court and for securing the ends of justice and or to give effect to any order under the Code.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Latifa Akhter and others Vs The State and another, 19BLD(AD)20</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—288</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The deposition of a witness recorded before the Committing Court may be treated as substantive evidence when it is transferred to the Sessions Court record either under section 288 Cr.P.C. if the witness is examined in the Sessions Court, or if the provisions of section 33 of the Evidence Act are satisfied.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jalal and 3 others Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD)588</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—295A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 295A of the Code on its language is applicable to those insults to religious beliefs which in addition to being deliberate and malicious are intended to outrage the religious feelings of the followers of that religion.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Shamsuddin Ahmed and others Vs The State and another, 20BLD (HCD)268</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—337</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Tender of pardon to accomplice</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The sole purpose of granting pardon to an accused in specified offences is to procure evidence against other accused persons when the prosecution is faced with the difficulty of gathering evidence to bring home the charge against them. An accused is granted pardon on condition of his making a full and true disclosure of the whole of the circumstances within his knowledge relating to the offence and to every other person concerned, whether as principal or abettor, in the commission thereof. The Court is not justified in tendering pardon to the accused without assigning any reason, particularly when the prosecution did not pray for it.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Zakir Hossain alias Jakir Hossain and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)509 </em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339B</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the 2 appellants were in custody in connection with another criminal case and inspite of the Court’s order for their production, they were not produced before the Court, the trial of the appellants in absentia treating them as absconders cannot be sustained in law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Muslim and others Vs. The State, 15 BLD (HCD)298</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—339B</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Court, whether is competent to proceed with the trial in the absence of any accused person who is either absconding or absenting after his release on bail.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Held:</strong> The Court is competent to proceed with the trial in the absence of any accused person who is either absconding or absenting after his release on bail. The practice of adjourning criminal trial frequently on the prayer of the defence inspite of appearance of the prosecution witnesses on the ground of absence of any accused over-looking provisions of Section 339B Cr.P.C. is contrary to law and should be discouraged.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mahabubur Rahrnan Khan @ Tipu Vs. The State ((1993)13BLD(HCD)268</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27 DLR251: BCR, 1990(HCD)236—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—339B and 537</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Special Powers Act, 1974 (XIV of 1974)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—27(6)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Though the provisions laid down in section 339B of the Code of Criminal Procedure for holding trial in absentia in a case involving offences under the Penal Code and the provisions for holding the trial in absentia by the Special Tribunal in a case involving offences under the Special Powers Act are similar, the Special Tribunal is under the statutory obligation to follow the procedures laid down in section 27 (6) of the Special Powers Act before holding the trial. The notification regarding the attendance of the accused though once published in the newspapers by the Magistrate while the case was before him, cannot be held to be a sufficient compliance with the requirement of law as envisaged by section 27 (6) of the Special Powers Act. This illegality is not curable under section 537 Cr.P.C.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Sadeque Ali Vs Judge, Senior Special Tribunal, Thakurgaon and others, 14BLD (HCD)369</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1 BCR (AD) 117; 31 DLR (AD) 301; 26 C. L.J.(1925)1336—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since calculation furnished by the trial Court shows that 310 working days have passed after deduction of the holidays. Fridays and the period of 56 days consumed by adjournments given by the Sherestader -in- charge due to the absence of the presiding Judge on account of leave and his transfer from the station, it is patent that more than 270 working days have passed on the date of filing of the petition for release under section 339C Cr.P.C. It is, therefore, clear that the trial of the case on the expiry of 270 working days is without jurisdiction as the proceedings of the case stood stopped and a right of release accrued to the accused persons on the expiry of the specified and extended time as mentioned in sub-section (4) of section 339C of the Code of Criminal Procedure.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Dr. Md. Azizul Haque and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)369</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Working Days</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The term ‘working days’ as used in section 339C has been intended to mean ‘days’ on which a Court actually functions or works for the disposal of a particular case. In computing the specified period for conclusion of the trial as ordained by section 339C only such working days shall have to be counted.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The days on which the Judge concerned was on leave, adjournments taken by the accused, the holidays and the period of stay of further proceedings of the case grantedd by the superior Court are required to be deducted in computing ‘working days’ for conclusion of the trial.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Zakir Hossain Khan and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)599</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 8 BLD (1988) (AD) 122; 47 DLR (HCD) 24; 15 BLD (AD) 130(Crl. Petition For Leave To Appeal No. 132/1994)—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section —339C(4)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If a trial is held in contravention of the prescribed procedure as laid down in the Code, it cannot be said to be a trial in accordance with law and the consequence provided in law must follow.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The High Court Division is right in holding that tht proceeuiiigs oi the [rial Court after the expiry of the presciibed period of 120 days and the order of conviction and sentence passed by the learned Magistrate and affirmed by the Court of appeal below is repugnant to the provision of section 339C of the Code the order of conviction and sentence is not tenable in law.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Narayan Chandra Goswami Vs. Dinendra Nath Chakraborty and others, 15BLD (AD)118</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Adjournments taken by the accused petitioners themselves, the time lost for inability Court to function for more than a month on account of leaking of the roof of the Court building and the time consumed due to abscondence of another accused person are relevant considerations in computing the period of 270 working days. The High Court Division rightly held that the time consumed on those counts should be excluded in computing limitation.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abdul Motaleb Shaikh and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD)130</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339C before amendment by Act 42 of 1992</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The whole purpose of unamended section 339C was to whip up the prosecution and activise the trial Court so as not to delay the trial of a case unnecessarily. Its purpose was not to give the accused a right not to be tried any more on the same charge or to give him a clean bill of acquittal, even if he was accused of a heinous crime.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">By Act 42 of 1992 the period of trial was extended to 360 days. By deleting subsection (3) of section 339C the grace period of thirty days was done away with and by substituting sub-section (4) of section 339C with a new sub-section (4), the proceedings can not be stopped now and the accused can now only obtain bail to the satisfaction of the Court concerned with the expiry of 360 days. The present position, therefore, is that there is no scope of stopping of the proceeding or of revival there.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Wadud Vs. The State, 15BLD(AD) 189</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—339C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Bail to an under-trial prisoner</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the fact that accused-appellant has been in custody for about 2 years and in the course of this long period only one witness has been examined so far and in view of the uncertainty for the conclusion of trial, the appellant is entitled to get the benefit of section 339C of the Code of Criminal Procedure in getting bail.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Alsam Vs. The State, 18BLD(HCD) 163</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339C(3)(4)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 339C (3) provides that if the trial of a case cannot be concluded within the specified and extended time i.e., within 270 working days, further proceedings in respect of the trial stand stopped and the accused persons released.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The provision of section 339C (4) meant for stopping further proceedings in respect of the trial of a case and releasing the accused persons is a mandatory provision. The subsequent amendment of law cannot take away the right of release of the accused persons before the amendment of law.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Babul and others Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD)88</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339C (4)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the amended provision of section 339C by Act No. 42 of 1992, when the trial of a case cannot be concluded within 360 released on bail even in a case of non-bailable days, the accused, if in custody, should be offence unless for special reasons the Court directs otherwise. Inordinate and unreasonable delay in holding the trail provides a good ground for considering an application for bail even in a case involving grave offences.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Manzurul Hoque alias Monju Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)604</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> PLD 1974 (SC) 224; 1994 BLT (HCD) 134—Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339C(2B)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Before sub-section (2B) came into effect on 1.4.1991, a Sessions Case was required to be concluded within 240 days from the date on which the case was received for trial by the Court concerned. If the trial could not be concluded within the specified or the extended time (240+30=270 days), sub-section (4) would be attracted and further proceedings in respect of the trial would stand stopped and the accused persons released.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Ana Miah and another, 14BLD(AD)143</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sub-section (2B) of section 339C (XVI of 1991)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provides that notwithstanding the transfer of a case from one Court to another Court, the time specified in sub-section (1) or sub-section (2), shall be the time for concluding the trial of a case. The provisions of subsection (2B) are not attracted to the transfer of a case made before 1.4.1991.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Samad and others Vs. The State, 14BLD (AD)247</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—339(b)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is well-settled that the provision of section 339(b) Cr.P.C. is a mandatory one and unless it is duly complied with, it vitiates the trial.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 339(b) provides that in a case of trial in absentia, notification in official gazette and also publication in at least one Bengali daily newspaper, asking the absconding accused to surrender within a date fixed by the Court, is necessary. In the instant case, notification was issued but there was no compliance of paper notification as required under section 339B of the Code of Criminal Procedure. So, it is clear that there was no compliance of the mandatory provision of section 339B of the Code of Criminal Procedure and this vitiated the entire trial.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Ali Flossain Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)102</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339 B(1)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the absence of any notification in respect of the absconding accused in any newspaper as was required under the law, the Special Judge acted illegally in proceeding with the trial in violation of the express provision of law.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">On perusal of the order-sheet of the learned Divisional Special Judge, it appears that though there was an order for issuance of warrant of arrest, proclamation and attachment against the present appellant who was absconding since the beginning of the case, yet no execution report of them was received by the Court below. It is also apparent that notification in the official gazette as required by the existing provision of section 339B(l) was published but there was no notification in any newspaper which was required under the law at the relevant time and inspite of that the Special Judge proceeded with the trial. This is a violation of the express provision of law as was existing at the relevant time and therefore the trial is vitiated for non-compliance of the requirement of law.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Jamsed Ahmed Vs. The State, 14 BLD (HCD)301</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the mandatory provision of section 339C Cr.P.C. the trial held and judgment pronounced beyond the prescribed time limit must be held to be without jurisdiction.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Siddique Ali and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)138</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339(4)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Question as to whether following stoppage of proceedings and release of the accused under section 339C(4) of the Code, a fresh complaint/proceeding against the same set of accused persons by the same set of complainants upon the self-same allegations is permissible or not.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Held:</strong> There is no legal bar for instituting a fresh prosecution on the self-same facts after a proceeding is stopped and accused released under section 339C(4) of the Code.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Niamat Ali Sheikh and ors Vs Begum Enayetur Noor &amp; others 13BLD(AD)11</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1981) 33DLR (HCD)205 distingushed—Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Scope of—legislative intent</strong>—section 339C was enacted in order to inject a spirit of utmost expedition, speed and urgency in the trial of criminal cases, but it does not appear from the language used in section 339C that it was the intention of the legislature to bid a farewell to the well-known concept of criminal jurisprudence that a criminal complaint suffers from no law of limitation and that an alleged criminal act will never go untried. Legislative exercises are meant to energise and activate the criminal Courts in the disposal of cases and are not meant to give any false hope to the accused that the arms of law will not reach them at all once the sitpulated period is over.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Niamat Ali Sheikh and ors Vs Begum Enayetur Noor &amp; others 13BLD(AD)11</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1981) 33DLR (HCD)205 distingushed—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—339C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">This section required the trial Court to conclude the trial within the statutory period from the date the case was received by it and not from the date of framing of the charges.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 339C of the Code being a procedural law and having repealed altogether by Act XLII of 1992, the accused petitioners will now be governed by the new procedural law which does not provide for stoppage of the proceedings and release of the accused persons. Failure to conclude the trial within the stated statutory period would only provide a ground for bail.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Master Giasuddin and others Vs The State, 17BLD(AD)35</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—339C(4) &amp; 339D</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Proceeding stopped and accused released thereafter—whether congnizance should or should not be taken on the revival of proceeding.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Held:</strong> It depends on the facts and circumstances of each particular case. The provision as to revival of a proceeding as amended and other related considerations will be brought to bear at the time of taking conginzance second time against the accused.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Niamat Ali Sheikh and ors Vs Begum Enayetur Noor &amp; others 13BLD(AD)11</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1981) 33DLR (HCD)205 distingushed—Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Observation of Court</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It may be observed that generally a fresh cogilizance should not be taken where a proceeding for revival could be taken but was not taken without sufficient cause or where the revived proceeding terminates again in stoppage of the same.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Niamat Ali Sheikh and ors Vs Begum Enayetur Noor &amp; others 13BLD(AD)11</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1981) 33DLR (HCD)205 distingushed—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Interpretation of Statutes</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The correct way of looking at the legislation is to see whether the legislature created a fresh bar in the nature of section 403 or analogous to it, prohibiting a fresh proceeding. The legislature created no such bar and that is the yard-sick with which the intention of the legislature is to be read (See S 339C, 403 Cr.P.C.)</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Niamat Ali Sheikh and ors Vs Begum Enayetur Noor &amp; others 13BLD(AD)11</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1981) 33DLR (HCD)205 distingushed—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Repeal of section 339D Cr.P.C.</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Act 42 of 1992 although was not given any retrospective effect, but inspite of repeal of section 339D (provision of revival of a stopped proceeding), provision was made in section 6 of the new Act for application of section 339D, as if it was not repealed, when, before the coming into force of the new Act on 1st of November, 1992, a proceeding was already stopped under the unamended provision of sub-section (4) of section 339C. If a proceeding is now stopped after the coming into force of the new Act either by the High Court Division or by the Appellate Division, the prosecution will now have only corresponding right of revival of the proceeding under the repealed provision of section 339D Cr.P.C. In such a case, the accused will be scot free and no proceeding can be continued against him without a fresh complaint or FLR. Under unamended provision of subsection (4) of section 339C, the accused did not enjoy an unqualified right or privilege of stoppage of proceeding and release if the trial could not be completed within 270 days. The new Act has effected a change in the procedural law but it has not affected any vested right of the accused and the prosecution. The right of the accused for stoppage and release was fettered and chained with the prosecution’s right to revive the case. By necessary intendment the new Act will be retrospective in operation in so far as sub-section (4) of Section 339C of the new Act is concerned.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of repeal of sub-section (4) of section 339C Cr.P.C. followed by reenactment of the said sub-section, the new procedural law will be applicable in all pending cases although instituted when the old pro-vision was in force.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abdul Wadud Vs. The State, 15BLD (AD) 189</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 19 DLR (SC) 242; 15 BLD (1995) (HCD)88—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—340</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Right of an accused to be defended by a lawyer</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provides that any person accused of an offence before a criminal Court, or against whom proceedings are instituted under this Code in any such Court has a right to be defended by a lawyer. When the offence is punishable with death, either the State or the Court shall appoint a lawyer to defend him as is enjoined by the Legal Remembrancer’s Manual.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Nurun Nahar Zaman Vs. The State and another, 15BLD(HCD)537</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—340</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Legal Remembrancer’s Manual,1960 Chapter-XII</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">State defence for undefended accused charged with offence punishable with death.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Before the trial proceeds against an undefendant accused charged with an offence punishable with death, it is necessary that state defence be arranged for defending him. It is all the more necessary when an accused is tried in absentia.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gopal Chandra Chakraborty Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)224</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27DLR(AD) 1; 46 DLR 175 Cited.</p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1053	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">CODE OF CRIMINAL PROCEDURE, 1898 [SECTION 341 - 495]</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  13BLD(HCD)461, 14BLD(HCD)167, 16BLD(HCD)120, 14BLD (HCD)280, 15 BLD(HCD)234, 15BLD (HCD)449, 16BLD (HCD)580, 17BLD(HCD)82, 18BLD(HCD)695, 19BLD(HCD)74, 20BLD(HCD)168, 16BLD(AD)293,19BLD(HCD)411, 19BLD (HCD)542, 18BLD(HCD)439,18BLD (HCD)485, 20BLD(HCD)493</p><p><b>Subject:</b>   CRIMINAL PROCEDURE</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>CODE OF CRIMINAL PROCEDURE, 1898</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Omission or defect in the examination of the accused persons under Section 342 Cr.P.C., whether occasions any miscarriage of justice.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Held:</strong> Omission or defect in the examination of the accused persons under section 342 Cr.P.C. does not occasion any miscarriage of justice and the same is mere irregularity and curable under section 537 of the Code of Criminal Procedure.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Hafez Munshi alias Hafizur Rahman and others Vs. The State, 13BLD(HCD)461</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 39 DLR 437—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">This section is intended for giving the accused an opportunity to explain the circumstances appearing against him in the evidence and this is entirely for the benefit of the accused. The prosecution can get no benefit out of it.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abdul Karim Vs Shamsul Alam and another, 14BLD(HCD)167</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When confession is the only evidence of involvement of the accused in the alleged offence and his attention is not drawn to it while examining him under Section 342 Cr.P.C for affording him opportunities to offer his explanation thereon, it must be held that the requirement of law was not fulfilled. The accused has been seriously prejudiced.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Kashem and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)120</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 10 DLR 61—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">This section has been provided for the protection and benefit of the accused and it imposes a duty upon the Court to examine the accused properly and fairly by bringing all the incriminating materials to the notice of the accused for enabling them to furnish reasonable explanations. Failure to comply with this requirement of law prejudices the accused persons.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Zafar and others Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)280</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When in his statement under section 342 Cr.P.C. the accused gives a reasonable explanation in support of his innocence, the Court is required to consider the said explanation in the light of the facts and circumstances of the case and the evidence on record. The Court is not justified in ignoring the statement of the accused recorded under section 342 of the Code of Criminal Procedure.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>A.K.M. Hafizuddin Vs. The State, 15 BLD(HCD)234</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 8DLR(WP)64; 9DLR(SC) 14; 14 DLR 292; 25DLR (SC)73; 5 DLR (WP)67 (Old 106)—Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provides that the attention of the accused persons must be drawn to all incriminating evidence and circumstances appearing in the evidence against them and they must be given the opportunity to explain the circumstances and the evidence to be used against them. Failure to do so vitiates the order of conviction.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Kamala and others Vs The State, 15BLD (HCD)449</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Examination of accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It requires that the incriminating evidence must be brought to the notice of the accused to enable him to explain his position. Violation of this requirement of law prejudices the accused.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Nizamuddin Dhali Vs. The State 16BLD (HCD)580</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 5 D.L.R. 509; 39 DLR 14; PLD 1960 (Dhaka)13;41 DLR(AD)158; 16DLR 223, 39DLR 437; 28DLR(AD)35; 33 DLR 191—Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Incriminating evidence must be brought to the notice of the accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The only evidence against the accused being the alleged recognition at the T.I. Parade, this vital fact must be brought to the notice of the accused while examining him under section 342 of the Code. Non-mentioning of the vital fact while examining him under section 342 of the Code has deprived him of the opportunity to offer his explanation to the matter and the same has seriously prejudiced him and the trial stands vitiated on that score.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md. Mizanur Rahman alias Miza alias Mizan Vs The State, 17BLD(HCD)82</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1982)3 SCC 368; 4IDLR 32; 19 DLR (1967) 662; 29 DLR 355; A.I.R. 1964 (All) 290—Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Provision of section 342 of the Code being a mandatory provision of procedural law, the departure from the fundamental principles of the said section causes grave prejudice to the accused. Since the accused-appellant was not given any opportunity to explain the circumstances, the order of conviction appears to have been vitiated and it is liable set aside.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Nurul Islam alias Nur Islam Vs The State, 18BLD(HCD)695</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 342 of the requires that all incriminating piece of evidence and circumstances appearing against the accused must be brought to his notice so that he may offer his explanation thereto. The exercise under section 342 of the Code is not an idle formality but a legal necessity affording a valuable right to the accused and as such a definite duty is cast upon the trial Court to see that the provision of section 342 is duly complied with.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The casual and irresponsible manner in which the accused appellants have been examined under section 342 of the Code clearly betrays a sense of total lack of application of a judicial mind and judicial responsibility on the part of the learned Special Judge and the same has definitely prejudiced the appellants.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Alauddin Khan Pathan and others Vs The State, 19BLD(HCD)74</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> AIR 1936(P.C) 253, 41DLR 64, 50 DLR 17—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">This provision of law is intended for the benefit of the accused, the trial court is under an obligation to properly comply with the requirement of law so as to avoid any possible prejudice to the accused on this count.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Al-haj Nurul Islam Chowdhury and another Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)168</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The trial Court should draw the attention of the accused to the main incriminating evidence against him, particularly the confessional statement, while examining him under section 342 Cr.P.C. But mere omission to specifically draw the attention of the accused to such evidence does not always prejudice him.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the accused did not retract his confession either by application from jail or by directly filing such application in the Court at any time, no grievance on the unsubstantiated allegations of inducement and torture can be entertained at the last stage of the trial.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mezanur Rahman and others Vs. The State, 16BLD(AD)293</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—342 and 537</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is a settled law that exculpatory statement uncorroborated by any other evidence, cannot be the basis of a conviction. Since attention was not drawn while the appellant was examined under section 342 of the Code either to his confessional statement or to the statement of Dulal, the appellant is obviously prejudiced and as such the trial stands vitiated. Such defect is not curable under section 537 of the Code.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abu Jamal and others Vs The State,19BLD(HCD)411</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Children Act, 1974 (XXXIX of 1974)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—2(f)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">According to section 2(f) of the Act a child means a person under the age of 16 years. In the instant case the appellant was a regular student of Class IX at the time of commission of the offence and he was aged about 14 years when the Court examined him under section 342 of the Code and as such the trial is without jurisdiction.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md Shamim Vs The State, 19BLD (HCD)542</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—366 and 369</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The delivery of judgment means the pronouncement of judgment within the meaning of section 366 of the Code while the provision of signing of judgment is contained in section 367 thereof. Section 369 of the Code provides that once a judgment is delivered and signed a finality is attached to it. The Criminal Court has no authority whatsoever to review its judgment and to make any alteration or correction therein except correcting clerical mistakes.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is the universal practice of the High Courts of the sub-continent that judgments are dictated and delivered in the open Court and the verdict of the Court is made known to all concerned right at the moment of its pronouncement. Thereafter judgments are transcribed and signed by the Presiding Judge or Judges, Between the delivery of the judgment and the signing of the same, there invariably intervenes a time gap which varies from case to case depending on the size of the judgment and the time consumed for transcription of the same and other incidental factors and in the process there emerges a time gap. Just because a judgment has not been formally signed after its transcription by mechanical devices but it has duly been delivered in the open Court, it cannot be conceived that the Court can still review its judgment by way of rehearing the matter.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Moniruzzaman alias Muhammad Maniruzzaman Vs. The State, 18BLD(HCD)439</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 33DLR88; 14DLR (SC)76; 4BLD (AD) 168—Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—367</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Contents of Judgment</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is the function of the trial Court to analyse the evidence, both direct and circumstantial, in the back ground of the respective case of the prosecution and defence and to separate the grains from the chaffs. If the trial Court is swayed by the oratory of the lawyer of a party to the case and without properly analysing the evidence on record in the context of the case of the prosecution and defence, arrives at any finding on consideration of irrelevant matters assigning artificial reasons, then those reasons are likely to be manifestly wrong and perverse and decision arrived at on the basis of such reasons may result in failure of justice.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Dulal Miah alias Dulal alias Nurun Nabi Vs Ruhul Amin and others, 18BLD (HCD)485</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—367 and 424</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">While disposing of a criminal appeal, the appellate Court is required to formulate the points for determination, the decisions thereon and the reasons for the decisions. This necessarily implies that the appellate court must consider at least the material evidence of the case and arrive at his independent findings on all material points at issue. Mere saying that it concurred with the findings of the trial Court without referring to the material evidence on record is not sufficient to meet the requirements of law.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Yasin Mollah and another Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)493</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—369</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Judgment in a criminal case</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a criminal case a judgment means a judgment of conviction or acquittal. Every order passed in a criminal case cannot, therefore, be termed as a judgment. So, an order dismissing a criminal revision for default is not a judgment within the meaning of section 369 of the Code. In the absence of any specific bar in the Code of Criminal Procedure, a petitioner, whose revisional application has been dismissed for default and not on merit, is not debarred from a filing second revisional application to challenge an illegal order.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Anowar Hossain and others Vs. Md. Idrish Miah, 15BLD(HCD)457</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref.</strong> 12 DLR 681; 35 DLR 208—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—374</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a reference under section 374 of the Criminal Procedure Code, whether the proceedings shall be submitted to the High Court Division and the sentence shall not be executed unless it is confirmed by the High Court Division—Whether a Death Reference may be disposed of even if the condemned accused is absconding.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Held</strong> : (i) In a reference by the Sessions Judge under section 374 Cr.P.C., the proceedings shall be submitted to the High Court Division and the sentence shall not be executed unless it is confirmed by the High Court Division.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">(ii) A Death Reference made under section 374 Cr.P.C. may be disposed of by the High Court Division even if the condemned accused is absconding.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs Md.Tuku Biswas, 13BLD (HCD)306</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 21 DLR(SC)109; PLD 1969(SC) 89—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—374</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sentence of death passed by a Court of Session shall have to be referred to the High Court Division for confirmation</p><p style="text-align: justify;">A sentence of death passed by a Special Martial Law Court against the two petitioners and confirmed by the Chief Martial Law Administrator but the sentence remained unexecuted till the withdrawal of Martial Law, shall have to be referred by the Sessions Judge to the High Court Division for its confirmation under section 374 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. In such a case although the death sentence has not been awarded by the Sessions Judge himself, the execution of sentence of death passed by the Special Martial Law Court having not reached its finality for non- execution, the Sessions Judge is under a legal obligation to follow the provision of section 374 of the Code as he is required to follow in the case of sentence of death passed by him in the trial held by himself.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abdul Baset and another Vs. Government of Bangladesh, through the Secretary, Ministry of Home Affairs and others, 15BLD (HCD)210</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 41 DLR 484,44DLR(AD)16—Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—376</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Commutation of death sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The condemned-prisoner is in the condemned-cell for a period of 4 years and 11 months and he has been suffering the mental agony of death in the death cell. Considering the fact and circumstances of the case and the fact that death sentence remained pending for 4 years and 11 months, the High Court Division held that the sentence of life imprisonment instead of death will meet the ends of justice.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs Md. Monir Ahmed alias Monir Hossain, 18BLD(HCD)605</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—376</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Penal Code, 1860 (XLV of 1860)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—34</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Vicarious liability and commutation of death sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since the condemned prisoners Omar Ali, Quasem Ali did not give the fatal blows they are only vicariously liable under section 34 of the Penal Code for the offence of murder but ends of justice will be met if their sentence is commuted and reduced to one of imprisonment for life. Since the accused Akkel Ali gave the channy blow on the stomach and.the ribs and there was infection as a result of that injury the High Court Division inclined to maintain his sentence of death.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs Akkel Ali and ors, 20BLD (HCD)484</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—376</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Commutation of sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The condemned prisoner was arrested on 11.12.91, conviction and sentence to death on 20.9.94. He has suffered the agony of death sentence for more than 5 years, moreover he is a man of 25 years. He has an old mother, one wife (1st wife) and two children to support and look after. He is not a hardened criminal. He found his wife (deceased) in illicit connection and in an inappropriate situation with Delwar and thus suffered from a sense of being wronged by her. He cannot be termed as ‘vicious macho male’ and accordingly the sentence of death is reduce to life imprisonment.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>The State Vs Bellal Hossain, 20BLD (HCD)45</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> Nausher Ali Sarder Vs. State, 39DLR(AD) 194—Cited</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—410</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If a superior Court disposes a criminal matter, more particularly a criminal appeal of such a great importance in such a light hearted manner without any application of judicial mind then that will have a demoralizing effect on the subordinate judiciary in disposing criminal justice in Bangladesh.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mahmudul Islam @ Ratan VsThe State, 20BLD(AD)249</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 18BLD (AD) (1998)254: 4 MLR (AD)29—relied</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—401 (I)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Government power to suspend or remit sentences</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>General Clauses Act, 1879 (X of 1879)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Section—21</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Acting President’s Order No. 70 of 1990</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Acting President’s Order dated 14.1.1991 was passed under section 401(1) of the Code of Criminal Procedure and it was given effect to on and from 14.1.1991 and under this Order numerous convicts got release before 1.6.1996 passed subsequently by the Government. If now the Government’s Order dated 1.6.1996 is made applicable in the case of the present detenu, it will lead to discrimination in the matter of enjoying amnesty benefits given by the Acting President on 14.1.1991.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a legal right is accrued in favour of a man and if any order or action to this end is given effect to, then such right and consequence cannot be taken back or nullified by a subsequent Govt. order under section 21 of the General Clauses Act.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mohammad Jahangir Alam Vs Government of Bangladesh and others, 17BLD (HCD) 195</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27 DLR 315; 18 DLR 92; PLD 1960 (SC)310; PLD 1963 Lahore 53 (DB), PLD 1965(Pesh)47 (DB); 12DLR865(DB)—Cited.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Sections—410 and 423</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Disposal of a criminal appeal acquitting the accused-appellant in a summary manner just on an untenable medical ground and without considering the entire evidence on record, particularly the evidence of the eye witnesses, acting on untenable premises is not sustainable in law.
