The State, 14BLD(HCD)477</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—30</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confessional statement, whether is evidence itself?</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Confessional statement is not evidence itself. It can only be taken into consideration against the co-accused if corroborated by some other independent evidence. This section applies to confession made by an accused affecting himself and his co-accused in a joint trial for the same offence and not the statement whereby he does not admit his guilt</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Gafur and others, Vs. The State, 13BLD(HCD)598</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—30</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Section 30 of the Evidence Act provides that the confession of a co-accused can be taken into consideration to lend assurance to other substantive evidence on record but it never says that such confession amounts to proof. In the instant case, there being no substantive evidence, either direct or circumstantial, implicating the appellant in the alleged murder or in the abetment of the same except as to some evidence about the motive of the offence, the High Court Division was wrong in treating the confessional statement of the co-accused as substantive evidence and treating the evidence of P.Ws. 4 and 7 as corroboration thereof.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ustar Ali Vs. The State, 18BLD (AD)43</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref: </strong>1LR76 Indian Appeals 147; 27DLR 29; 44DLR(AD) 10—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—30</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Confession of co-accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 30 of the Act provides that the confession of a co-accused can be taken into consideration to lend assurance to other substantive evidence on record but it never says that such confession amounts to proof. In the instant case, there being no substantive evidence, either direct or circumstantial, implicating the appellant in the alleged dacoity or in the abatement of the same except as to some evidence about the motive of the offence, the trial Court was wrong in treating the confessional statement of the co-accused as substantive evidence against the appellant.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mojibar Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)273</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> Ustar Ali Vs. The State, 18BLD (AD) (1998)43: 3BLC(AD)53; Bhuboni Safer Vs. The King, 1LR76 Indian Appeals 147; Lutfun Nahar Vs. State, 27DLR29; Babor Ali Mollah Vs. State, 44DLR(AD)10 ; 2DLR (PC) 29—relied.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—30</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 30 of the Act provides that the confession of a co-accused can be taken into consideration to lend assurance to other substantive evidence on record, but it never says that such confession amounts to proof. In the instant case there being no substantive evidence, either direct or circumstantial, implicating Ansar Ali, Montaz, Bhola and Hormuz Ali in the alleged commission of dacoity, the learned Assistant Sessions Judge was wrong in convicting the appellants on the basis of the confessional statement of their two co-accused.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Ansar Ali Vs The State, 19BLD (HCD)224</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1 8BLD(AD)( 1998)43—relied upon</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—32(1)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dying declaration</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is alleged by the defence that P.W.1 admittedly not a witness of the occurrence implicated the accused persons out of enmity at the instance of P.W.4 who during cross- examination himself stated categorically that before going to the thana for lodging F.I.R. he consulted the matter with the informant PW. 1 which lends support to the defence version that the accuseds have been falsely implicated in the case in collusion with him. It is also in the evidence of P.W.5 that regarding dying declaration alleged to have been made by the deceased on query to her wife but Mobessera did not say that she reported the matter to P.W. 1 before going to thana for lodging</p><p style="text-align: justify;">F.I.R. Moreovor the daroga deposed that she did not state to him that her husband the victim told her the names of the accused persons or she saw them running away. This statements are also absent in the first information report. Moreover the principal accused Kamrul Islam have been acquitted by another Bench of the High Court Division disbelieving the prosecution story in Criminal Appeal No. 605 of 1995.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Liton Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)219</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—32(1)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case, there are two dying declarations made by deceased Hatem Ali Sikder and in both the dying declarations he mentioned the names of the appellants. The second dying declaration being nearer to death, the High Court Division believed the same and acted on it. Moreover, P.Ws 4 and 7 in clear terms mentioned the inflicting of injuries by the appellants on the person of the deceased, which is supported by the medical evidence. The High Court Division ought not to have rejected the evidence of these two witnesses merely on the ground that they were not disinterested witnesses, notwithstanding the fact that the defence failed to shaken their credibility in cross-examination. The order of conviction is maintained.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Samad Sikdar Vs. Md. Abdul annan Sikder, 17BLD(AD)239</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—32 (1)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dying declaration—Conditions for acceptance</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Three conditions will have to be fulfilled before a dying declaration is acted upon. The first condition is whether the victim had the physical capability of making the statement. Secondly, whether the witnesses heard the statement correctly and reproduced the names of the assailants correctly in the Court. Thirdly, whether the maker of the dying declaration had the opportunity to recognise the assailants correctly.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abul Kashem Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD)205</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—32(1)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Statement of a dead person when relevant?</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Statement, written or verbal, of relevant facts, made by a person who is dead is relevant when the statement is made by a person as to the cause of his or her death in cases in which the cause of that person’s death comes into question.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Abdul Khaleque, 13BLD (HCD)354</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> PLD 1956 (FC)43=8 DLR (FC) 24; BCR 1982 (Special Bench )344; 12 BLD 49; 40 DLR(AD)281; 21DLR(SC)89=PLD 1969 -SC 89; Pakala Narayan Swami Vs. The King Emperor (1939)56 Indian Appeals 66 (76); 27 DLR (AD) 29—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—80</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Examination of the recording Magistrate</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It dispenses with the necessity of a formal proof of a confession duly recorded by a Magistrate in accordance with the provisions of section 164 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. In such a case the examination of the recording Magistrate is not imperative.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Tajul Islam and 8 others, 15BLD(HCD)53</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—32(1)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dying declaration</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A dying declaration if believed can alone from the basis of conviction, as it is a substantive evidence under section 32 of the Act. In the instant case the dying declaration was duly recorded by a competent Magistrate who proved the same before the Court. The dying declaration was recorded on 22.5.1989 and the victim died on 15.6.1989. The intestine of the victim came out and he was operated upon immediately. Thereafter on the requisition of hospital authority the dying declaration was recorded by a competent Magistrate which shows critical condition of the victim. Moreover the victim himself corroborated, it by saying that he could not speak further and there- fore merely because the victim died some days after recording the dying declaration will not render the dying declaration inadmissible under section 32 of the Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Akkel Ali and ors, 20 BLD(HCD)484</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—80</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Memorandum by Magistrate conducting</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>TI. Parade—Not admissible in evidence</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Memorandum recorded by the Magistrate conducting T.I. Parade is not a judicial proceeding and as such it is not admissible under section 80 of the Evidence Act, unless the Magistrate deposes before the Court and proves it. Non-examination of the Magistrate who held the T.I. Parade renders the memorandum drawn by him inadmissible in evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mizanur Rahman alias Miza alias Mizan Vs The State, 17BLD(HCD)82</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—101</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Burden of Proof</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a criminal case the onus of proving the charge against an accused lies on the prosecution. The prosecution must prove its case beyond reasonable doubt and the accused has no obligation to account for the death of the victim for which he is placed on trial. But when the murder of the wife of the appellant takes place in his house while she was living with him in the same house, the accused owes a duty to explain as to how his wife has met with her death.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ali Hossain Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD)307</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—101 and 106</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The burden of proving the manner of the incident as alleged by the prosecution lies squarely on the prosecution and it never shifts. If the manner of the incident is not proved, the prosecution must necessarily fail, no matter, whether the defence version of the case has not been proved either.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In order to bring a case within the ambit of section 106 of the Evidence Act, ‘special knowledge” of relevant facts must be attributed to the accused persons in respect of commission of the offence. Only in such exceptional cases the burden of proving those facts is shifted to the accused persons for dispelling special knowledge’ in the commission of the offence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kawsarun Nessa and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)21</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—102</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Burden of Proof</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The gravity of the offence or its heniousness is no ground in determining the guilt of the accused. For securing conviction the prosecution must prove its case by cogent and legal evidence. The guilt of the accused cannot be said to have been established only with reference to the statements made in the FIR, it never being substantive evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Md. Habibur Rahman Khan, 17BLD(HCD)527</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—102</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Burden of proof</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In our criminal jurisprudence the burden of proving the guilt of the accused lies squarely on the prosecution and it does not ordinarily shift on to the accused, yet, in anticorruption cases under the Criminal Law Amendment Act, 1958 certain burden has been thrust upon the accused to account for the goods and properties entrusted to him.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case the defence miserably failed to discharge that onus to dispel the effect of the overwhelming evidence on record proving the guilt of the accused beyond reasonable doubt and as such there is no substance in the instant appeal and accordingly it fails.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>G. M. Nowsher Ali Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD)1 77</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—106</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Burden of proving fact especially within the knowledge of any person—Alibi in a quasi-criminal proceeding</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Even in a quasi-criminal proceeding if a plea of alibi is taken the burden to prove it is on the person who takes such a plea, because that fact is within his special knowledge. The petitioner had opportunities to prove his absence from the country since 1982 at the trial Court, the lower appellate Court and the High Court Division but he did not avail of the opportunities. Even before the Appellate Division the petitioner failed to produce any proof in support of his plea of alibi. Under such circumstances the petition merits no consideration.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abdul Tahid alias Tahid Ulla Vs. Md. Kadaris All, 16BLD(AD)248</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—106</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Murder of wife—Alibi of the husband— Presumption</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The accused and his wife were sleeping in the same bed. The wife died due to multiple injuries on her person. In such circumstances a heavy burden is cast upon the husband to satisfactorily explain as to how his wife died.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gouranga Kumar Saha Vs The State, 17BLD(HCD)259</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114 (g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Adverse presumption</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Unless it is shown that the witnesses named in the charge sheet were material witnesses in the case, no adverse inference against the prosecution should be drawn under section 114(g) of the Evidence Act for non-examination of all those witnesses.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Reazuddin Sardar alias Md. Reazuddin and others Vs. The State, 14BLD (AD)178</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Non-examination of important witnesses, particularly some of the neighbours, without reasonable explanation raises a presumption against the prosecution to the effect that had they been examined, they would not support the prosecution case.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong>—Even if there may be elements of truth in the prosecution case against the accused, that by itself is not sufficient for conviction. Between “may be true” and “must be true” there is inevitably a long distance to travel and whole of the distance must be covered by the prosecution by legal and reliable evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dula Mm alias Nurul Islam and others The State, 14BLD(HCD)477</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Non-examination of independent witnesses, especially some of the close neighbours, calls for a presumption under section 114(g) of the Evidence Act to the effect that had they been examined they might have deposed against the prosecution.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kawsarun Nessa and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)21</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 25 DLR 398; AIR 1936 (All) 833; 41DLR(HCD) 349; 31 DLR (AD) 75—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Suggestion by the defence lawyer</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Suggestion by the defence lawyer cannot be construed as admission of guilt by the accused. The accused is not required to prove his innocence. The prosecution must prove his guilt beyond reasonable doubt.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dula Mia alias Nurul Islam and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)477</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27 DLR(AD)29; 44DLR(AD) 10, I.L.R. .76 Indian Appeals 147; 12 DLR (SC) 156; 12DLR (SC) 217; 36 DLR 185; 16 DLR 147; 45 DLR 171; A.I.R. 1939 (PC) 47; 25 DLR 399; A.I.R. 1957 (SC)107; 6BLD(AD)1— Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Although law does not require that all the chargesheeted witnesses will have to be examined at the trial but nevertheless when the prosecution withholds material witness and fails to examine natural witnesses from the vicinity of the place of occurrence without any explanation, it casts a serious reflection on the prosecution case. Under such circumstances an adverse presumption under section 114(g) of the Evidence Act should be drawn against the prosecution.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Momin alias Md. Mominul Huq Vs The State, 16BLD(HCD)246</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>The Court may presume existence of certain facts</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the alleged occurrence took place in broad daylight on a busy pathway and the prosecution failed to examine even a single witness from the neighbourhood and furnished no cogent explanation for non- examination of such witnesses adverse presumption under section 114(g) of the Evidence Act must be drawn against the prosecution.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Alamgir Hossain alias Alaingir Hossain and another Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)270</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Non-examination of the Investigation Officer</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Non-examination of the Investigation Officer prejudices the defence and deprives the accused of an opportunity of cross-examining him as to the manner and method of the investigation that leads to the submission of two charge-sheets on the self-same occurrence.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Abdus Sobhan Howlader and others Vs. The State,13BLD(AD)131</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 40 DLR(AD) 282—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114 (g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Presumption against the prosecution for withholding witnesses and evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the prosecution did not examine the doctor who held postmortem examination, the investigation officer and other charge-sheeted material witnesses without satisfactory explanation, adverse presumption under section 114(g) of the Evidence Act must be drawn against the prosecution.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Munsurul Hossain alias Babul Vs The State, 16BLD(HCD)326</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 36 DLR 333; 28DL R 128—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114 (g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In this case admittedly there are dwelling houses on all sides of the place of occurrence but no owner of these dwelling houses was examined in this case by the prosecution without any explanation. Non-examination of these natural, probable and disinterested witnesses calls for an adverse presumption against the prosecution under section 114(g) of the Evidence Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Tomezuddin Biswas alias Kalu and another Vs The State, 17BLD(HCD)174</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref :</strong>40DLR348; 1 1BLD(1991)23 1—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114 (g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The evidence of P.W.2 Mona corroborated by the Medical Officer PW 3 and informant P.W I to the effect that accused Paltu caught hold of victim Kafi while accused Ashraf struck an iron rod blow on his chest was not accepted by the High Court Division in view of this fact that P.W.2 omitted to state the fact in his statement before the Investigating Officer.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Ashraf Ali and others, 14BLD(AD)127</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When from the evidence of the handwriting expert it is clear that the three disputed cheques, on the basis of which moneys were withdrawn, were in the hand-writing of the accused person and he did not challenge that fact and did not explain why he wrote the three cheques belonging to a fictitious account- holders, his guilt is established and he is not entitled to get benefit of doubt out of minor omissions made by the prosecution witnesses.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Ibrahim Hossain Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD)253</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Evidence being contradictory on the material question of recognition of the accused persons and there being no circumstantial evidence lending support to the deposition of P.W2, the sole eye-witness of the case, the accused are entitled to benefit of doubt.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul and others Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)221</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The only public witness to the occurrence is the baby taxi driver P.W. 2 Aslam who failed to recognise the accused persons and he could not say the name of the baby taxi driver or the number of the babi taxis from which the contraband phensidil syrup was recovered and he did not see from whose physical possession the said syrups was recovered. The baby taxi driver in question being neither a witness nor an accused in the case and no neighbouring shop-keeper having been examined as a witness in the case, the prosecution case becomes doubtful. Under such circumstances the accused are entitled to benefit of doubt.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Farque Hossain and 2 others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)163</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1973 Cr1. L. J. (All) 1096—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of Doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In order to succeed the prosecution must prove its case beyond all reasonable doubts by cogent reliable and sufficient evidence. The accused is entitled to get all benefits of doubt till the persecution succeeds in proving its case convincingly. Simply because several other cases are pending against the accused persons, no adverse presumption can be drawn against them in determining their guilt.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Md. Musa alias Musaiya alias Shafir Bap, 15BLD(HCD) 169</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 5 BLD (HCD) 9; 46 DLR (HCD) 77; 27 DLR (AD) 29; IIDLR (SC) 84; 20 DLR 780; A.I.R. 1916 (All) 50; SC Cases 1982, 368 (1); 1IBLD(HCD) 295—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of Doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a case where bitter enmity between the parties is proved some sort of corroboration of the evidence of interested witnesses is required as a rule of prudence. In the absence of such corroboration of the evidence of interested witnesses, the accused becomes entitled to the benefit of doubt.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When FIR mentions gun shot injuries on the head and waist of victim Waliullah but the postmortem report does not mention any such injuries on the body of the victim and the Investigating Officer did not find any alamat of firing, the prosecution case becomes doubtful.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Serajul islam and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)613</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Witnesses to the case being totally disinterested and they having deposed uniformly on the material question of recovery of the revolver from the possession of the appellant, the case has been proved against him beyond the shadow of reasonable doubts. In such circumstances, the appellant is not entitled to the benefit of doubt.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mahbubnr Rahman Khan alias Tipu Vs. The State 16BLD(AD)268</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—114(g)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Benefit of doubt</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">For securing conviction in a criminal case the prosecution must prove beyond all reasonable doubts the individual liability of each and every accused person. Since there were fourteen inmates living in the house of occurrence on the fateful night and the prosecution has failed to prove beyond doubts as to which of them actually committed the alleged murder of victim Chapa, it cannot be said that the guilt of the 3 appellants has been proved. Although the defence plea that some miscreant has committed the murder from out side the window falls through and it is found that victim Chapa was murdered by some of the inmates of the house, still then since individual liability of the appellants could not be ascertained, the accused are entitled to get benefit of doubt.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Zahirul Alam Kamal and another Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)428</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 39 DLR(AD)177—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—118</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Child witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 118 of the Evidence Act a prosecutrix, who is the victim of rape or of sexual offence, is undoubtedly a competent witness and she is entitled to receive the same weight as is attached to an injured person in case of physical violence. The same degree of care and caution must therefore attach to the evaluation of her evidence as in the case of an injured witness in a case of physical violence. Once the Court is satisfied that the evidence of the prosecutrix can be safely accepted and relied upon there is no need for any corroboration to her evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jahangir Hossain Vs The State, 16BLD (HCD)238</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1960) 12 DLR (SC) 165; (1967) 19 DLR (SC) 259;13 BLD(AD)79; 1952 SCR 377; A.I.R. 1983(SC)753; A.I.R. 1980 (SC) 658; 47 DLR 54: 15 BLD 34—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—118</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Evidence of a child witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 118 of the Evidence Act provides that all persons who can understand the questions put to them or can give rational answers to those questions are competent witnesses to testify in Court.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is not imperative for the Court to subject a child witness to a preliminary examination before reception of his evidence. The Court may satisfy itself during the progress of the evidence in Court that the witness is capable of understanding the questions put to him and of giving intelligible reply. In case of such satisfaction, the evidence becomes admissible. It is desairable that the Court (trial court) should make an endorsement about its satisfaction in the ordersheet, in the deposition sheet or in the body of the judgment. There is no legal compulsion that the Court must ask preliminary questions to test the capacity of a witness to testify.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Siraj Miah Vs The State, 17BLD (HCD)295</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1952 SCA 40; 143 Indian Cases 479; 11 DLR (Dhaka) 338; 25 DLR 4—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—118</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Child witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A child witness is a competent witness to give evidence in the Court provided it appears from its deposition that it could understand the questions put and give rational answers thereto.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">If the child, though of tender age, was found to be intelligent to understand the questions put to him and to give rational answers to those questions then his capacity to give evidence was on the same footing as that of any other adult. But in such a case it would be desirable for the Court before examining the child as a witness, to test his intellectual capacity by putting a few simple and ordinary questions to him and to record a brief proceeding so that the higher Court may feel satisfied as to the capacity of the child to give evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Ali Hossain,18BLD (HCD) 655</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> AIR 1937 (Patna) 662; 1BLC (1996) 173; 43DLR(AD)(1991)234;A1R1953 (Patna) 246—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—134</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provides that no particular number of witnesses is necessary to prove any fact. The consensus of judicial opinion is that conviction can be based on the solitary evidence of a witness, if not tainted in any way.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case, since the evidence of the only witness seeing the infliction of the fatal dagger blow on the neck of victim Abdun Nabi by condemned prisoner Munshi Miah suffers from infirmities and a number of persons present near about the place of the occurrence that took place in broad daylight do not support the informant in so far as it relates to the infliction of the fatal injury, it is highly unsafe to base conviction on the solitary evidence of the informant. Discrepancy in the medical evidence makes the prosecution case doubtful.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Munshi Miah, 15BLD (HCD)139</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref.</strong> 29 DLR (AD) 21i—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—134</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Number of witnesses</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Though it provides that no particular number of witnesses is required for the proof of any fact but still then in order to convict an accused solely on the basis of the evidence of police personnels, who made the search and seizure, the Judge must ensure that their evidence is unimpeachable and unshaken in character and the other witnesses to the search and seizure, who are alleged to have resiled from their previous stand, are unworthy of credence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Talebur Rahman alias Taleb Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)86</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 8BLD 106; 21 DLR 684; 44 DLR 159—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—134</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Law does not require any particular number of witnesses to prove a case. Conviction may be well founded even on the testimony of a solitary witness provided his credibility is not shaken by any adverse circumstances appearing on the record against him and the Court, at the same time, is convinced that he is a truthful witness. As a general rule, a court can act on the testimony of a single witness though uncorroborated. One credible witness outweighs the testimony of a number of other witnesses of indifferent character. Evidence on a point is to be judged not by the number of witnesses produced but its inherent truth.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Al Amin Vs. The State, 19BLD (HCD) 307</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—137 and 139</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Examination and cross-examination of witnesses—Appreciation of evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The proper procedure for appreciation of evidence is to evaluate the evidence of a witness in its entirety and not to bank on a particular portion of it, evidence of a witness in his cross-examination is as good as that of his examination-in-chief, while considering the legal incidence and the essence of the impressions that it creates upon the mind of the Judge. In arriving at the proper conclusions, the Court is also required to take note of the attending circumstances of the case, which at times become a determining factor in judging the guilt or innocence of witness ignoring his cross-examination an vital circumstances surrounding the case, must be held to be no proper finding in the eye of law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Taizal Biswas Vs The State, 20BLD (HCD)322</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—154 and 142</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Witness—Cross-examination by the party calling it</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provides that the Court may, in its discretion, permit a party to put questions to its witness which are usually put in cross- examination by the adverse party. It may so happen that due to gaining over or unwillingness or faulty memory a witness does not support the case of the party who brings him in the witness box but gives unfavourable evidence, in that case the Court is given the discretion to relax the rule of ‘leading questions’ as defined in section 142 of the Evidence Act and allow the said party to put questions to its own witness as in done by the cross-examination by the adverse party.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Babul Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD) 386</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A.I.R. 1931 Cal 401—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—155</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Impeaching credit of witnesses</strong>—When the prosecution made out a case in the Court totally different from the F.I.R.  case and the statements of the witnesses recorded under section 161 Cr.P.C. and the witnesses are found to be suppressing material evidence, the prosecution case becomes unworthy of credence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Zakir Hossain alias Jakir Hossain and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)509</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To base a conviction upon circumstantial evidence it must be incompatible with the innocence of the accused and it must exclude all reasonable hypothesis of his innocence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Haji Md. Jamaluddin and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)33</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Corroboration</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Corroborative evidence is not an imperative component of Judicial credence in every case of rape. Corroboration as a condition for judicial reliance on the testimony of a victim of sex crime is not a requirement of law but merely a guidance of prudence under given circumstances. The rule is not that corroboration is essential before there can be a conviction. The testimony of the victim of sexual assault is vital and unless there are compelling reasons which necessite looking for corroboration of her statement, the Court should find no difficulty in acting on the testimony of a victim of sex crime alone to convict an accused where her testimony inspires confidence and is found to be reliable.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Al Amin Vs. The State, 19BLD(HCD)307</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Credibility of a witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The credit to be given to the evidence of a witness is a matter which is not governed by rule of law. It depends upon his knowledge of facts to which he testified, his disinterestedness, integrity and veracity. In judging the credibility of a witness the Court has to consider the surrounding circumstances as well as the broad probabilities. When a witness is found to be trustworthy, straight</p><p style="text-align: justify;">forward and reliable and his evidence intrinsically rings true, the evidence of such a witness can be safely relied upon.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Daliin and another Vs. The State,15BLD(HCD)133</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Corrobaration of the statement of the prosecutrix</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It has long been a rule of practice for insisting coroboration of the statement of the prosecutrix but if the Judge feels that without corroboration in a particular case conviction can be sustained without independent corroboration, then the Judge should give some indication in his judgment that he has/had this rule of caution in his mind and then should proceed to give reasons for considering it necessary to require corroboration and for considering that it was safe to convict the accused in a particular case without corroboration.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Saidur Rahman Neoton Vs. The State 13BLD(AD)79</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 12 DLR(SC)165; 19 DLR(SC)256; 1952 Supreme Court Reports 377—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Death of a witness after examination-chief and before cross-examination</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a witness died after he had been examined-in-chief and before his cross examination had been concluded, his evidence was admissible, but the degree of weight to be attached will depend on the circumstances of the case.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is no provision in the Evidence Act that the evidence of witness which is admissible at the time he gave it should become inadmissible for the simple reason that he could not be cross-examined for some unavoidable reason.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Chowdhury Mia Vs. Dhanindra Kumar Skil (1993)13BLD(HCD)216</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> Davies Vs. Otty(1865)55 E.R.875;</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">W. Strewart Vs. New Zealand Insurance Co. Ltd.(1912)16C.W.N.991=17 IC. 188; Narsing Das Vs. Gokul Prasad, A.I.R.1928 All 140 =50 All.113; Ahmed Ali Vs. Joly Prosad, 1944 All. 188—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Expunction of Evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Once a witness has deposed in the Court, his deposition has to remain on record for whatever it is and there is no law for expunction of his evidence at the instance of the prosecution.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mainuddin Howlader Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)82</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Evidence of prosecutrix in a rape case</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">On principle the evidence of a victim of sexual assault stands at par with the evidence of an injured witness. Just as a witness who has sustained an injury in the occurrence is the best witness in the sense that he is least likely to exculpate the real offender, the evidence of a victim of rape or of a sexual offence is entitled to a great weight, absence of corroboration notwithstanding. If the evidence of the victim of a sexual offence does not suffer from any basic infirmity and “probabilities factor” does not render it unworthy of credence there is no reason to insist on corroboration to the evidence of the prosecutrix. Law does not require that the evidence of the prosecutrix requires corroboration.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a sex offence case there is no legal bar in believing the sole testimony of the prosecutrix, nay, she must prima facie be believed, except in a rarest of rare cases where she is found unreliable. The necessity of corroborative evidence will arise and that the legal custom of insisting on corroboration in every case or alternatively of stating the reason for waiving such corroboration is not applicable in our country.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Jahangir Hossain Vs The State, 16BLD (HCD)238</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Evidence—False in part, may not be false in entirity</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In our country there is a tendency in the witness to exaggerate, embroider and also to falsely implicate some persons in addition to the real offenders. But even then the Court is to scan the evidence carefully so as to come to a correct decision as to which part of the evidence is acceptable and which part is to be rejected. Only when it becomes impossible to separate the truth from the falsehood, the Court will be justified in rejecting the entire evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Masum and others Vs. The State, 16BLD (HCD)151</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Non-examination of the Investigating Officer</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When non-examination of the Investigating Officer deprives the defence of its valuable right to shake the credit of the prosecution witnesses by bringing out the contradictions between their statements made during the investigation and the statements made before the Court, in such a case non- examination of the I.O. is fatal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Zafar and others vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)280</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Police personnel —Interested witness</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The public witness did not support the prosecution case and the prosecution failed to come with reasonable explanation as to why they did not support the prosecution case nor any enmity with the informant of this case. So, the persistent evidence of the public witnesses regarding denial of their presence at the time of alleged recovery in no way can be cured by the official witnesses (police personnel) who are none but interested in this case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Aslam Jahangir Vs The State, 20 BLD (HCD) 426.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1061	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">EXCISE AND SALT ACT, 1944</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  18BLD(HCD)54</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  EXCISE AND SALT </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>EXCISE AND SALT ACT, 1944</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>(I OF 1944)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—37</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Punishment for violation of the provisions of the Act</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Excise and Salt Act, 1944 and the rules framed thereunder do not authorise the police to seize manually manufactured cigarettes (Bins) on obtaining license under the said Act on the suspicion that those were being marketed by evading payment of necessary leviable excise duty as being without the requisite band-rolls thereon. The police also have no authority to arrest and prosecute any person directly for the alleged violation of the provisions of the Excise and Salt Act for alleged evasion of excise duties. The police can only act in aid of the excise authority when called upon by the former to do so. The seizure of the bins in question and arrest of the accused persons and initiation of the impugned proceeding by lodging an FIR by the police are acts not authorised by law and the same amount to an abuse of the process of the Court and as such the impugned proceeding is liable to be quashed.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Idris Miah alias Idris Ali and others Vs. The State and others, 18BLD(HCD)54</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1062	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">JANANIRAPATHA (BISHESH BIDHAN) AIN, 2000</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  20BLD(HCD)575.</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  JANANIRAPATHA (BISHESH BIDHAN) </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>JANANIRAPATHA (BISHESH BIDHAN) AIN, 2000 (VII OF 2000)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—8/10/12/14</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Having considered the vagueness of the prosecution case, the statements of 2, witnesses made under section 14 of the Am and the mysterious dropping of accused Jalal and Lahauddin from the charge sheet, the High Court Division inclined to grant bail to the appellant.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bashani alias Shirajuddin Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)575.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1063	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">LIMITATION ACT, 1908</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  15BLD (HCD)276, 16BLD(HCD)155, 13BLD(AD) 199 , 13BLD (HCD)170, 13BLD (HCD)579</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  LIMITATION</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>LIMITATION ACT, 1908</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>(IX OF 1908)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—5</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Condonation of delay</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A criminal appeal was filed after it was barred by 735 days. Poverty and helplessness of the appellant and absence of any adult male member in the family for preferring the appeal earlier were considered mitigating circumstances and compassionate grounds for condonation of the delay.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shamsul Huq Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD)276</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section-—5</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Condonation of Delay</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Condonation of delay in filing an appeal is the discretion of the appellate Court but such discretion must be exercised judicially and not arbitrarily. The hot haste in which the learned Sessions Judge condoned the delay without hearing the State and the complainant cannot be approved as being opposed to the principle of natural justice.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Minhaz A. Chowdhury Vs. Manzurul Huq and another, 16BLD(HCD)155</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—5</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>High Court Division’s satisfaction regarding the ground for delay</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the High Court Division is satisfied with the grounds for considering the delay in filing the appeal, there is nothing for the Appellants Division to interfere with the discretion.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Haque Vs. The State, 13BLD(AD) 199 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—5</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The prayer for condonation of the delay caused in filing the appeal is required to be allowed so as to enable the appellant-peitioner to get a chance of getting the appeal heard and disposed of on merit according to law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mahirun Nessa Vs. The State, 13BLD (HCD)170</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—5</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a case where limitation has not been specifically prescribed and trial is held in absentia, the pronouncement by the superior Courts, particularly by the Supreme Court, is that nobody can be made to suffer any harm to his rights including those relating to property, person and reputation, without giving him an opportunity of being heard.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Siddique V. The State, 13BLD (HCD)579</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 39DLR(1987)164; 42DLR15; P.L.D 1970 (Lahore)—Cited</p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1064	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">MLR NO. 1 OF 1982</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  13BLD(AD)17</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  MLR</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>MLR NO. 1 OF 1982</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Regulation No—3</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>The term ‘very’—what signifies?</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The term ‘vary’ used in the regulation implies an authority to change the judgment and order under review by modification or even by substitution or by qualification or otherwise.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">A review of the proceedings of a Martial Law Court is an essential part of the original proceeding which is unknown to the ordinary procedure of trial of criminal cases as under the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Any attempt to measure the extent or limit of the power of review with reference to the appellate or revisional powers exercisable under the Code will be misleading. The extent and limit of the said power will have to be determined by examining the terms of the regulations.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">A judgment, sentence or order of a Martial Law Court is merely tentative in nature so long it is not reviewed and an accused can not be said to have derived any vested right from such an order or judgment. All proceedings of the Special Martial Law Courts (as in the present cases) were to be submitted to the C.M.L.A. for review.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Upon a plain reading of the regulation, it can not be doubted that the reviewing authority, among others, has the power to ‘vary’ a judgment, even if it is of acquittal. The word ‘vary’ in regulation 3(4) includes the power to convert a judgment/order of acquittal into one of conviction.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Helaluddin Ahmed @ Swapan Vs. Bangladesh and others 13BLD(AD)17</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 43 D.L.R.(AD) 104; 33D.L.R.(1981) 233 and 358; 92 Commonwealth Law Reports,526(528); 33D.L.R.(AD) 154; PLD 1973 (SC)49; PLD 1974(SC) 51; PLD 1989 (SC) 26; 32 DLR(AD)1 10—Cited.</p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1065	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">MUSLIM FAMILY LAWS ORDINANCE, 1961</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  14BLD(HCD)149</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  MUSLIM FAMILY LAW</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>MUSLIM FAMILY LAWS ORDINANCE, 1961 (VIII OF 1961)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section</strong>—<strong>6(5)(b)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">As the complaint has compromised the case with her husband, the parties should be permitted to compound the offence for enabling them to live peacefully. The accused-petitioner is acquitted of the charge brought against him on compromise.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Azizul Haque Howlader Vs The State and another, 14BLD(HCD)149</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1066	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">NARCOTIC CONTROL ACT, 1990</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  16BLD(AD)239.</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  NARCOTIC CONTROL</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>NARCOTIC CONTROL ACT, 1990 (XX OF 1990)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—19(1)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Penalty for breach of the provision of</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—9</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Act has mentioned ‘heroin’ as narcotic substance which is sold as such in the market, a substance which is in traffic as such around the world. Law in every country is very stringent in dealing with a narcotic drug because of its disastrous potential on social, national and international life. In these contexts the Appellate Division took the view that if a substance which is commercially accepted as ‘hereoin’ contains what is chemically called ‘diacetylmorphine’ the whole substance is to be treated as ‘heroin’ for the purpose of the Act. The law intends to punish dealer of the narcotic irrespective of whether it is in the purest form or not.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case, since it has been proved that the seized packets contained ‘heroin’, the whole of the contents must be treated heroin for the purpose of punishment. It is necessary for the prosecution to prove the “actual and real heroin content” for the purpose of a conviction under 1(b) of the Table of section 19. The High Court Division was wrong to ignore the real perspectives of the act and took an erroneous view that the purpose of the law is only to punish the preparation, carrying and dealing etc. of ‘laboratory heroin’ and not the stuff which was being clandestinely carried by the accused taking great personal risk under a remote control system managed by the captains of the crime sitting across the seas.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the circumstances of the case there was no scope for altering the conviction from one under Clause 1(b) to Clause 1(a) of the Table and consequently there could not be any question of reduction of sentence on compassionate grounds as was done by the High Court Division.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Miss Eliadah McCord 16BLD(AD)239.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1067	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">NARI-O- SHISHU NIRJATAN (BISHESH BIDHAN) AIN, 1995</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  18 BLD(HCD)18, 18BLD (HCD)81, 19BLD (HCD)527, 19BLD(HCD)150, 18 BLD(HCD)18</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  NARI-O- SHISHU NIRJATAN (BISHESH BIDHAN)</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>NARI-O- SHISHU NIRJATAN (BISHESH BIDHAN) AIN, 1995 (XVIII OF 1995)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—23</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Applicability of the Code of Criminal Procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 23 of the Act the provisions of the Code of Criminal Procedure have been made applicable in respect of investigation, trial and disposal of cases coming under this Act. Regarding investigation, time limit has been fixed in section 18 of the Act without making any provision as to what legal consequences shall follow if the investigation is not completed within the specified time. So in this regard the provisions of section 167(5) Cr.P.C. as amended by Act XLII of 1992 shall be applicable and the accused is entitled to bail in suitable cases.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Anwar Hossain Vs. The State, 18 BLD(HCD)18</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—20(5)(Ka)(Kha)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The trial Court issued processes for arrest of the accused and for proclamation and attachment of his properties but the service return of the said processes was yet to be received by the Court. Without taking further steps in the matter the Court resorted to the provision of section 20(5)(Kha) by publishing a notification in the daily It, directing the accused to appear before the Court within 30 days of such publication. Since there was no material before the Court to be satisfied that the accused was avoiding arrest and he was concealing himself from appearing in the Court and his attendance or arrest could not be secured at an early date, the impugned notification in the newspaper was in clear violation of section 20(5)(Kha) of the Act.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The intention of the legislature in making the provision for publishing the notification in question in at least one daily news paper under section 20(5) (Kha) is obviously to indicate a widely circulated national daily and not a district based daily newspaper with a very limited circulation.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Belayet Howlader Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)81</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—6(3)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">From the oral evidence and from the facts and circumstances, it is quite clear that the informant was raped on the night of occurrence by accused appellants. Though on medical examination of the victim no sign of rape was found and though no mark of violence was noticed on the body of the victim, in view of the entire evidence on record it appears that the victim was raped by the appellants. So, the learned Judge committed no illegality in convicting the appellant under section 6(3) of Nari-O-Shisu Nirjatan (Bishes Bidhan) Ain, 1995.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Badal and another Vs The State, 19BLD (HCD)527</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—9(Kha) and 14</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">For bringing accusation of kidnapping a woman for accomplishment of one of the purposes as mentioned in section 9 of the Act or to put a person on trial to answer the accusation of kidnapping a woman for one of the purposes mentioned in the section there must be kidnapping of a woman for one of the purposes or in other words there must be kidnapping with a mind to accomplish one of the purposes mentioned in the section.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Framing of charge against the petitioners under sections 9(Kha) and 14 of the Am is not legal and as such the continuation of the proceedings of Nari-O-Shishu Case would amount to harassment to the petitioners and that also would amount to an abuse of the process of Court.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Younus Ali &amp; 3 others Vs. The State, 19BLD(HCD)150</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Scction—23</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Applicability of the Code of Criminal Procedure</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 23 of the Act provisions of the Cr.P.C. have been made applicable to cases coming under this Act in matters of investigation and trial. Although time limit for completing investigation has been fixed in section 18 of the Act, no provision has been made as to what legal consequences shall follow if the investigation is not completed within the specified time. So, the amended provision of section 167(5) Cr.P.C. shall apply in this regard.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Anwar Hossain Vs. The State, 18 BLD(HCD)18.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1068	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">NARI-O-SHISHU NIRJATAN (SPECIAL PROVISION) AIN, 1995</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  18BLD(HCD)392,17BLD(AD)193, 17BLD(HCD)440. </p><p><b>Subject:</b>  NARI-O-SHISHU NIRJATAN (SPECIAL PROVISION)</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>NARI-O-SHISHU NIRJATAN (SPECIAL PROVISION) AIN, 1995 (XVIII OF 1995)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—2 (Cha), 10 and 11</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dowry</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The word ‘dowry’ used in sections 11 and 10 of the Am shall have to be understood in its ordinary meaning, namely property brought by woman to her husband at marriage or vice versa. CIT (dowry) has been used in these sections of the Am not only to mean property or valuable security agreed to be given at the time or before or after the marriage but also property etc. demanded after the marriage for which there was no previous agreement.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dipak Kumar Roy alias Kazal Vs. The State, 18BLD(HCD)392</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 46 DLR(AD) 169—relied</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—17</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In taking cognizance of an offence under this Act the Bishesh Adalat will consider whether a prima facie case has been disclosed under the said Act against the accused on perusal of the FIR, the charge-sheet and other materials on record. If the said Adalat takes cognizance of the case, it will give a hearing to the prosecution and the accused for the purpose of framing charge under section 241 Cr.P.C. with liberty to decide on its own whether to frame charges or not.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mohiuddin and others Vs Md. Motiur Rahman and another, 17BLD(AD)193</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—20</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Act does not provide for any consequence for failure of the trial Court to conclude the trial within one hundred and twenty days. The legislature having not provided for any penal provision in case of failure of the trial Court to conclude the trial within the fixed period does not result in any penal consequence. This limitation of time for disposal of cases is merely directory and not mandatory in nature.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Habibur Rahman Zakir and others Vs The State, 17BLD(HCD)440.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1069	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">NEGOTIABLE INSTRUMENTS ACT, 1881</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  19BLD(AD)166, 17 BLD (HCD)498</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  NEGOTIABLE INSTRUMENTS</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>NEGOTIABLE INSTRUMENTS ACT, 1881 (XXVI OF 1881)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—138</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In order to make a person liable under section 138 the payee of a cheque has to give a notice in writing to the drawer of the cheque within 15 days of the receipt of information by him from the Bank regarding the return of the cheque as unpaid in terms of clause (b) to the proviso. The drawer of the cheque is obliged to make the payment within 15 days of the receipt of the said notice failing which the cause of action for prosecution will arise under clause (c).</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>S.M. Anwar Hossain Vs Md. Shafiqul Alam (Chand) and another, 19BLD(AD)166</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—138 and 141</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—561A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The cheque in question was issued by the appellant on 21.12.1995 which was presented for encashment on 23.12.1995 but it was dishonoured on the same day whereupon the complainant issued notice to the appellant on 24.12.1995 for payment of money for which the cheque was issued following the clause (b) to the proviso to section 138 of the Act. On receipt of the said notice the appellant in order to avoid payment fraudulently informed the complainant through a lawyer on 4.1.1996 that he had lost the cheque written in the complainants name and made a GD Entry in that behalf. The cause of action for prosecution will arise under clause (c) of the proviso to section 138 on the failure of the appellant to pay the amount within 15 days of the receipt of the notice of the complainant. Relying on the complainant’s own case it is contended on behalf of the appellant the accused-appellant must be fixed with notice for payment at least from 4.1.1996 and after the expiry of 15 days from that date i.e; from 19.1.1996 the cause of action should be taken to have arisen due to non-payment within the said period and complaint was required to be filed within one month from 19.1.1996 in compliance with clause (b) of section 141 of the Act. Admittedly the petition of complaint was filed long after that date i.e., on 18.4.1996 and thus cognizance could not be taken upon such complaint. The subsequent allegations will not save the limitation because the requirement under the law is that the complaint has to be filed within one month of the date on which the cause of action arises under clause (c) of the proviso to section 138 of the Act and hence the impugned proceeding is quashed.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>S.M. Anwar Hossain Vs Md. Shafiqul Alum (Chand) and another, 19BLD(AD)166</em></strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>NEGOTIABLE INSTRUMENTS (AMENDMENT) ACT, 1994 (XIX OF 1994)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—138 and 141</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dishonour of cheque for insufficiency of fund and complaint by the payee</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Penal Code, 1860 (XLV of 1860)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—420</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cheating</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 141 of the Negotiable Instruments(Amendment) Act puts a bar on taking cognizance of an offence punishable under section 138 thereof except upon a complaint in writing made by the payee or the holder of the cheque. Section 138 of the Negotiable Instruments (Amendment) Act is for dishonour of a cheque simpliciter for insufficiency of fund whereas an offence under section 420 of the Penal Code is for cheating a person. The two offences are two distinct offences under two different laws. The rule of law about the peremptory application of the special law in preference to the general law for trial of an offence under section 420 of the Penal Code has no application in the instant case. The learned Magistrate committed no illegality in taking cognizance of the case under section 420 of the Penal Code. The institution of a money suit for realisation of the money in question does not debar the prosecution from proceeding with the criminal case.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Nurul Islam Vs. The State, 17 BLD (HCD)498</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27DLR(AD)175: 46DLR(AD)180— Cited</p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1070	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">PENAL CODE, 1860</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>   14BLD(HCD)204,  19BLD(HCD)607, 15BLD(HCD)53, 14BLD (HCD)401, 19BLD (HCD)225, 14BLD(HCD)178, 14BLD(HCD)391, 15 BLD (HCD)133, 16BLD(HCD)91, 14BLD(AD)178, 17BLD(HCD)352,19BLD(HCD)303,13BLD (AD)11 7, 20BLD(HCD)372, 14BLD(HCD)308, 16BLD(HCD) 140,  20BLD(HCD)</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  PENAL CODE</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PENAL CODE, 1860</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>(XLV OF 1860)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—1</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It lays down that the Penal Code extends only to offences committed in Bangladesh and not to offences committed outside Bangladesh.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Haque Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)204</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—21, Clause 12</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Principal of a private College is not a public servant</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Clause 12 of section 21 of the Penal Code provides that every person in the service or pay of the Government or remunerated by the Government by fees or commission for the performance of public duty is a public servant.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Public duty is one which is created and conferred by law by which an individual is vested with some portion of the sovereign function of the Government to be exercised by him for the performance of the duty for the term and tenure prescribed by law. There is no such law in the instant case and as such the petitioner cannot be said to perform public duty and called a public servant.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Matiur Rahman Vs The State, 19BLD(HCD)607</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 30DLR(SC)127; PLD1964 Dhaka 330; A1R1957(SC)13 and A1R1918 Lahore 1 52—distinguishable</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—34</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Common Intention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">This section-does not create any distinct offence. It is intended to meet a case where the members of a party acted in furtherance of the common intention of all but it was difficult to prove exactly the part played by each of them. It means that if two or more persons intentionally do a thing jointly, it is just the same as if each of them had done it individually ‘Common intention’ within the meaning of this section pre-supposes a prior concert. There must be a prior meeting of the minds leading to a pre-arranged plan to commit an offence. The common intention to commit the offence invites the application of section 34 of the Penal Code. In offences involving physical violence, the presence of the accused at the scene of the occurrence renders him liable on the principle of joint liability but where the offence consists of diverse acts and it may be committed at different times, the presence of the accused at the scene of the occurrence is not necessary.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Tajul Islam and 8 others, 15BLD(HCD)53</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref: </strong>44DLR 83; A.I.R.l957(SC)381; 16 DLR(SC) 94; 16 DLR (Dhaka) 189; 29DLR (SC)271; A.I.R. 1978 (SC) 1248; A.I. R. 1939 (Cal) 65; A.I.R. 1929 (Bombay) 327; PLD 1957 (West Pakistan) Lahore, 956; A.LR. 1946 (Sind) 43; 12DLR (SC) 217; 27 DLR (AD) 29; 44 DLR (AD) 10; A.I.R. 1952 (SC) 474; A.1.R. 1976 (SC) 2027—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—34</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under section 34 of the Penal Code the essence of joint liability is to be found in the existence of a common intention animating the accused in the doing of a criminal act in furtherance of such intention. Before application of this section to a case, it must be shown: (a) a criminal act was done by several persons, (b) all the accused intended the commission of the offence and (c) the criminal act was done in furtherance of the common intention of all.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Kalam Azad Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)401</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—34 and 149</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sections 34 and 149 of the Penal Code are two distinct and separate offences with different ingredients. Section 34 of the Penal Code involves a direct overt act on the part of the accused sharing a ‘common intention’ with others for the commission of an offence while section 149 of the Penal Code is essentially a vicarious liability for being a member of an unlawful assembly with the ‘common object’ of committing the offences. These two offences are of different nature.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abu Talukder Vs The State, 19BLD (HCD)225</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—34 and 149</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Common intention is an intention to commit the crime actually committed and each accused person can be convicted if he shared the common intention.
