The common intention contemplated by this section is anterior to the commission of the crime and it does not refer to the time when the offence is actually committed.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">A person cannot be found guilty under section 148 of the Penal Code unless he carried with him a dangerous weapon. A general statement that the accused persons were armed with dangerous weapons like dhal, katra, lathi and sorki is not sufficient to warrant a conviction under this section.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Nurul Haque Matbar and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)178</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—34 and 149</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 34 applies in a case where a criminal act is done by two or more persons in furtherance of the common intention of all while section 149 applies in the case of a member of an unlawful assembly when a criminal act is committed by any member of the unlawful assembly in prosecution of the common object of that assembly.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ataur Rahman and others Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)391</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR 157—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—99 and 100</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Right of private defence of person even extends to causing of death when there is a reasonable apprehension that the intended assault by the aggressor would cause death or grievous hurt. But no right of private defence of person is available against an unarmed man.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dalim and another Vs. The State, 15 BLD (HCD)133</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—100</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Right of private defence—When it extends to causing death?</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the accused had scuffles with the deceased sometime before the occurrence and the fear of retaliation from the deceased party overpowers the mind of the accused, it is not possible for him, whose mental excitement can be better imagined than described, to weigh the position in golden scales. In such a situation when he is faced with assaults from his rival party it is not unnatural that he would strike a decisive blow to defend himself and to free himself from the clutches of his adversaries. In the instant case, accused Ruhul Amin gave only one knife blow to deceased Moktar Ali and then ran away. The attending circumstances indicate that he gave the knife blow only to free himself from the grip of deceased Moktar Ali and ran away for safety. This conduct of the accused satisfies the legal requirement of the right of private defence. The accused can not be said to have exceeded the right of self-defence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Ruhul Amin Mondal Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)91</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—109</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Abetment</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Abatement is an offence under the Penal Code and a person may be charged for abetting an offence punishable under a special law even though the word ‘abetment may not be mentioned as an offence under the Special Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Hussain Mohammad Ershad, former President Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD)178</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 44 DLR (AD) 215; 33DLR 379; 17 DLR (SC) 261—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—120B</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal Conspiracy</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Assembly at Bangabhaban on the occasion of oath taking ceremony took place after the occurrence of the previous night and the presence of the petitioner at Bangabhaban on the following day cannot by itself be a sufficient ground for even an inference for a criminal conspiracy. Her jubilation might be her husband but for that it cannot be said that she was in the conspiracy the result of moral support in the activities of her husband but for that it cannot be said that she was in the conspiracy.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mrs. Jobaida Rashid Vs The State, 17BLD(HCD)352</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> ‘Principles and Digest of the Law of Evidence’—By M. Monir; Queen Vs. Blake, 1844 6QB 126; 17 BLD (1997) 11; 17 BLD (AD)( 1997)54; 17BLD(AD)( 1997)163:2 BLC (AD) (1997) 75—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—141, fifth clause and 143</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">If an assembly of five or more persons takes a decision to observe Hartal by themselves and their associates, then the decision does not come under the mischief of fifth clause of section 141 of the Penal Code. This decision does not contemplate holding of any procession or picket or any activity or activities to implement the decision. But if an assembly of five or more persons takes the decision to observe hartal to be participated by the people at large so that their common object is to compel others obviously by show of criminal force to do what they are not legally bound to do, then the said assembly must be an unlawful assembly according to fifth clause of section 141 of the Penal Code and the members of that unlawful assembly are liable to be punished under section 143 of the Penal Code. Consequently the processions or other activities in support of or to force such hartal shall be unlawful assemblies. Similarly every assembly of five persons or more to protest or to oppose hartal shall be an unlawful assembly. Activities of the members of these assemblies shall be cognizable offences according to their behaviour under the relevant sections contained in Chapter VIII of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Mr Md Zillur Rahman and ors., 19BLD(HCD)303</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—149</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">For applying section 149 of the Penal Code against an accused, three conditions must be fulfilled: (a) the accused must have been a member of the unlawful assembly at the time the offence was committed; (b) the offence must have been committed in prosecution of the common object, or (c) the offence must be such as the members of the assembly knew likely to be committed in prosecution of that object.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Before applying section 149, the Court must have indubitable evidence that the members of the unlawful assembly constituted the statutory number of five, though some of them might not have been named, or identified, or brought to trial.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Rafiqul Islam Vs. The State, 13BLD (AD)11 7</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A.I.R.1953 SC 364; 1969 SCMR 537; AIR 1960 SC(290); AIR 1963 SC 174; AIR 1974 Sc 1567; A1R1975 SC 1917 (1921 and 1922); AIR 1978 sc 1233—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—188</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It was obligatory on the part of the learned Magistrate to make a written complaint alleging the nature of the order made by him which was alleged to have disobeyed by accused and the manner of violation in order to form an opinion that accused persons have committed an offence punishable under section 188 of the Penal Code</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Ahad @ Md Abdul Ahad Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)372</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—201</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To sustain a charge U/s 201 of the Penal Code it is essential to prove that an offence has been committed and that the accused knew or had reason to believe that an offence has been committed and with the requisite knowledge and intent to screen the offenders from legal punishment causes the evidence thereof to disappear or gives false information in respect of such offence, knowing or having reason to believe the same to be false.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Khandkar Md. Moniruzzaman Vs.The State, 14BLD(HCD)308</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 35 DLR (AD) 127; 18 DLR(SC)289; 15 DLR (SC) 150; AIR 1952 (SC) 354; 34 DLR238; 45DLR386; 30DLR58; 1984 B.C.R. 231; P.L.D. 1978 Lahore 1285—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—295A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Deliberate and malicious acts, either spoken or written, or by visible representation intended to outrage religious feelings of any class of citizens constitute an offence under section 295A of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Homeo Baba Jahangir Beiman –al-Shuresari Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD) 140</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 40 DLR 359, 1993 B.L.D. 45; 7 DLR (WP) 17 (F.B); 8 DLR (FC) 110; S.C.C. Vol.IV, 213—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—295A</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Deliberate and malicious acts intended to outrage religious feelings of any class by insulting its religion or religious beliefs</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 295A of the Code on its language is applicable to those insults to religious beliefs which in addition to being deliberate and malicious are intended to outrage the religious feelings of the followers of that religion.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">After going through the publication very carefully and meticulously as to its entirity the High Court Division held that the same has not been written or published with any intention to hit the religious feeling or sentiments of the Muslims, rather, it was written against the narrow interpretation or distorted meaning given or spread out in our country, specially, by less educated and half educated fanatic religious Mollas and Islamic Fotowabaj. Reading of the entire caption and publication establishes that its inner or real meaning is not at all intended to hit the feeling of any Muslim or to distort the meaning of the said Sura of the Holy Quaran.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shamsuddin Ahmed and others Vs The State and another, 20BLD(HCD)268</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> “Jesus In Heaven on earth” and Working Muslim Mission and Literary Trust, Lahore, and of Civil and Military Gazette, Limited Vs. The Crown, 7DLR(W.P.C. Lahore) 17—relied</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—299 and 300</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere killing of a person or mere causing of a person’s death is not murder or a culpable homicide but it is so when caused with certain guilty intention or guilty knowledge. Three classes of cases have been described in section 299 as “culpable homicide” and four classes of cases have been described in section 300 as “murder”. The essential difference between mere “culpable homicide” and “murder” is the degree of probability of causing death. When death is probable, it is culpable homicide but when death is most probable, it is murder.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Ashraf Ali and others, 14BLD (AD)127</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—300 and 304 Part—I</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When it is clear from the evidence that the free fight between the parties took place following an altercation it stands out that death of the victim was caused without any premeditation in a sudden fight in the heat of passion and without the offenders having taken undue advantage or acted in a cruel or unusual manner. This attracts Exception 4 of section 300 of the Penal Code and brings the case U/s 304 part 1.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Kalam Azad Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)401</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> PLD1950 (Lahore)90; 14BLD (1994) 33; 40DLR443—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—302</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In a case where two persons have been murdered at dead of night, it is but natural to inform the police first about the occurrence. Non-mentioning of any name in the F.I.R. rings a truth in the F.I.R.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shahjahan Sardar and others Vs. The State 13BLD(AD)58</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—302</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In case where allegation had been made that a husband had murdered his wife, whether the husband had a duty to explain how and by whom she was murdered—the Public Prosecutor, whether is supposed to know the law and has a responsibility to work with devotion.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In case where the allegations had been that a husband had murdered his wife and then absconded, the husband in such a situation had a duty to explain how his wife was murdered and by whom she was murdered and in case of non-explanation by the husband or his silence in the matter or he having absconded immediately after the murder, would be considered to be a good ground for a finding that the husband is guilty of murder of his wife if, however, there is no suggestion or circumstances to show to the contrary that other inmates of the house also used to beat her and killing her in the process.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">(ii) The Public Prosecutor is supposed to know the law and has a responsibility to work with devotion keeping in mind that he is representing not a party but the people in the administration of criminal justice.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Nurul Huq 13BLD (HCD)99</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 172 I.C.374; 39C.L.J 123; AIR 1977 (SC)1116; 126 I.C.689; 62 I.C. 545; P.L.D. 1964 (SC)813; 31DLR 312; 16 D.L.R (Dacca) 598; A.I.R 1973(SC)2474—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—302</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Circumstantial evidence—</strong>Chain of circumstances wanting—presumption that deceased was last found in the company of accused—whether the accused is the killer of the deceased.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The circumstantial evidence found against the accused is incapable of explanation on any reasonable theory except that of the guilt of the accused his persons.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Accused presumed to be innocent of the charge till guilt is established by legal evidence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Principle to be followed in criminal case based on circumstantial evidence.</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is the fundamental principle of criminal jurisprudence that circumstantial evidence should point inevitably to the conclusion that the accused and accused only was the perpetrator of the offence and such evidence should be incompatible with the innocence of the accused.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Last seen theory—</strong>in the absence of any eye-witness to the murder and in the absence of any positive evidence that appellant Malai was found, following deceased Siddique Ali with sharp cutting weapons in hand and in the absence of any overt act on the part of the deceased it cannot be said with reasonable certainty that appellant Malai was responsible for the murder of deceased Siddique Ali</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Litigation—</strong>litigation existing between the accused and some of the witnesses is not enough to bear grudge by the accused so as to commit the offence of murder, rather such litigation sometime can be taken as a cause for false implication.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Malai Miah Vs. The State (1993) 13BLD (HCD)277</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—302</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When admittedly a wife sleeps at night with the husband in a room or hut not approachable by others, whether the husband is rightly convicted under section 302 of the Penal Code on the basis of indubitable evidence on record?</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When admittedly a wife sleeps at night with the husband in a room or hut which is not approachable by others and there is no probable circumstance explaining the cause of death of the wife and she is found to have been killed by in a brutal manner by strangulation, the husband is rightly convicted under section 302 of the Penal Code on the basis of indubitable evidence on record against him.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Hamid @ Sofaruddin Vs. The State, 13BLD(HCD)563</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 38DLR235; 40 DLR(AD) 139—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—302</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Motive</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When there is sufficient direct evidence to prove an offence, motive is immaterial and has no importance. While trying a case under section 302 of the Penal Code or hearing an appeal involving section 302, the Court must not consider first the motive of the murder, because motive is a matter of speculation and it rests in the mind and special knowledge of the accused persons. Motive is not a necessary ingredient of an offence under section 302 of the Penal Code. The Court will see if sufficient direct evidence is there or not. If not, motive may be a matter for consideration, specially when the case is based on circumstantial evidence. <strong>[Per Mustafa Kamal, J]</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Giasuddin and others, 18 BLD(AD)254</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—302</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Plea of alibi</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a wife killing case it is always presumed that the husband was with the deceased-wife at the time of occurrence, unless any alibi is set up by the defence. In that case the burden of proving such plea rests on the husband in order to absolve him of any criminal liability.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Salam Vs The State, 19BLD (HCD)98</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—302</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since the sentence prescribed under section 302 of the Penal Code is death or imprisonment for life, the Court before recording a conviction must be satisfied beyond reasonable doubts about the guilt of the accused persons on careful scrutiny of the evidence on record. A conviction even on grave suspicion and high probability is not tenable in law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Jiaur Rahman Vs. The State, 15BLD (HCD)459</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—302</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sentence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sentence is a complex matter which needs special considerations in the context of proved facts. In the instant case the broad facts that stare at the face are that there were hot altercations and exchange of hot words between the parties immediately preceding the occurrence and there was grappling by Salam and 2 others on one side and victim Jalal on the other and in the course of such quarrel and on the hit of passion condemned prisoner Abdul Aziz Mina inflected dagger blows on the victim. Under such circumstances, it is to be found that the condemned prisoner had no premeditation for killing victim Jalal and he acted on the heat of passion. The sentence of death is therefore commuted to a sentence of imprisonment for life.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Abdul Aziz Mina, 16BLD(HCD)183</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—302/109</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the fact that the condemned prisoner did not inflict any injury on victim Hazera, although he was a silent spectator to the cruel and gruesome murder of his wife by his companions, who were acquitted for want of legal evidence, it is reasonable to hold that he could not be convicted under section 302 of the Penal Code but should be found guilty for abetment under sections 302/109 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Awal Vs. The State, 14BLD(AD)224 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—302134</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Evidence Act, 1872(1 of 1872)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—3</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is complete chain of circumstances that the appellants assaulted deceased victim Biswajit severely and dealt fatal blow causing his death when appellant Guizar participated in the occurrence most actively and he was found by PW4 for the last time with the deceased victim when Gulzar was chasing by the eastern side of the khal and the circumstances of the case taken cumulatively are forming a claim so complete that there is no escaped from the conclusion that the murder of victim Biswajit was committed by the appellant Gulzar and his associates and none else.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Guizar Biswas and others Vs The State,20BLD(HCD)550</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—302/34</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The wife of the deceased deposed in Court that she had recognised the assailants of her husband and accused Akkel Ali gave channy blow, Delwar gave dao blow, accused Omar Ali gave Lathi blow and accused Quasem gave rifle blow on her husband who succumbed to the injuries on 15.6.1989 in the hospital which is corroborated by PWs. 1,2, 3, 4, 6 and 8 and the dying declaration and there is nothing to disbelieve the credibility of their evidences and hence the prosecution proved the case beyond all reasonable doubt and therefore the conviction and sentences under section 302/34 of the Penal Code against the condemned convict is sustainable.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Akkel Ali and ors, 20BLD (HCD)484</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—302/34 and 201/34</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Non-recovery of the deadbody</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Even in a case of non-recovery of the deadbody of a victim a conviction can be secured for an offence of murder under section 302 of the Penal Code if there be legal and sufficient evidence on record to prove the commission of murder by the accused. In the face of clear evidence of eye-witnesses proving murder of the victim by the accused by inflicting assaults on his person and the subsequent removal of the deadbody by the accused persons for the purpose of causing disappearance thereof, Conviction of the appellant under sections 302/34 of the Penal Code is justified. There is no warrant of law altering the charge from section 302 to section 364 of the Penal Code merely because the dead body was not recovered.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shaha and others Vs The State 17BLD (AD)241</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—302 and 302/149</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A conviction under section 302 of the Penal Code and a conviction under sections 302/149 of the Penal Code are different kinds of conviction as section 302 of the Penal Code involves direct and personal liability of the accused whereas sections 302/149 of the Penal Code involve a vicarious liability. The trial Court convicted the accused-appellant under sections 302/149 of the Penal Code and as such the High Court Division was wrong in affirming the order of conviction and sentence under section 302 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Altaf Hossain Vs The State, 18BLD (AD)231</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—302, 304 and 326</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The line of demarcation between culpable homicide and grievous hurt is rather thin. In the former case injury must be such as is likely to cause death whereas in the latter case that is likely to endanger life. Offence of culpable homicide pre-supposes an intention or knowledge of likelihood of causing death. In the absence of such intention or knowledge the offence committed may be a grievous hurt notwithstanding death being caused.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Humayun Matubbar Vs The State, 18 BLD (HCD)492</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—304 Part II</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">On scrutiny and careful analysis of the evidence of the P.Ws it appears that accused Shamsul Huq did not foresee that throwing of brick towards victim Kastura Bibi would cause death to her. He had no intention to cause death or to cause such bodily injury as was likely to cause death. Victim sustained violent blow on the abdominal wall by the brick thrown towards her chest and got senseless but death occurred when accused Abdul Hoque (since deceased) pressed her on the neck resulting in her spontaneous death. Death would not have occurred if accused Abdul Hoque would not have played the part of pressing the victim on her neck. Accused Shamsul Huq had no premeditated intention to kill the victim and the intention to kill is lacking in the instant case. Although, he had no intention to kill the victim, it must be held that he had the knowledge that such throwing of brick was likely to cause her death and the act done by him was both rash and indiscreet and as such accused Shamsul Huq is liable for the commission of an offence punishable under section 304 Part II of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Jabbar and another Vs. The State, 18BLD(HCD)109</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 5BLD(AD)198; 6BLD(AD)318; 10 BLD (AD)210; 5BLD 129; 8BLD 129; 48DLR 446—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—363 and 361</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Custody of a victim girl, if the allegations are that of kidnapping of a minor girl out of the keeping of the lawful guardian.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Kidnapping of a minor girl out of the keeping of the lawful guardian is an offence under Section 363 of the Penal Code. For the purpose of custody of the victim girl as may be prayed for in the criminal Court in a pending proceeding, the Court has to proceed on the basis that the female is a minor under sixteen years of age as laid down in section 361 of the Penal Code. For proving the offence of kidnapping the minority of the victim will have to be established at the trial.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Wahed Ali Dewan Vs. The State and another, 14BLD(AD)32</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—376</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The offence committed by accused appellant finds corroboration from the medical report, namely the report of P.W. 1 who found marks of violence on the person of the victim and opined that this is a case of rape. The evidence of the witnesses has clearly established that accused appellant committed the crime of rape on victim Kazal Rekha. The learned Judge has rightly found the accused guilty of the offence under section 376 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Tofazzal Hossain Khan Vs The State, 17 BLD(HCD)306</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—376</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Cruelty To Woman (Determent Punishment) Ordinance, 1983 (LX of 1983)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—4(b)(c)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The High Court Diyision on consideration of evidence of PWs rightly found that accused-petitioner Bazlu raped victim Mahinur Begum and that it was Bazlu who wanted to marry the victim girl Mahinur Begum and as such the finding of conviction and sentence of the High Court Division are based on proper appreciation of evidence on record.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bazlu Talukder Vs The State, 20 BLD (AD)227</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—379</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When growing of the case crops by the complainant and the cutting and dishonestly and taking away of the same by the accused are proved, the accused is guilty of theft under section 379 of the Penal Code. In such circumstances, no plea of bonafide claim of right to the case land can save the accused from the criminal liability.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Motaleb Sardar and others Vs. The State and another, 19BLD(HCD)407</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—379</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Theft and bonafide claim of right</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is well-settled that a co-sharer in exclusive possession of a parcel of land is entitled to maintain his possession therein to the exclusion of his co-sharers until the ejmali property is partitioned by metes and bounds but before effecting such partition the co-sharers or co-owners out of possession have no right to disturb his possession, not to speak of cutting and taking away crops grown by him. In such a case the bonafide claim of right in the land cannot extend to cutting and taking away crops grown by a co-sharer in possession. Bonafide or contested claim of right is no defence against a charge under section 379 of the Penal Code for cutting and taking away crops grown by the co-sharer in possession.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nasiruddin Shah and others Vs Nazrul Islam and others, 18BLD(HCD)634</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—392</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Punishment for robbery</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—394</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Punishment for voluntarily causing hurt in committing robbery</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the fact that the two appellants and some other unidentified persons illegally confined the informant shop-keeper and others in the shop and by curtailing their liberty forcibly looted away valuable goods from their possession but without causing hurt to anybody, the appellants committed the offence punishable under section 392 of the Penal Code not under section 394.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gohar Ali and another Vs. The State 16BLD(HCD)398</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—394</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Non-examination of investigating officer does not always prejudice the accused</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Admittedly the appellants remained in abscondence during the whole trial and in such a situation the non-examination of the investigating officer cannot be said to have caused any prejudice to the appellants.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gohar Ali and another Vs. The State 16BLD (HCD)398</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—395</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Dacoity is usually committed at dead hours of the night and in that view of the matter the means of recognition is of vital importance. The Court is to scrutinise the evidence of witnesses with great care and caution in respect of recognition of the accused persons.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Where the informant’s testimony remains uncorroborated and the identity of the accused is not established beyond reasonable doubts, it is unsafe to convict the accused persons.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kuti alias Bellal and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)9</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—395</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In a case of dacoity committed in the darkness of night the question of recognition is of vital importance. When the only eye witness to the occurrence does not state in his evidence about the means of recognition of the alleged dacoits, the order of conviction cannot be sustained.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Subal Chandra Das Vs. The State, 15 BLD (HCD) 121</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—396</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In absence of any evidence of dacoity by any of the witness and in absence of any recover of any article taken away during the dacoity from the possession of any of the accused person it can be safely said that the prosecution has hopelessly failed to prove the case of dacoity and therefore the charge under section 396 P.C. must fail on the ground of absence of evidence to prove any of the ingredient of section 391 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs Md. Abdul Ali and others Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)327</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—405</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere inability to pay back a sum of money entrusted, the sale proceeds of a property entrusted, whether will establish the fact of criminal misappropriation?</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The expression ‘property’ occurring in section 405 of the Criminal Procedure Code should not be given a narrow construction. Blank forms of tickets are also property when the same are converted into tickets after sale.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere delay in payment of money entrusted to a person, when there was no particular obligation to pay at a certain date, does not amount to misappropriation.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere inability to pay back a sum of money entrusted, the sale proceeds of a property entrusted will not establish the fact of criminal misappropriation, if mens rea is not established. The prosecution must also establish, apart m entrustment that the accused had dishonestly misappropriated the property entrusted.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.H.M. Siddique Vs. The State 13BLD (HCD)85</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 9DLR(SC) 14; 3lCr.L.J.(1930)1198; A.I.R.( 1940)330;AIR(1976)(SC) 1966—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—406 and 420</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The question of offence of cheating, whether arises when there is nothing to show that any entrustment of property was made to the accused?</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The question of offence of the cheating does not arise (in the instant case) as there is nothing to show that the accused has dishonestly induced the complainant to sell the fish to him on credit. There is nothing to show that any entrustment of the fish was made to the accused for sale of fish on credit according to the direction of the person making the entrustment</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Islam Ali Mia alias Md.Islam Vs. Ama! Chandra Mondal and another, 13BLD (AD)28</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—406/420</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is no specific promise for payment by any specific date and as such the High Court Division did not find any existence of initial intention for deception on the part of the accused petitioner. What happened between them was in due course of normal and regular business transaction for which no criminal action lies. At best the informant may go for civil action against the accused petitioner.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mohiuddin Md Abdul Kader Vs The State and another, 20BLD(HCD)499</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 18BLD(AD)289; 49DLR(AD)180— Not applicable</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—406/420</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The allegations made in the petition of complaint it clearly shows that the petitioner had initial intention to deceive the complainant and thereby misappropriated the money. So, it cannot be said that it is a case of civil nature. The petition of complaint undoubtedly discloses criminal offence against the accused-petitioner. The Appellate Division held that the High Court Division rightly refused the prayer for quashing the proceeding.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abu Baker Siddique Vs. The State &amp; anr, 18BLD (AD)289</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—406/420</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Dishonouring of the cheque itself does not constitute the offence of cheating.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">As regards the argument that the accused- petitioner issued a cheque knowing fully well that he had no money in the account and that conduct amongst to cheating we are of the view that dishonouring of the cheque itself does not constitute the offence of cheating.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Motaleb Hossain Vs The State and another, 20BLD(HCD)573</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> Md. Asaduzzaman Vs. Salamatullah, 1 9BLD( 1999) (HCD)46 1—relied.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—406/420</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The alleged transaction in between the complainant and the appellant is clearly and admittedly a business transaction. The appellant had already paid a part of the money under the contract to the complainant. The failure on the part of the appellant to pay the complainant the balance amount under the bill does not warrant any criminal proceeding as the obligation under the contract is of civil nature. The High Court Division were not justified in refusing to quash the proceeding in question although the transaction in question between the parties is clearly of a civil nature.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dewan Obaidur Rahman Vs. The State and anr, 19BLD (AD)128</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref: </strong>45DLR(AD)27—relied upon</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—409</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Awarding sentence of fine along with sentence of imprisonment for life, whether can be said to be illegal.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Awarding sentence of fine alongwith imprisonment for life cannot is not illegal in view of the said provisions of section 409 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.M.A. Wazedul Islam Vs. The State 13BLD (HCD)296</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—409</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The word “banker” used is section 409 of the Penal Code, whether has been used in the technical sense of the Banking Companies Act.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Held:</strong> The word “Banker”occurring in section 409 of the Penal Code has not been used in the technical sense of the Banking Companies Act but it signifies any person who discharges any of the functions of the customary business of banking. The word also includes a firm or company that carries on such business.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mustafizur Rahman Vs. The State and others, 13BLD(HCD)287</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1960 Cr. L.J. 188; 44 DLR(AD) 192; 23DLR(SC)4 1—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—409</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Mere delay in payment entrusted to a person, whether misappropriation?</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere delay in payment of money entrusted to a person, when there was no particular obligaton to pay at a certain date, does not amount to misappropriation.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.H.M. Siddique Vs. The State, 13BLD (HCD)85</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—409</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In section 409 of the Penal Code there is no provision for confiscation of property. Yet the Appellate Division refused to consider the prayer of the petitioner at this stage as this point was not specifically raised before the High Court Division.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bibhuti Bushan Talukder Vs The State, 17BLD(AD) 168</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—409</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere retention of money by the accused for some time without actual use for which it was meant or mere delay in disbursement of money due from him, if properly explained, does not constitute an offence under section 409 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.K.M. Hafizuddin Vs. The State, 15 BLD(HCD)234</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—409</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The ingredients of section 409 of the Code are misappropriation to commit criminal breach of trust in respect of property over which he had dominion as public servant. The appellant had no criminal intention to commit such criminal breach of trust in respect of the property which was held within his dominion, rather it shows his bonafide intention to help one of the customers of the Bank in tiding over his financial difficulties and as such the appellant is entitled to acquittal as of right.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.K.M. Mohiuddin Vs The State, 20 BLD(HCD)172</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—411</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Dishonestly retaining or receiving stolen property</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In order to sustain a conviction under section 411 of the Code the prosecution must prove affirmatively by reliable evidence that the accused had exclusive possession and effective control or domain over the stolen property or he received or retained the same knowing or having reason to believe it to be a stolen property.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Afsar Ali Pramanik Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)356</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—415</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Simple dishonouring of a cheque itself is not cheating</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To constitute an offence of cheating as described in section 415 of the Penal Code, there must be a specific allegation that the accused had initial intention to deceive the complainant. It is also true that such intention can be gathered from the facts and circumstances of a because such intention normally is concealed in the mind and is not expressed. If there are allegations that goods were delivered on credit on specific promise of repayment within a specific date but the payment was not made within the specific time, it may be inferred that there was initial intention of deception.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Asaduzzaman Vs Md Salamatullah, 19BLD(HCD)461</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 46 DLR (AD) 180; 30 DLR 327; 27 DLR (AD) 175; 1954 Crl.L.J. (SC) 1806; 10 BLD (AD) 168: 42DLR(AD)240—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—415</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is a settled principle that the initial intention to deceive must be established to justify a conviction for cheating. The intention is to be gathered from the surrounding circumstances.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Arifur Rahman alias Bablu Vs. Shantosh Kumar Sadhu and another, 14 BLD(AD) 78 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—420</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To sustain a charge of cheating, the prosecution must prove the initial intention of the accused to deceive.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Karim Vs Shamsul Alam and another, 14BLD(HCD)167</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 42 DLR (AD) 31; 27 DLR (AD)175; 36 DLR (AD) 14—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—420</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Whenever a loan is taken by one from another on a representation to repay the same dishonestly inducing the person to lend money having no intention to repay, whether it will be an offence of cheating punishable under section 420 B.P.C. Intention of a person, whether can only be gathered from his conduct?</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Whenever a loan is taken by one from another on a representation to repay the same dishonestly inducing the person to lend the money, having no intention to repay the same, it will be an offence of cheating as defined under section 415 and to be punished under section 420 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Intention of a person can only be gathered from his conduct at the time of the occurrence and the surrounding circumstances.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Shafiuddin Khan Vs. The State &amp; another, 13BLD(HCD)362</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 13 CWN, 728; 10 DLR 325, 26 DLR 146; 27 DLR (AD) 175; 7 BLD 164—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—420</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Dishonouring of the cheque itself cannot be considered as an ingredient of the offence of cheating unless there is evidence to show that after issuing it he has done something more to defraud the payee. Even such a cheque issued with the knowledge that he has not such amount in the Bank account at the moment it will not amount to cheating if he has intention to deposit the money before the cheque is presented for encashment. Mere dishonouring of the cheques itself is not an ingredient of cheating.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mohiuddin Md Abdul Kader Vs The State and another, 20BLD(HCD)499</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">19BLD(HCD)46 1—relied</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—441/447</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal trespass</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It provides that whoever enters into or upon property in the possession of another with intent to commit an offence or to intimidate, insult or annoy any person in possession of such property, or having lawfully entered into or upon such property, unlawfully remains there with intent thereby to intimidate, insult or annoy any such person, or with intent to commit an offence, is said to commit “criminal trespass”.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case the dominant intention of the appellant was to annoy the complainant who was in possession of the case land. The complainant might not be present at the time of the illegal entry but he came to the scene thereafter and opposed the appellant who, despite his protest, carried on the work of construction. So the ingredients of section 441/447 of the Penal Code have been well-established.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Mohammad Ali Member Vs Abdul Fazul Mia Md. Mazedul Huq and another, 19BLD (AD)260</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—447</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal trespass</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In view of the fact that theft of the case crop by the accused by the cutting and taking away of the same out of the possession of the complainant and damaging some crops in the course of the same transaction necessarily involves their entry into the case land and the accused are punished for the said offences, a separate conviction under section 447 of the Penal Code is wholly unwarranted. Moreover, in the absence of any finding by the trial Court on the intention or the object of the illegal trespass of the accused, conviction of the accused under sections 447 or 448 of the Penal Code is not maintainable in law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Motaleb Sardar and others Vs. The State and another, 19BLD(HCD)407</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—471</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">For the offence under section 471 of the Penal Code an accused can be punished, as provided in section 465 of the Penal Code, upto 2 years rigorous imprisonment or with fine or with both. The imposition of 4 years rigorous imprisonment under section 471 of the Penal Code is not sustainable in law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abul Hossain Mollah alias Abu Mollah Vs The State, 17BLD(AD)170</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—493</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A mere promise of marriage made by the accused to a woman or to her guardian intending never to fulfil his promise does not warrant a conclusion that a false belief was caused in her mind that she was the lawfully married wife of the accused.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Makhan alias Putu Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)122</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 34 DLR 366—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—499</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Eighth Exception</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Complaint made by the petitioners in good faith to the Hon’ble Chief Justice regarding the conduct of the complainant judicial officer is not defamation as the same is covered by 8th exception of section 499 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abdun Noor and others Vs The State and another, 18BLD(HCD)624</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—499</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ninth Exception—Defamation</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Bringing anything which is unjust or improper to the notice of the public at large is certainly for the public good. In the instant case, since the alleged offending imputation was made at a press conference by a person who has not been made an accused in the case and the matter was earlier published in other newspapers and the present publication was made in good faith, the offending publication per see satisfies the requirement of Ninth Exception to section 499 of the Penal Code and as such further continuation of the impugned proceeding amounts tà an abuse of the process of the Court and the same is quashed.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Syed Mohammad Afzal Hossain Vs. S.M. Selim Idris, 15BLD(HCD)362</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref</strong>. 19DLR (SC) 198—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—500</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To constitute defamation, the offending words spoken must contain imputation concerning a person intending to harm or knowing or having reason to believe that such imputation will harm the reputation of such a person. Explanation 4 of section 499 of Penal Code provides that no imputation harms a person’s reputation unless that imputation lowers the moral or intellectual character of that person in the estimation of others.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>A.K.M. Enamul Haque Vs. Md. Mizanur Rahman and others, 14BLD(HCD) 201</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—599/500</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Imputations, if made by a lawyer iii the discharge of his or her professional duty on the character of any person in good faith, whether will constitute any offence of defamation—A lawyer while acting under the instructions of his or her client, whether is entitled to special protection?</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Imputation, if any, made by a lawyer, in the discharge of his or her professional duty, on the character of any person, in good faith and for protection of the interest of the person making it or of any other person or for the public good will not constitute any offence of defamation.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">A lawyer, while acting under the instructions of his or her client and proceeding professionally, has a qualified privilege; while acting as such, the lawyer does not, if not otherwise implicated, come within the ambit of defamation unless and until there is an express malice on his or her part; and mere knowledge in such cases can not be equated with express malice or malice in fact. The privilege enjoyed by the lawyer is only a qualified privilege and he will not come within the bounds of the offence of defamation unless and until there is an “express malice” or malice in fact on his or her part.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">An Advocate is entitled to special protection, when is called in question in respect of defamatory statements made by him or her in course of his or her duties as an Advocate. The Court ought to presume that he or she acted in good faith upon instructions of the clients and ought to require the party to prove the express malice.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mrs. Sigma Huda @ Sigma Huda Vs.Ishfaque Samad. 13BLD(HCD)152.</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1976) 78 Born. L.R.234; A.I.R.1927 Cal. 823; A.I.R. 1932 Bom. 49O—Cited.</p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1071	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">PREVENTION OF CORRUPTION ACT, 1947</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  19BLD (AD)2, 14BLD (HCD)102</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  PREVENTION OF CORRUPTION</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PREVENTION OF CORRUPTION ACT (II OF 1947)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—5(1)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Criminal misconduct</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The accused-petitioners conduct is certainly a criminal misconduct because receipt of fresh money from Nurun Nahar and consequent making of a fresh recall order as a public servant have been proved.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">It has been proved that the accused-petitioner obtained from Nurun Nahar Begurn tk. 3,093/- towards satisfaction of the agricultural loan taken by her husband. The accused-petitioner did not deposit the said money in the proper head. In that view of the matter it cannot be said that the accused- petitioner’s role was that of a bonafide act performed without mens rea.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Azizul Hoque Vs The State, 19BLD (AD)2</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—5(2)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sanction for prosecution</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sanction to prosecute a public servant is a pre-condition for initiation of a criminal proceeding against him. Absence of sanction from the appropriate authority has vitiated the entire proceeding, rendering the conviction untenable in law.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is well-settled that sanction to prosecute a public servant is a matter is a precondition for initiating a proceeding against him. No sanction order was produced by the prosecution. The Court did not obtain any sanction order or moved the appropriate authority for according sanction. When there is no sanction to prosecute the appellant, who is admittedly a public servant, the entire proceeding stands vitiated and only on that ground the order of conviction and sentence is liable to be set aside.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Ali Hossain Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)102.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1072	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">PRISONS ACT, 1894</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  14BLD(HCD)266,  20BLD(HCD)204.</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  PRISONS </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>PRISONS ACT, 1894</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>(IX of 1894)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—27(2) and Jail Code</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Paragraphs 962 and 963</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Paragraph 962 of the Jail Code provides that a prisoner under 21 is considered as juvenile or adolescent and such prisoners should be kept separated from the adult prisoners. Paragraph 963 of the Jail Code provides that confinement of juvenile offenders in jail is objectionable for many good reasons.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Deputy Commissioner, Satkhira, and others, 14BLD(HCD)266</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—30</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 30 sub-section 1 of the Act provides that every prisoner under sentence of death shall, immediately on his arrival in the prison after sentence, be searched by, or by order of, the Jailer and all articles shall be taken from him which the Jailer deems it dangerous or inexpedient to leave in his possession. Sub-section (2) of Section 30 of the Act further provides that every such prisoner shall be confined in a cell apart from all other prisoners, and shall be placed by day and by night under the charge of a guard.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Major (Retd) Baziul Huda Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD)204.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1073	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">SECURITIES AND EXCHANGE COMMISSION</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  19BLD (HCD)603, 18BLD(HCD)61</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  SECURITIES AND EXCHANGE COMMISSION</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SECURITIES AND EXCHANGE COMMISSION ORDINANCE, 1969</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>(XVII OF 1969)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—25A</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—265B</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 265B lays down that the prosecution ‘shall’ open his case by describing the charge and stating by what evidence it proposes to prove the charge. The word ‘shall’ in the section indicates that the directions stated in the section must be strictly followed, more so in view of section 25A of the Security and Exchange Ordinance, which lays down that upon the accused lies the burden of proving that he had not contravened any provision of the Ordinance.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The burden of proof is neither as heavy as that upon the prosecution nor so onerous and that it is not higher than the burden which rests upon a plaintiff or defendant in civil proceeding.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Aba Tayeb, Chairman Vs. The Security Exchange Commission and ano., 19BLD (HCD)603</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> (1936)2 All E.R.1 138—relied upon</p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SECURITIES AND EXCHANGE COMMISSION ORDINANCE, 1993</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>(III OF 1993)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>  <br/></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—17</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since a duly constituted enquiry committee in its report opined that the accused persons contravened the provisions of section 17 of the Ordinance and the learned Magistrate on being satisfied about the same has taken cognizance against the accused persons they would get chance to defend themselves in the case and as such there has occasioned no failure of justice in taking cognizance of the offence against them.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shainpukur Holding Limited Vs. Security Exchange Commission, 18BLD(HCD)61</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27DLR1 11; 30DLR(SC)58; 29DLR 122; 32 DLR(AD)35; 1940A.C1014 (at P.1022).—Cited. </p><p style="text-align: justify;"> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SECURITIES AND EXCHANGE COMMISSION ACT, 1993 (XV OF 1993)</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—3(2), 5(5), 11(ga)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Under the provisions of section 3(2) of the Act Security Exchange Commission (SEC) is a body corporate and shall sue and be sued in its name. Under the provision of section 5(5) of the Act the Chairman is the Chief Executive of the Security Exchange Commission. Clause (ga) of Section 11 of the Act provides that all the responsibilities and liabilities of the Government under the provisions of the Act shall be the responsibilities and liabilities of the Commission.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shainpukur Holding Limited Vs. Security Exchange Commission, 18BLD(HCD)61.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1074	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">SPECIAL POWERS ACT, 1974</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  15BLD(HCD)533, 15BLD(AD) 138,  15 BLD (HCD)81, 20BLD (HCD)412, 14BLD(HCD)206, 15BLD(HCD)246, 14BLD (HCD)500, 15 BLD (HCD)81, 15BLD(HCD)655, 18BLD (HCD)165,14BLD (HCD)156, 15BLD(HCD)533,  17BLD(HCD)395, 20BLD (HCD)412, 17BLD(HCD)395, 14BLD(HCD)206, 15BLD(</p><p><b>Subject:</b>  SPECIAL POWERS </p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>SPECIAL POWERS ACT, 1974</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>(XIV OF 1974)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—2(f)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Prejudicial Act</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Collecting children with intent to smuggle them out of Bangladesh does not come within the purview of “prejudicial act” as defined in section 2(1) of the Special Powers Act, 1974.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>M.A. Hashem Vs. Government of Bangladesh and others, 15BLD(HCD)533</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—2(f) and 8</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Burden of proof in matters of detention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The burden of proof to show that the order of detention is lawful lies on the detaining authority. The detaining authority making return to the rule is to place all relevant facts before the Court. Where the respondents do not file any return, the Court cannot satisfy itself as to the justification of detention.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the absence of any return by the respondents, the appellant’s contention that the grounds were served on the detenu beyond the statutory period prescribed in section 8 of the Special Powers Act and that the detenu was deprived of his right to make an effective representation before the advisory board remain unchallenged. The Court drew an inference against the respondents and held the order of detention illegal and without lawful authority.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Shameem Vs. Government of Bangladesh and others, 15BLD(AD) 138</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 45 DLR (AD) 89; (1975)4 SCC 114; 1989 Cri. L.J. 1270-Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—2 (f) and 8</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">When in the grounds of detention it is not stated as to when, where and in what manner the detenu indulged in anti-government and anti-state activities and no particular is given about his activities as a member of an illegal anti- state secret organisation, the grounds so furnished must be held to be absolutely vague, indefinite and unspecified depriving the detenu of his right to make an effective representation to the appropriate authority against his detention as is provided in section 8 of the Special Powers Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Dhiman Chowdhury Vs. The State, represented by the Secretary, Ministry of Home Affairs, Bangladesh and others, 15 BLD (HCD)81</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—3(2)(3)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Sub-section (3) section 3 provides that no order shall remain in force for more than thirty days after making thereof unless in the meantime it is approved by the Government. Neither in sub-section (2) nor in sub-section (3), there is any provision of passing the order with a condition that the order will come in to force on the date of service of the order upon the detenu. Rather, it has been clearly stated that “no such order shall remain in force for more than thirty days after making thereof.” An order is made on the date it is signed. In the instant case, the order was signed on 24.6.99. There is no scope of passing any order under sub-section (2) or (3) of section 3 of the Act with a qualification that it will come in the force on the date of service of the order upon the detenu.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md Mosharaf Hossain@ Mash Vs Govt. of Bangladesh and ors, 20BLD (HCD)412</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—3(3)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Extension of detention</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The liberty of the citizens being a precious possession, it should not be denied, curtailed or otherwise circumscribed by any authority without any legal and reasonable basis. The order of extension of detention must be made with proper application of mind of the detaining authority and it must not be a mechanical routine work by the office staff made much ahead of the date of expiry of detention.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mrs. Samirannesa Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh and others, 14BLD(HCD)206</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—3 and 8</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When most of the grounds of detention appear to be vague, indefinite and unspecific, it would be held that the detention order is a colourable exercise of a statutory power. In a case of preventive detention, it is now well- settled that the grounds served on the detenu must be sufficient to enable him to make an effective representation to the appropriate authority against his detention.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the detenu is a foreign national, the Government instead of detaining him for preventing him from doing prejudicial acts, could better direct him to remove himself from Bangladesh.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Shahidul Alam Vs. Government of Bangladesh and others, 15BLD(HCD)246</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—3 and 8</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Compliance of the provisions of section 8 of the Special Powers Act, 1974 is mandatory and any detention order made under section 3 of the Special Powers Act without furnishing the grounds of detention is illegal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bachhu Miah Vs. The State, 14BLD (HCD)500</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> PLD 1957 (Lahore)497—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—3 (1)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The pre- requisite of applying sub-section (1) of section 3 of the Special Powers Act is, first of all, to arrive at a satisfaction by the detaining authority that it is necessary to order detention of the detenu with a view to preventing him from committing a “prejudicial act” as defined in section 3(1) of the Act. There must be a nexus between the grounds mentioned in the order of detention and the grounds of detention supplied to the detenu.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">In the instant case, in the ground of detention the purpose of detention was to prevent the detenu from committing an act prejudicial to law and order whereas in the order of detention there was an added purpose of preventing the detenu from committing acts prejudicial to economic and financial interest of the State. From this it follows that the detaining authority itself was not certain as to what prejudicial act or acts the detenu was likely to commit. This in turn suggests lack of proper application of mind of the detaining authority in arriving at the necessary “satisfaction” within the meaning of section 3 (1) of the Special Powers Act, which is an indispensable prerequisite for curtailing the liberty of a citizen guaranteed by the Constitution. It is the constitutional duty of the High Court Division to satisfy itself that the essential prerequisite regarding “satisfaction” for passing the order of detention has been meticulously and carefully fulfilled and in case of any omission to fulfil the above requirement the order of detention must be held to be without lawful authority.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere availability of materials for coming to a reasonable satisfaction by the detaining authority that the activities of the detenu were “pre-judicial acts” as defined in section 2(t) of the Special Powers Act does not make the order of detention ipso facto lawful if the detaining authority fails to pass the order of detention and prepare and furnish the grounds of detention after strictly complying with the requirements of the various provisions of the Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Dr. Dhiman Chowdhury Vs. The State, represented by the Secretary, Ministry of Home Affairs, Bangladesh and others, 15 BLD (HCD)81</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—3(l)(a) and 3(2)(3)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Government is not vested under section 3(3) of the Special Powers Act with the power of extension of the initial order of detention passed under section 3(2) of the Act by the District Magistrate or the Additional District Magistrate. The Government has, however, independent of the order of the District Magistrate, the power to issue an order of detention under section 3(l)(a) of the Special Powers Act, 1974</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">In the instant case, the initial order of detention passed by the District Magistrate being for 30 days, any extension of the said period of detention upon approval by the Government is not contemplated under section 3(3) of the Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Yeasmin Akhter Vs. Bangladesh and others, 15BLD(HCD)655</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref</strong>: 47DLR(HCD) 12;—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—3(1)(3)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Government could have passed a fresh order of detention under sub-section (1) of section 3 of the Special Powers Act, but that was not done and as such the second order of detention under sub-section (3) of section 3 of the Special Powers Act and consequently subsequent orders the extending the second order of detention passed by the Government are all illegal and without jurisdiction.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdus Samad Vs. The State, 18BLD (HCD)165</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 15BLD655; 47 DLR 12—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—3(2)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Special Powers Act being a special law, its provisions need be construed strictly with reference to the materials or grounds supplied by the Government to justify that the powers were exercised on a rational basis.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The grounds of detention being vague and indefinite and specific criminal cases being pending for trial do not form a rational basis for detention and, as such, the order of detention is not sustainable in law.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mallick Tarikul Islam Vs. The Secretary, Ministry of Home Affairs and others, 14BLD (HCD)156</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 1 B.S.C.D. 119; 38DLR 93; 28 DLR 259; 27 DLR(SC) 41—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—3(2) and 4</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—80</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Power of the District Magistrate to direct detention of a person and notification of the substance of warrant.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">As soon as a person is taken in custody by a public authority and the fact of such detention is brought to the notice of the High Court Division, a constitutional duty under Article 102 (2) (b) (i) of the Constitution and a legal duty under section 491 Cr.P.C. are cast upon it to examine the legality or propriety of the order of detention. The detenu or any person interested in him at once acquires a right to move the Court to institute an enquiry. Although there is no specific provision in the Special Powers Act, 1974 to serve the order of detention upon the detenu, it is essential that the order of detention must be served upon him for enabling him to know under what order and under what law he was being detained.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 4 of the Special Powers Act provides that the order of detention passed under section 3 of the Special Powers Act shall be executed as provided in section 80 of the Code of Criminal Procedure. So, section 4 of the Special Powers Act, 1974 has to be read with section 80 of the Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 and these 2 sections read together make it abundantly clear that an order of detention passed under section 3 of the Special Powers Act must be served upon the detenu.
