Non-service of the order of detention upon the detenu renders the detention order illegal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>M.A. Hashem Vs. Government of Bangladesh and others, 15BLD(HCD)533</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A.I.R. 1944 (Patna) 354—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—3(2) and 8</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since on the ground of reasonableness of the materials the alleged three secret reports are totally lacking reasonableness, rational basis and any probative value, it is not safe to pass any order of detention relying upon such materials.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Bilkis Akhter Hossain Vs Bangladesh and others, 17BLD(HCD)395</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—7</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 7 of Act relates to the procedure of punishment of an absconder and does not in any way empower the detaining authority to detain a person by an order made more than thirty days prior to the service of the order upon that person.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Md Mosharaf Hossain @ Mash Vs Govt. of Bangladesh and ors, 20BLD (HCD)412</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—8</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It is now well-settled that the materials and grounds of detention upon which the detaining authority bases its satisfaction are subject to judicial scrutiny. If the High Court Division finds that materials and grounds of detention are unreasonable and without any rational basis and probative value, it can strike down the detention on such ground alone.</p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong><em>Bilkis Akhter Hossain Vs Bangladesh and others, 17BLD(HCD)395</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—8(2)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>The General Clauses Act ( X of 1897)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—9</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Communication to the detenu of the grounds of detention within 15 days as required by section 8(2) of the Special Powers Act has to be calculated from the date of detention but in view of section 9 of the General Clauses Act the date of detention is to be excluded while computing the period of 15 days.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mrs. Samirannesa Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh and others, 14BLD(HCD)206</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—10</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 10 of the Act requires that the case of a detenu shall have to be placed before the Advisory Board for its consideration within 120 days of the order of detention, failing which the detention becomes illegal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Samirannesa Vs.Govt. of Bangladesh and others, 14BLD(HCD)206</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 42 DLR 272; 40 DLR 193, 207 and 353—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—25A</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Penalty for counter feiting currency notes, Govt. stamps etc.</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Mere possession of counterfeit currency notes is by itself no offence as contemplated under section 25A of the Special Powers Act. In order to succeed in the case, the prosecution is required to prove that the accused used the counterfeit currency notes as genuine ones knowing or having reason to believe them to be counterfeit.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abdus Salam alias Abdus Salam and another Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)477 </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—25B</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To establish a charge of smuggling under section 25B of the Special Powers Act it must be proved that the goods in question were brought into Bangladesh in violation of any prohibition or restriction imposed by or under any law or by evading customs duties and / or other taxes. In the instant case, there being no special mark in the cattle-heads to brand them as Indian and similar cattle-heads being available in the local market and the cattle-heads having been seized in Bangladesh at a distance of 5 miles of from the International border, the appellants cannot be held liable for smuggling.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">The sale of seized-bullocks only one day after these were seized, even before the investigation of the case started, and without the permission of the Court, is uncalled for and disapproved. The unusual hurry in which the bullocks were sold in auction at a nominal price is to the detriment of the Government revenue and is also pre-judicial to the defence.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Kabil Miah and others Vs. The State, 14 BLD(HCD)432</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 40 DLR (HCD) 348—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—25B</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To sustain a conviction under section 25B of the Special Powers Act, it has to be fully established that the seized goods are contraband goods and illegally brought into Bangladesh without payment of customs duties and other taxes.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Mokhlesur Rahman and another Vs. The State, 14BLD(HCD)126</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 40 DLR 348—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—25B</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To substantiate a charge of smuggling the prosecution must prove that the seized articles were recovered from the possession of the accused and those were contraband goods. In the instant case there being nothing on record to show that the seized articles are contraband goods the impugned order of conviction for smuggling cannot be sustained.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Tomezuddin Biswas alias Kalu and another Vs The State, 17BLD(HCD)174</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—25B</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Penalty for smuggling</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">To saddle an accused with the liability of possessing contraband goods, the prosecution must prove the exclusive possession or domain of the accused over the goods in question. When circumstances suggest that others may also have possession of and access to the contraband goods, the conviction of the accused appellant is not legally sustainable. Moreover, the prosecution failed to produce the alleged contraband goods before the Court without any plausible explanation rendering the prosecution case doubtful.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Taher alias Md. Taher Vs The State, 18BLD (HCD)691</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—25B(1)(2)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Penalty for smuggling and possession of smuggled goods</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When the accused is not apprehended while smuggling goods from India to Bangladesh and he is not apprehended with the smuggled goods in the border areas, he cannot be held liable for smuggling punishable under section 25B(l) of the Special Powers Act. If, however, smuggled goods are actually recovered from his possession, which he held for the purpose of sale, well inside Bangladesh territory, he is liable to be hauled up for the offence punishable under section 25B(2) of the Special Powers Act, providing a maximum sentence of 7 years imprisonment.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gopal Chandra Chakraborty Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)224</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—27(1)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Although section 27(1) of the Act empowers a Special Tribunal to take cognizance of an offence tribale under the Act without the accused being committed to it for trial, the Magistrate has still then jurisdiction to discharge an accused in an appropriate case. This power of the Magistrate is subject to the discretion exercisable by the Special Tribunal.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Since no allegation was made against the petitioner in the F.I.R. and no evidence could be collected against him during investigation and the police recommended for his discharge, the learned Magistrate was within his competence to discharge the accused petitioner despite the fact that the case was triable exclusively by the Special Tribunal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Narayan Chandra Das Vs. The State. 16BLD(HCD)421</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—27(4)</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Where the evidence of the witnesses was recorded in full together with their full cross- examination, whether it could not be taken to mean that the summary procedure of trial was actually adopted?</p><p style="text-align: justify;">Where the evidence of the witnesses was recorded in full together with their full cross examination, it could not be taken to mean that the summary procedure of trial was actually adopted.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Humayun Kabir Vs. The State, 13BLD(HCD)406</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 45C.W.N. 139—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—27(6)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—537</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Non-compliance of the mandatory provision of law, that is, non-publication of notice under section 27(6) of the Special Powers Act directing the accused-appellant to answer the charge is illegal and without jurisdiction and the same is not curable under section 537 of the Code of Criminal Procedure.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Samiul Haider alias Kuiba Vs The State, 18BLD(HCD)515</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Sections—28 and 29</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Section 28 of the Special Powers Act prescribes the power of the Special Tribunals while section 29 of the said Act provides that the provisions of the Code of Criminal Procedure, so far they are not inconsistent with the provisions of the Special Powers Act, shall apply to the proceedings of the Special Tribunals and the Special Tribunals shall have all the powers of the Court of Sessions exercising original jurisdiction.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>S.A. Hasnat Khan and others Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD)440</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—30</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">When an application for bail in a case involving offences under the Special Powers Act is filed before the Sessions Judge before the submission of charge-sheet, the learned Judge decides the bail matter as the Sessions Judge and not as the Special Tribunal in as much as cognizance is yet to be taken under the Special Powers Act. No appeal against the rejection of the prayer for bail in such a case lies to the High Court Division under Section 30 of the Special Powers Act.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Md. Abul Kalam Vs.The State, 15BLD (HCD)167</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—30</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is no certainty when the appeal will be disposed of and the trial shall commence. Since all the co-accuseds are on bail and since it is uncertain when the aforesaid appeal will be disposed of and then the trial shall commence the High Court Division in the special circumstances of the case allowed bail to the appellant.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Liton Vs The State, 17BLD(HCD)370</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—30</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Penal Code, 1860(XLV of 1860)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—342</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The Special Tribunal had only jurisdiction to try cases as enumerated in the schedule of Special Powers Act and not beyond that. An offence under section 342 of the Penal Code is not included in the schedule of the Act and cannot be basis of conviction as the same is a non schedule offence. Hence the High Court Division acted wrongly and without jurisdiction in convicting the appellants under section 342 of the Penal Code when the same is not triable by the Special Tribunal at all. The alteration of conviction from a schedule offence to an offence which is only referable under Penal Code is not legally permissible.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdur Rahman and others Vs The State, 19BLD(AD)4</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—30</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Code of Criminal Procedure, 1898 (V of 1898)</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—339C</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is an unnecessary delay in holding the trial and there is no certainty when the witnesses will turn up and when the trial can concluded. In such a position of uncertainty regarding the conclusion of the trial, the High Court Division granted bail to the accused-appellant.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Anowar Hossain @ Mohasin @ Anar Vs The State, 20BLD(HCD) 103</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—32</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Long delay in holding trial provides a good ground for bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The appellant has been in custody since 3.5.1992 but no charge has yet been framed against the accused. The prosecution could not show any cogent reason for not holding the trial as yet although charge-sheet was submitted on 5.11.1993 against the appellant and 8 others, without any fault on the part of the appellant. Other accused persons have been granted bail by the Special Tribunal. This protracted delay in holding the trial provides a good ground for granting bail to the appellant.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Nurul Amin alias Bada Vs. The State, 16BLD(AD)200</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Section—32</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Bail</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Whether in a pending appeal, bail can be granted to appellants in view of contradictions in prosecution evidence on material point?</p><p style="text-align: justify;">There are contradictions in the prosecution witnesses on material points In view of this matter, the Court is inclined to enlarge the appellants on bail pending disposal of the appeal.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Akbor All alias Md. Akbor All and others Vs. The State, 13BLD(HCD)585.</em></strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
1075	<h2 class="blog_entry-title">WORDS AND PHRASE</h2>
<div class="entry-content"><div class="entry-content"><p><b>Citation:</b>  16BLD(HCD)270, 16BLD(HCD)246, 17BLD(HCD) 195, 17BLD (HCD) 148, 18BLD(HCD) 701,14BLD(HCD)566, 15BLD(HCD) 452, 17BLD(HCD)259, 13BLD (AD)17, 20 BLD(HCD)372, 20BLD (AD)24  </p><p><b>Subject:</b>  WORDS AND PHRASE</p><p><b>Delivery Date:</b>  1970-01-01</p><!DOCTYPE html><p> </p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong>WORDS AND PHRASE</strong></p><p align="center" style="text-align: center;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Moral conviction</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">A Judge is required to dispense justice in accordance with law and not according to his moral conviction. In deciding a case the well- settled and well-established legal norms and standards for assessment of the evidence on record are required to be followed.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Alamgir Hossain alias Alamgir Hossain Vs. The State, 16BLD(HCD)270</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 25 DLR (HC)398—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Suspicion</strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;">Suspicion is no evidence and it can be no substitute for proof. A suspicion, however grave it may be, cannot be the basis for convicting the accused.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Momin alias Md. Mominul Huq Vs. The, State, 16BLD(HCD)246</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>General clemency</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Order of clemency dated 14.1.1991 passed under section 40 1(1) Cr.P.C. was given effect to on and from 14.1.1991. Refusal of the said clemency benefit to the present detenu under a subsequent Government order is an act of discrimination in the matter of giving amnesty benefits to convicts.</p><p style="text-align: justify;">When a legal right accrues in favour of a man and it is given effect to, then such a right cannot be taken away or nullified by a subsequent government order under section 21 of the General Clauses Act—Acting Presidents Order No. 70 of 1990</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Mohammad Jahangir Alam Vs. Govt. of Bangladesh and others, 17BLD(HCD) 195</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 27 DLR 315; 18 DLR 92; PLD 1960 (SC)310; PLD 1963 Lahore 53 (DB), PLD 1965 (Pesh) 47 (DB); 12 DLR 865 (DB)Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Abscondence and suspicion</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">Unfounded suspicion of abscondence of an accused cannot be a ground for keeping him in custody for unlimited period.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Kader Faruque alias Mohd. Faruque Vs. The State, 17BLD (HCD) 148</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Corroboration</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">In a case where enmity is admitted the evidence of witnesses are liable to be closely scrutinised and unless there is corroboration by cogent. independent and disinterested witnesses the evidences of such witnesses who are enemically deposed, are not to be accepted as the basis for conviction (particularly in a murder case).</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>The State Vs. Hosen Sheikh alias Hochen &amp; Hashem Sheikh, 18BLD(HCD) 701</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 7 BLD(AD)1987)1; 15 BLD(AD)54; A.I.R. 197 1(SC)804; 6 BCR(AD)229; BCR 1986 (AD)225; 44DLR(AD)( 1992)60—Cited.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Malafide—</strong>Malafide vitiates everything—Even a malafide investigation can not be sustained and it is liable to be struck down.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>MUBASHWIR ALI AND OTHERS VS. THE STATE, 14BLD(HCD)566</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 37 DLR (HCD) 185; 27 DLR (HCD) 342; 31 DLR (AD) 69; 35 DLR (AD) 127; 36 DLR (AD) 14; 37 DLR (HCD) 182; 34 DLR (AD) 222; 39 DLR (AD) 1; 38 DLR (AD) 41-Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>No evidence</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The term ‘no evidence’ is not only confined to dearth of evidence but also it extends to a case where the evidence is reasonably incapable of supporting a finding.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Sohail Ahmed Chowdhury Vs. The State, 15BLD(HCD) 452</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Ref:</strong> A.I.R. 1987 page 1621; 45 DLR (AD)175; 40 DLR(1988) 348; 12 DLR page 220; A.I.R 1959 (Allahabad) 559; A.I.R. 1951(Allahabad) 441—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Plea of Alibi—Burden of the accused to prove it</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The plea of the accused was that he was off to Faridpur for purchasing clothes and he was not present at home at the time of the occurrence. In the absence of any evidence to prove that the accused went to Faridpur and stayed there on the night and purchased there, the plea of alibi falls to the ground. The defence failed to prove the said plea of alibi entailing facts within his special knowledge.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Gouranga Kumar Saha Vs. The State, 17BLD(HCD)259</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Vary</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">The term ‘vary used in the regulation implies an authority to change the judgment and order under review by modification or even by substitution or by qualification or otherwise, MLR No. 1 of 1982</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Helaluddin Ahmed alias Swapan Vs.Bangladesh and others 13BLD (AD)17</em></strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;"><strong>Ref:</strong> 43 ,DLR(AD)104; 33 DLR (1981) 233 and 358; 92 Common Wealth Law Reports, 526(528); 33 DLR(AD)154; PLD 1978 (SC)49; PLD 1974(SC)1513; PLD 1989 (SC) 26; 32 DLR(AD)1 10—Cited</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"> </p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong> </strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Written complaint</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">It was obligatory on the part of the learned Magistrate to make a written complaint alleging the nature of the order made by him which was alleged to have disobeyed by accused and the manner of violation of the order to form an opinion that accused persons have committed an offence punishable under section 188 of the Penal Code.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em>Abdul Ahad @ Md Abdul Ahad Vs. The State, 20 BLD(HCD)372</em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong><em> </em></strong></p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>‘See’ and ‘verify’</strong></p><p style="text-align: justify;">There is no scope for extending the meaning of the words ‘see’ and ‘verify’ used in regulation 80 to mean examine the worth of the answer script.</p><p style="margin-bottom: 12.0pt;"><strong>Chairman Secondary &amp; Higher Secondary Education Board, Rajshahi and another Vs </strong></p><p><strong>Arifur Rahman, 20BLD (AD)24 </strong></p><p><span id="pastemarkerend"> </span></p></div></div>
